##      address into; NULL is EFAULT.
##
##      Known limitation (deferred): Linux gates this option
##      on CAP_SYS_ADMIN-or-PTRACE permission for non-self
##      queries; gdb attaches with PTRACE so the check is
##      trivially satisfied in practice.  We do not enforce
##      capabilities at all yet; revisit when the capability
##      framework lands.
##
##      Self-tests cover:
##        * arg3/arg4/arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL (each in turn).
##        * NULL pointer -> EFAULT.
##        * Scratch buffer pre-filled with sentinel; after
##          dispatch the buffer reads 0 (kernel context: no
##          set_tid_address registration).
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 25s).


## 103. Linux ABI batch 74: real per-PCB PR_MCE_KILL /
##      PR_MCE_KILL_GET round-trip with strict argument
##      validation matching Linux.
##
##      Before this batch, both options fell through to the
##      generic `_ => EINVAL` catch-all in sys_prctl.
##      Container runtimes (Podman, container-init) and
##      OOM-handling daemons set this to PR_MCE_KILL_EARLY on
##      processes that hold large shared-memory regions where
##      recovery isn't worth the latency.  EINVAL on an
##      option that should succeed silently disables the
##      optimisation.
##
##      Fix: add `linux_mce_kill_policy: u32` (default 2 =
##      PR_MCE_KILL_DEFAULT) to the PCB.  Encode the
##      user-visible value directly (0=LATE, 1=EARLY,
##      2=DEFAULT) rather than mirroring Linux's two-bit
##      PF_MCE_PROCESS / PF_MCE_EARLY encoding — makes the
##      GET path a trivial read.
##
##      Inherited verbatim across fork (Linux: both PF_MCE
##      bits are in task_struct::flags copy path).  Preserved
##      across exec (flush_thread does not touch them).
##
##      PR_MCE_KILL (33) — strict Linux semantics:
##        * arg2 (our arg1) = PR_MCE_KILL_CLEAR (0) or
##          PR_MCE_KILL_SET (1); other values -> EINVAL.
##        * arg3 (our arg2) ignored on CLEAR (must be 0 or
##          EINVAL); on SET must be PR_MCE_KILL_LATE (0),
##          PR_MCE_KILL_EARLY (1), or PR_MCE_KILL_DEFAULT (2);
##          others -> EINVAL.
##        * arg4/arg5 must be 0 or EINVAL.
##        * CLEAR and SET-with-DEFAULT both store DEFAULT
##          (matches Linux clearing both PF bits).
##        * Returns 0 on success.
##
##      PR_MCE_KILL_GET (34) — returns the policy DIRECTLY as
##      the syscall return value (no user pointer).
##      arg1..arg5 must all be 0 or EINVAL.  Kernel context
##      reports the default (2).
##
##      Known limitation (deferred): we do not implement
##      machine-check exception handling at all.  The stored
##      policy is round-tripped for ABI compatibility only and
##      has no effect on what actually happens on MCE.  When
##      MCE handling lands it should consult
##      get_mce_kill_policy(pid) to decide between
##      SIGBUS-now vs. let-recovery-try-first.
##
##      Self-tests cover:
##        * PR_MCE_KILL arg4/arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL.
##        * arg1 not in {0, 1} -> EINVAL.
##        * CLEAR + arg2 != 0 -> EINVAL.
##        * SET + arg2 not in {0, 1, 2} -> EINVAL.
##        * CLEAR / SET(0) / SET(1) / SET(2) all -> 0 in
##          kernel context.
##        * PR_MCE_KILL_GET arg1..arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL.
##        * Kernel context -> 2 (default).
##        * pcb storage layer round-trips
##          DEFAULT -> EARLY -> LATE -> DEFAULT -> destroy -> None.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 24s).


## 104. Linux ABI batch 75: PR_TASK_PERF_EVENTS_DISABLE /
##      PR_TASK_PERF_EVENTS_ENABLE — bare success.
##
##      Before this batch, options 31 and 32 fell through to
##      EINVAL in sys_prctl.  Perf-aware userspace (`perf stat
##      -p $$`, valgrind's CALLGRIND_START/STOP_INSTRUMENTATION
##      wrappers, glibc's __libc_pre_main hot-restart hooks)
##      calls these to bracket measurement regions.  EINVAL
##      makes them emit "could not disable perf events,
##      continuing" warnings on every run.
##
##      Fix: return 0 from both.  We do not implement a perf
##      event subsystem, so there is nothing attached to
##      disable / enable.  No per-PCB state is needed — there
##      is no corresponding PR_GET for these options on Linux,
##      so the bit is purely a side-effect command.  If we
##      ever add perf events, the disable/enable handlers
##      will iterate the per-task event list at that time;
##      until then, success is the correct user-visible
##      answer.
##
##      Linux ignores arg2..arg5 for both options; we mirror
##      exactly (no validation), so probe code that passes
##      garbage in the extra args still sees success.
##
##      Self-tests cover both options with garbage in
##      arg1..arg5 — must return 0.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).


## 105. Linux ABI batch 76: real per-PCB PR_SET_MDWE /
##      PR_GET_MDWE round-trip (Memory-Deny-Write-Execute),
##      with sticky-monotone semantics matching Linux.
##
##      Before this batch, both options fell through to the
##      generic `_ => EINVAL` catch-all in sys_prctl.  Modern
##      sandbox / hardening layers (systemd's
##      MemoryDenyWriteExecute=, chromium's renderer
##      sandbox, openssh's seccomp-MDWE combo, podman's
##      hardened-container preset) call PR_SET_MDWE during
##      init.  EINVAL there silently disables MDWE and the
##      process falls back to weaker W^X enforcement, which
##      is a measurable hardening regression.
##
##      Fix: add `linux_mdwe_bits: u32` (default 0) to the
##      PCB with strict sticky-monotone semantics matching
##      Linux's task_struct::mm->flags MDWE bit pair:
##        * Bit 0 = PR_MDWE_REFUSE_EXEC_GAIN (1)
##        * Bit 1 = PR_MDWE_NO_INHERIT       (2)
##        * Valid mask = 3.
##
##      Inheritance: verbatim across fork.  Linux duplicates
##      `mm->flags` MDWE bits via `dup_mm_flags`, so the child
##      starts with the same MDWE policy as the parent
##      regardless of NO_INHERIT (which is an exec-time, not
##      fork-time, gate).  fork_create copies the field
##      verbatim.
##
##      Across exec: Linux clears the bits iff
##      PR_MDWE_NO_INHERIT was set.  We preserve
##      unconditionally for now (same caveat as
##      DUMPABLE/KEEPCAPS — exec hook not yet landed).  When
##      the exec hook lands it must check NO_INHERIT and
##      clear linux_mdwe_bits on the new image.
##
##      PR_SET_MDWE (65) — strict Linux semantics:
##        * arg3 / arg4 / arg5 (our arg2/arg3/arg4) must all
##          be 0 or EINVAL.
##        * arg2 (our arg1) > u32::MAX -> EINVAL.
##        * arg1 with bits outside the valid mask -> EINVAL.
##        * NO_INHERIT without REFUSE_EXEC_GAIN -> EINVAL
##          (Linux: NO_INHERIT is meaningless without the
##          refusal bit).
##        * Sticky monotone: once set non-zero, the only
##          legal subsequent value is the same value;
##          attempting to clear or change -> EPERM.  Matches
##          Linux's prctl_set_mdwe which refuses any
##          transition out of a previously-set state.
##        * Returns 0 on success.
##
##      PR_GET_MDWE (66) — returns the bits DIRECTLY as the
##      syscall return value (no user pointer).  arg1..arg5
##      must all be 0 or EINVAL.  Kernel context reports 0.
##
##      Known limitation (deferred — IMPORTANT): mmap and
##      mprotect do NOT yet consult linux_mdwe_bits.  This
##      means PR_SET_MDWE succeeds, PR_GET_MDWE truthfully
##      reports the value, but the security promise of
##      refusing PROT_WRITE | PROT_EXEC (or transitioning a
##      mapping to PROT_EXEC after having been writable) is
##      NOT honoured.  A process that sets MDWE today still
##      gets W+X mappings.  This is an active hardening
##      regression vs Linux for any caller that trusts the
##      kernel to enforce MDWE after a successful PR_SET.
##      Fix: when mmap / mprotect land, both must consult
##      get_mdwe_bits(pid) and, if REFUSE_EXEC_GAIN is set,
##      reject any mapping that grants PROT_EXEC while
##      having had PROT_WRITE at any point, returning
##      EACCES.  Track this in the mmap / mprotect work
##      items.
##
##      Storage layer (get_mdwe_bits / set_mdwe_bits) does
##      NOT enforce monotonicity.  The sticky-monotone EPERM
##      is enforced at the syscall surface only, so future
##      kernel callers (e.g. exec hardening reloads) can
##      adjust the bits without violating user-visible
##      stickiness.
##
##      Self-tests cover:
##        * PR_SET_MDWE arg3/arg4/arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL.
##        * arg1 > u32::MAX -> EINVAL.
##        * arg1 with bit outside valid mask (e.g. 4) ->
##          EINVAL.
##        * NO_INHERIT (2) without REFUSE_EXEC_GAIN ->
##          EINVAL.
##        * Valid values 0, 1, 3 all return 0 in kernel
##          context (kernel-context path skips state mutation
##          so stickiness check doesn't reject the sequence).
##        * PR_GET_MDWE arg1..arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL.
##        * Kernel context PR_GET_MDWE -> 0 (default).
##        * pcb storage round-trip:
##          0 -> 1 -> 3 -> 0 (helper accepts because surface
##          enforces monotonicity, not the helper) ->
##          destroy -> None.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 24s).


## 106. Linux ABI batch 77: PR_GET_TIMING / PR_SET_TIMING —
##      Linux-faithful constant-mode handling.
##
##      Before this batch options 13 and 14 fell through to
##      the generic EINVAL catch-all in sys_prctl.  Boot-time
##      profilers and accounting wrappers (sysstat's `sar`,
##      gnu coreutils `time` when probing for high-resolution
##      timing, and a handful of older glibc init paths)
##      issue PR_GET_TIMING at startup to detect whether the
##      kernel offers timestamp-based timing.  EINVAL there
##      makes them emit a "kernel does not support timing
##      mode selection" warning on every run.
##
##      Linux's prctl_set_timing accepts only
##      PR_TIMING_STATISTICAL (0); PR_TIMING_TIMESTAMP (1)
##      was reserved in the original API but never
##      implemented and is rejected with EINVAL.  arg3..arg5
##      are ignored entirely.  prctl_get_timing always
##      returns PR_TIMING_STATISTICAL (0) and ignores
##      arg2..arg5.  Mirror exactly.
##
##      No per-PCB storage is needed because there is only
##      one valid mode — PR_GET is a constant.
##
##      PR_SET_TIMING (14):
##        * arg1 == 0 -> 0 (success).
##        * arg1 != 0 -> EINVAL.
##        * arg2..arg5 not validated.
##
##      PR_GET_TIMING (13):
##        * Always returns 0.
##        * arg1..arg5 not validated.
##
##      No known limitations: this option is purely a probe
##      surface in modern userspace because Linux itself
##      never implemented timestamp-mode timing.  Anyone
##      relying on it for real measurement uses
##      CLOCK_PROCESS_CPUTIME_ID or `getrusage`.
##
##      Self-tests cover:
##        * PR_SET_TIMING(0) -> 0.
##        * PR_SET_TIMING(1) -> EINVAL.
##        * PR_SET_TIMING(0xdead) -> EINVAL.
##        * PR_SET_TIMING(0) with garbage arg2..arg5 -> 0.
##        * PR_GET_TIMING with garbage arg1..arg5 -> 0.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).


## 107. Linux ABI batch 78: PR_GET_SECCOMP — truthful "no
##      seccomp installed" report.
##
##      Before this batch option 21 fell through to the
##      generic EINVAL catch-all in sys_prctl.  Every sandbox
##      library (libseccomp, audit-libs, container runtimes,
##      python's pyseccomp startup check, golang's seccomp
##      bindings) probes PR_GET_SECCOMP at startup to learn
##      whether a filter is already installed (e.g. by a
##      parent process) before deciding what to install.
##      EINVAL there looks like a kernel bug because Linux
##      `prctl_get_seccomp` cannot fail.
##
##      Linux semantics: returns `current->seccomp.mode`
##      (0=DISABLED, 1=STRICT, 2=FILTER) with no argument
##      validation.  arg1..arg5 are ignored entirely.  Cannot
##      fail.  We mirror exactly and always return 0
##      (DISABLED) because we have no seccomp infrastructure.
##
##      No per-PCB storage: until we actually implement
##      seccomp, the answer is a constant.
##
##      Companion PR_SET_SECCOMP (22) is INTENTIONALLY left at
##      the EINVAL catch-all.  Pretending to install a seccomp
##      filter would be an active security lie: the sandbox
##      would believe its syscall whitelist was enforced when
##      it isn't.  The correct answer for PR_SET_SECCOMP is
##      ENOSYS ("kernel does not implement this feature") so
##      sandboxes can fall back to weaker hardening rather
##      than crash on EINVAL, but changing the errno is a
##      behavioural change with its own ABI surface area
##      (sandbox-init code paths that distinguish EINVAL vs
##      ENOSYS) — defer to a future batch where the change can
##      be paired with audits of the major sandbox callers we
##      care about (chromium renderer, firefox content
##      process, systemd-nspawn).
##
##      Known limitation (deferred): PR_SET_SECCOMP still
##      returns EINVAL; should be ENOSYS once we've decided
##      the migration story for the few callers that probe
##      via PR_SET with a NULL filter to feature-detect.  We
##      should NEVER return success from PR_SET_SECCOMP until
##      we have a real BPF interpreter — that would be a
##      security regression.
##
##      Self-tests cover:
##        * PR_GET_SECCOMP with zero arg1..arg5 -> 0.
##        * PR_GET_SECCOMP with garbage arg1..arg5 -> 0
##          (mirroring Linux's ignore-extra-args).
##        * PR_SET_SECCOMP(FILTER, NULL, 0, 0, 0) STILL ->
##          EINVAL (anti-regression: ensure we haven't
##          accidentally started lying about seccomp support).
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).


## 108. Linux ABI batch 79: per-PCB PR_SET_IO_FLUSHER /
##      PR_GET_IO_FLUSHER round-trip with strict argument
##      validation matching Linux.
##
##      Before this batch options 57 and 58 fell through to
##      the generic EINVAL catch-all in sys_prctl.  Storage
##      daemons (drbd-worker, multipathd, nbd-client,
##      dm_crypt_write) call PR_SET_IO_FLUSHER on themselves
##      at init.  Linux uses the PR_IO_FLUSHER bit in
##      task->flags to grant `__GFP_MEMALLOC`-style fast-path
##      access in the memory-reclaim path — without this
##      mark, a flusher can self-deadlock by needing free
##      pages to make writeback progress while reclaim is
##      waiting for writeback to finish.  EINVAL there made
##      every storage daemon log a startup warning.
##
##      Fix: add `linux_io_flusher: u32` (default 0, only
##      values 0 and 1 are legal) to the PCB.  Inherited
##      verbatim across fork (Linux: `PR_IO_FLUSHER` is a
##      `task->flags` bit copied by `copy_process`).
##      Preserved across exec (flush_thread does not touch
##      it).
##
##      PR_SET_IO_FLUSHER (57) — strict Linux semantics:
##        * arg3 / arg4 / arg5 must all be 0 or EINVAL.
##        * arg2 (our arg1) must be 0 or 1; anything else
##          (including out-of-range u64 values like
##          u64::MAX) returns EINVAL.
##        * Returns 0 on success.
##
##      Linux also requires CAP_SYS_RESOURCE; we don't
##      enforce capabilities at all yet, so any caller can
##      set the flag.  When the capability framework lands,
##      this surface should consult it.  Tracked as a known
##      limitation here.
##
##      PR_GET_IO_FLUSHER (58) — returns the flag value (0
##      or 1) DIRECTLY as the syscall return (no user
##      pointer).  Linux requires CAP_SYS_RESOURCE; we don't
##      enforce.  arg1..arg5 must all be 0 or EINVAL.
##      Kernel context reports 0 (the default).
##
##      Known limitation (deferred): we have no memory
##      reclaim or writeback path yet, so the flag is
##      round-tripped for ABI compatibility only and has no
##      effect on what actually happens under memory
##      pressure.  When reclaim lands it must consult
##      get_io_flusher(pid) and grant the GFP_MEMALLOC fast
##      path for tasks with the flag set, else flushers will
##      deadlock under pressure just like a Linux build with
##      this feature disabled.
##
##      Storage layer (get_io_flusher / set_io_flusher) does
##      not validate values — the helper accepts arbitrary
##      u32s so test fixtures can manipulate freely.  The
##      syscall surface is the validation site.
##
##      Self-tests cover:
##        * PR_SET_IO_FLUSHER arg3/arg4/arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL.
##        * arg1 not in {0, 1} -> EINVAL (2, 3, 0xff,
##          u64::MAX).
##        * (0) / (1) -> 0 in kernel context.
##        * PR_GET_IO_FLUSHER arg1..arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL.
##        * Kernel context PR_GET_IO_FLUSHER -> 0.
##        * pcb storage round-trip:
##          0 -> 1 -> 0 -> destroy -> None.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s) on
##      retry after one flake at RCU 'Callback invoked: OK'
##      boundary (the same RCU flake region documented in
##      todo 101).


## 109. Linux ABI batch 80: PR_GET_SPECULATION_CTRL /
##      PR_SET_SPECULATION_CTRL — consistent "not affected"
##      stance.
##
##      Before this batch options 52 and 53 fell through to
##      the generic EINVAL catch-all in sys_prctl.  Container
##      runtimes (podman, cri-o), browsers (Chromium
##      renderer sandbox), and OpenSSL since CVE-2018-3639
##      (Spectre v4) probe PR_GET_SPECULATION_CTRL at init
##      to learn whether the per-task SSBD / IBRS / L1D
##      mitigations are installed.  EINVAL there looks like
##      a kernel bug and forces them to install the
##      mitigation themselves, which on a non-affected CPU
##      is a measurable performance regression for nothing.
##
##      Linux semantics:
##        * PR_GET_SPECULATION_CTRL (52):
##          - arg2 (our arg1) = which:
##              PR_SPEC_STORE_BYPASS = 0
##              PR_SPEC_INDIRECT_BRANCH = 1
##              PR_SPEC_L1D_FLUSH = 2
##            Unknown -> ENODEV.
##          - arg3/arg4/arg5 must be 0 or EINVAL.
##          - Returns a bitfield:
##              PR_SPEC_NOT_AFFECTED = 0
##              PR_SPEC_PRCTL = 1
##              PR_SPEC_ENABLE = 2
##              PR_SPEC_DISABLE = 4
##              PR_SPEC_FORCE_DISABLE = 8
##              PR_SPEC_DISABLE_NOEXEC = 16
##            On a CPU that doesn't need the mitigation
##            (Apple Silicon, modern Intel post-mitigations,
##            most AMD), Linux returns NOT_AFFECTED (0).
##
##        * PR_SET_SPECULATION_CTRL (53):
##          - arg2 (our arg1) = which (same constants).
##          - arg3 (our arg2) = ctrl (ENABLE/DISABLE/…).
##          - arg4/arg5 must be 0 or EINVAL.
##          - Unknown which -> ENODEV.
##          - Known which on a NOT_AFFECTED CPU -> ENXIO
##            ("the mitigation isn't controllable because
##            the CPU isn't vulnerable").
##
##      Our stance: PR_SPEC_NOT_AFFECTED for all three
##      supported features.  ENODEV for unknown features.
##      ENXIO from PR_SET for known features (matching the
##      PR_GET NOT_AFFECTED report — internally consistent).
##
##      Known limitation (IMPORTANT, deferred): we report
##      NOT_AFFECTED even on CPUs that ARE affected (most
##      x86_64).  This is defensible for a hypervisor-only
##      kernel running in QEMU where the host already
##      mitigates, but if we ever boot on bare metal and
##      expose raw indirect branches we MUST either:
##        a. Implement IBRS/SSBD/L1D-flush ourselves and
##           report PR_SPEC_PRCTL | PR_SPEC_ENABLE (or
##           whatever the current state is), or
##        b. Restrict bare-metal boot to AMD CPUs known not
##           to need IBRS.
##      Otherwise sandbox callers will trust our "not
##      affected" report and skip the mitigation they
##      should have installed.  Track when we add bare-metal
##      boot support.
##
##      Self-tests cover:
##        * PR_GET valid which (0, 1, 2) -> 0.
##        * PR_GET arg2..arg4 != 0 -> EINVAL.
##        * PR_GET unknown which (3, 0xff, u64::MAX) ->
##          ENODEV.
##        * PR_SET known which (0/1/2) with any ctrl
##          (ENABLE/DISABLE/FORCE_DISABLE) -> ENXIO.
##        * PR_SET arg3/arg4 != 0 -> EINVAL.
##        * PR_SET unknown which -> ENODEV.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).


## 110. Linux ABI batch 81: per-PCB PR_SET_MEMORY_MERGE /
##      PR_GET_MEMORY_MERGE round-trip (KSM opt-in).
##
##      Before this batch options 67 and 68 fell through to
##      the generic EINVAL catch-all in sys_prctl.  VM hosts
##      (qemu/kvm setup paths), JVM-per-container deployments,
##      and Python multi-process pools all probe these
##      options at startup to opt into Kernel Same-page
##      Merging.  EINVAL there forces the runtime to either
##      assume KSM is unsupported (skipping a measurable
##      memory-savings win for VM workloads) or emit a
##      warning on every spawn.
##
##      Fix: add `linux_memory_merge: u32` (default 0, only
##      values 0 and 1 are legal) to the PCB.  Inherited
##      verbatim across fork (Linux: MMF_VM_MERGE_ANY is in
##      `mm->flags` and survives `dup_mmap`).  Preserved
##      across exec (the bit is in the mm-flags subset that
##      survives flush_old_exec).
##
##      PR_SET_MEMORY_MERGE (67):
##        * arg3/arg4/arg5 must be 0 or EINVAL.
##        * arg2 (our arg1) must be 0 or 1; else EINVAL.
##        * Linux additionally requires NO_NEW_PRIVS or
##          CAP_SYS_RESOURCE; we don't enforce caps.
##        * Returns 0 on success.
##
##      PR_GET_MEMORY_MERGE (68):
##        * arg1..arg5 must all be 0 or EINVAL.
##        * Returns the flag DIRECTLY as the syscall return.
##        * Kernel context reports 0 (the default).
##
##      Known limitation (deferred): we do not implement KSM
##      at all.  The flag round-trips for ABI compatibility
##      but no actual page merging happens.  When KSM lands,
##      the VMA-walk on each mmap must consult
##      `get_memory_merge(pid)` and queue mergeable anonymous
##      regions onto the KSM scanner's worklist.
##
##      Self-tests cover:
##        * arg3/arg4/arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL on PR_SET.
##        * arg1 not in {0, 1} -> EINVAL (2, 3, 0xff,
##          u64::MAX).
##        * (0) / (1) -> 0.
##        * arg1..arg5 != 0 -> EINVAL on PR_GET.
##        * Kernel context PR_GET -> 0.
##        * pcb storage round-trip 0 -> 1 -> 0 -> destroy ->
##          None.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 21s).


## 111. Linux ABI batch 82: per-PCB PR_CAP_AMBIENT
##      round-trip with strict argument validation matching
##      Linux.
##
##      Before this batch option 47 fell through to the
##      generic EINVAL catch-all in sys_prctl.  systemd
##      (every service unit with `AmbientCapabilities=`),
##      container runtimes (podman, docker, runc), and
##      `capsh --addamb` userspace tooling probe this
##      option to install ambient capabilities — the only
##      capability set that a non-setuid, non-file-cap
##      execve preserves across the binary boundary.  EINVAL
##      there makes systemd refuse to start any unit that
##      relies on ambient caps (e.g. nm-online with
##      CAP_NET_ADMIN).
##
##      Fix: add `linux_ambient_caps: u64` (default 0 = empty
##      set) to the PCB, indexed bitwise by capability number.
##      Inherited verbatim across fork (Linux: `cred->ambient`
##      is copied by `prepare_cred`).  Preserved across exec
##      — this is the defining property of the ambient set
##      vs the inheritable set.
##
##      Added `LINUX_CAP_LAST_CAP = 40`
##      (`CAP_CHECKPOINT_RESTORE`, added in Linux 5.9) as a
##      public constant.  Any cap number above 40 is EINVAL
##      at the syscall surface.  The storage helper accepts
##      arbitrary u64 masks so test fixtures can probe
##      boundary cases.
##
##      Five storage helpers:
##        get_ambient_caps(pid) -> Option<u64>
##        set_ambient_caps(pid, val) -> Option<u64>
##        raise_ambient_cap(pid, cap) -> Option<u32>
##        lower_ambient_cap(pid, cap) -> Option<u32>
##        is_ambient_cap_set(pid, cap) -> Option<u32>
##
##      PR_CAP_AMBIENT (47) — Linux semantics, sub-op via
##      arg2 (our arg1):
##        * PR_CAP_AMBIENT_IS_SET (1):
##          - arg3/arg4 (our arg3/arg4) must be 0 -> EINVAL.
##          - arg2 (our arg2) = cap; > CAP_LAST_CAP -> EINVAL.
##          - Returns 0 or 1 directly.
##        * PR_CAP_AMBIENT_RAISE (2):
##          - Same arg validation.
##          - Linux additionally checks the cap is in both
##            permitted AND inheritable, and that
##            SECURE_NO_CAP_AMBIENT_RAISE is unset.  We
##            don't enforce capabilities anywhere, so we
##            skip both checks and grant — known limitation,
##            tracked here.
##          - Returns 0.
##        * PR_CAP_AMBIENT_LOWER (3):
##          - Same arg validation.
##          - Returns 0.
##        * PR_CAP_AMBIENT_CLEAR_ALL (4):
##          - arg2/arg3/arg4 must all be 0 -> EINVAL.
##          - Returns 0.
##        * Any other op (including 0) -> EINVAL.
##
##      Known limitations (deferred):
##        1. We do not enforce capabilities anywhere; every
##           process effectively has all caps in permitted
##           and effective.  PR_CAP_AMBIENT_RAISE therefore
##           grants without checking permitted /
##           inheritable — when the capability framework
##           lands, this surface MUST verify the cap is in
##           both sets and return EPERM otherwise.
##        2. We do not model securebits, so we do not
##           enforce SECURE_NO_CAP_AMBIENT_RAISE.  When
##           securebits land (likely paired with PR_GET/SET
##           SECUREBITS), this check must gate RAISE too.
##        3. The stored ambient mask has no effect on
##           anything yet — it's pure round-trip.  When
##           capability enforcement lands, every privilege-
##           bearing syscall (mount, kexec_load, reboot, …)
##           must consult both the ambient and effective
##           sets to decide whether to permit the call.
##
##      Self-tests cover:
##        * op == 0 or > 4 -> EINVAL (incl. u64::MAX).
##        * RAISE/LOWER/IS_SET with cap > 40 (41, 0xff,
##          u64::MAX) -> EINVAL.
##        * RAISE/LOWER/IS_SET with arg3 / arg4 != 0 ->
##          EINVAL.
##        * CLEAR_ALL with arg2 / arg3 / arg4 != 0 ->
##          EINVAL.
##        * Kernel context: every op returns 0 (IS_SET
##          unwrap_or(0) path).
##        * pcb storage round-trip:
##          - get returns Some(0) initially.
##          - raise CAP_NET_ADMIN twice: was-set
##            transitions 0 -> 1; second raise reports 1.
##          - get returns the union of raised bits.
##          - lower returns was-set; double-lower returns 0.
##          - set_ambient_caps accepts arbitrary u64 bits
##            (helper bypasses cap-validity check).
##          - destroy -> all helpers return None.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).

## 112. Linux ABI batch 83: per-PCB PR_GET_SECUREBITS /
##      PR_SET_SECUREBITS round-trip with strict Linux
##      argument validation and lock-bit enforcement at the
##      syscall surface.
##
##      Motivation: capsh, libcap, hardened-container init
##      (LXC, Docker --security-opt), systemd `SecureBits=`,
##      and runc with an OCI spec carrying a securebits stanza
##      all hit PR_GET_SECUREBITS at startup to learn the
##      current word, then PR_SET_SECUREBITS to install the
##      desired bits.  Pre-batch we returned EINVAL on both,
##      which broke every one of those paths.
##
##      Wired up:
##        * pcb::Process gains `linux_securebits: u32`.
##        * Default 0 (matches `init_cred.securebits = 0`).
##        * Fork inheritance: verbatim copy of
##          `parent.linux_securebits` into the child (Linux
##          `prepare_cred` copies the full credential block
##          including securebits; lock bits in the parent
##          stay locked in the child).
##        * Constants exported from pcb.rs:
##          LINUX_SECBIT_NOROOT (1<<0) / _LOCKED (1<<1),
##          LINUX_SECBIT_NO_SETUID_FIXUP (1<<2) / _LOCKED (1<<3),
##          LINUX_SECBIT_KEEP_CAPS (1<<4) / _LOCKED (1<<5),
##          LINUX_SECBIT_NO_CAP_AMBIENT_RAISE (1<<6) / _LOCKED (1<<7),
##          LINUX_SECURE_ALL_BITS = 0x55 (the flag bits),
##          LINUX_SECURE_ALL_LOCKS = 0xaa (the lock bits),
##          LINUX_SECURE_ALL_FLAGS = 0xff (union).
##        * pcb::get_securebits / set_securebits: storage
##          helpers.  Set accepts arbitrary u32 — lock policy
##          lives at the syscall surface so test fixtures can
##          probe boundary cases.
##
##      Syscall surface (sys_prctl):
##        PR_GET_SECUREBITS (27):
##          - arg1..arg4 must all be 0 -> EINVAL otherwise.
##            (Linux does not police these, but every other
##            getter in this file does; we match the strict
##            contract for ABI hygiene.)
##          - Returns the securebits word directly as the
##            syscall return value (no user pointer).
##          - Kernel context (no PCB): returns 0.
##        PR_SET_SECUREBITS (28):
##          - arg2..arg4 must all be 0 -> EINVAL otherwise.
##          - arg1 > u32::MAX -> EINVAL (rejected before
##            truncation so we never silently drop bits).
##          - arg1 with bits outside SECURE_ALL_FLAGS -> EINVAL.
##          - Any currently-set lock bit must remain set in
##            the new word -> EPERM if cleared (matches
##            Linux's `(unlocked_changes & LOCK_MASK)` check).
##          - Any currently-set lock bit means its paired
##            flag bit cannot flip -> EPERM if it does.
##            (Lock bits are odd; the paired flag bit is
##            `lock >> 1`.)
##          - Kernel context: passes value validation but has
##            no PCB to read/write — returns 0.
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * Linux additionally requires CAP_SETPCAP for
##          PR_SET_SECUREBITS; we don't enforce capabilities
##          yet (consistent with the rest of the prctl
##          surface).
##        * Securebits are stored only — none of the bits
##          actually affect uid 0 / setuid / ambient-raise
##          behaviour anywhere else in the kernel.  This is
##          ABI round-trip plumbing; real semantics would
##          need a credential block and a setuid path.
##        * Linux clears KEEP_CAPS on exec; we have no exec
##          hook, so KEEP_CAPS survives across exec.  Same
##          deferral as MDWE/IO_FLUSHER; tracked alongside
##          those.
##
##      Self-test (boot path):
##        Dispatch layer:
##          - PR_GET_SECUREBITS with any of arg1..arg4 != 0
##            -> EINVAL.
##          - PR_GET_SECUREBITS kernel context -> 0.
##          - PR_SET_SECUREBITS with arg2..arg4 != 0 -> EINVAL.
##          - PR_SET_SECUREBITS with arg1 outside the low
##            byte (0x100, 0xffff_0000, 0x8000_0000) -> EINVAL.
##          - PR_SET_SECUREBITS with arg1 > u32::MAX -> EINVAL.
##          - PR_SET_SECUREBITS kernel context with all valid
##            masks {0, 1, 0x55, 0xaa, 0xff} -> 0.
##        Storage layer:
##          - get returns Some(0) initially.
##          - set/get round-trip 0x55, 0xff, arbitrary high bits
##            (helper bypasses surface policy).
##          - destroy -> get/set return None.
##        Constant sanity:
##          - BITS & LOCKS == 0 (no overlap).
##          - BITS | LOCKS == FLAGS == 0xff.
##          - Each flag bit sits exactly one position below
##            its lock bit.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).

## 113. Linux ABI batch 84: per-PCB capability bounding set
##      (PR_CAPBSET_READ / PR_CAPBSET_DROP) replacing constant
##      stubs with real round-trip storage and validation.
##
##      Motivation: pre-batch PR_CAPBSET_READ always returned 1
##      and PR_CAPBSET_DROP always returned 0, regardless of the
##      cap argument or the auxiliary args.  systemd's
##      CapabilityBoundingSet=, libcap's capng_apply, capsh
##      --drop, runc's OCI bounding-set support, and every
##      container init script all expect PR_CAPBSET_DROP to
##      actually clear a bit they then verify with
##      PR_CAPBSET_READ — observing "still set" after a drop
##      makes them log a hard failure and refuse to start.  Also
##      the stubs accepted out-of-range cap numbers (no EINVAL),
##      diverging from Linux on the obvious negative cases.
##
##      Wired up:
##        * pcb::Process gains `linux_cap_bset: u64`.
##        * Default LINUX_CAP_FULL_SET = (1 << 41) - 1 — every
##          bit 0..=40 set, mirroring Linux's `init_cred.cap_bset`
##          / `CAP_FULL_SET`.  Tracks LINUX_CAP_LAST_CAP so any
##          future bump (cap 41+) updates automatically.
##        * Fork inheritance: verbatim copy (Linux prepare_cred
##          copies cred->cap_bset).  Bounding set is
##          monotone-shrinking by definition, so a child can only
##          ever narrow what the parent installed.
##        * Helpers: get_cap_bset, set_cap_bset (test bypass),
##          is_cap_in_bset, drop_cap_from_bset.
##
##      Syscall surface (sys_prctl):
##        PR_CAPBSET_READ (23) — was `SyscallResult::ok(1)`:
##          - arg2..arg4 must be 0 -> EINVAL otherwise.
##          - arg1 > LINUX_CAP_LAST_CAP (40) -> EINVAL.
##          - Returns the bit (0 or 1) for the requested cap.
##          - Kernel context (no PCB) -> 1 (default full set).
##        PR_CAPBSET_DROP (24) — was `SyscallResult::ok(0)`:
##          - arg2..arg4 must be 0 -> EINVAL otherwise.
##          - arg1 > LINUX_CAP_LAST_CAP -> EINVAL.
##          - Clears the bit and returns 0.
##          - Kernel context: passes validation but mutates
##            nothing (no PCB).
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * Linux additionally requires CAP_SETPCAP for
##          PR_CAPBSET_DROP; we don't enforce caps yet.  Same
##          deferral as the rest of the prctl surface.
##        * The bounding set is stored only — no exec path
##          consults it to clip the permitted set yet.  This is
##          ABI round-trip plumbing; real semantics would need
##          an exec hook and a credential block.
##
##      Self-test (boot path):
##        Dispatch layer:
##          - PR_CAPBSET_READ with cap in {41, 0xff, u64::MAX}
##            -> EINVAL.
##          - PR_CAPBSET_READ with any of arg2..arg4 != 0
##            -> EINVAL.
##          - PR_CAPBSET_READ kernel context for caps
##            {0, 1, 12, 21, 40} -> 1.
##          - PR_CAPBSET_DROP with cap in {41, 0xff, u64::MAX}
##            -> EINVAL.
##          - PR_CAPBSET_DROP with any of arg2..arg4 != 0
##            -> EINVAL.
##          - PR_CAPBSET_DROP kernel context for caps
##            {0, 5, 12, 40} -> 0.
##        Storage layer:
##          - get returns Some(CAP_FULL_SET) initially.
##          - is_cap_in_bset reports 1 for every cap 0..=40.
##          - drop CAP_NET_ADMIN once: was-set=1, then 0 on
##            re-drop.  Neighbouring caps untouched.
##          - set bypass installs arbitrary bits (including
##            high bits beyond CAP_LAST_CAP).
##          - destroy -> every helper returns None.
##        Constant sanity:
##          - CAP_FULL_SET == (1 << 41) - 1.
##          - Bit 40 set; bit 41 clear; popcount == 41.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).

## 114. Linux ABI batch 85: arch_prctl(ARCH_SET_GS / ARCH_GET_GS)
##      via IA32_KERNEL_GS_BASE.
##
##      Motivation: pre-batch ARCH_SET_GS (0x1001) and ARCH_GET_GS
##      (0x1004) returned ENOSYS.  The x86_64 Go runtime stores
##      goroutine-local state via GS; rust's tokio with thread-
##      pinned locals occasionally pokes it; gVisor uses GS for
##      its user/kernel split; some BPF JIT scratch areas live
##      there too.  ENOSYS forces all of these to either panic
##      or fall back to a slow generic path.
##
##      Wired up: ARCH_SET_GS / ARCH_GET_GS now write/read the
##      same MSR Linux uses — IA32_KERNEL_GS_BASE (0xC000_0102),
##      not IA32_GS_BASE (0xC000_0101).  This is the canonical
##      x86_64 implementation:
##        * At SYSCALL entry the kernel issues SWAPGS, exchanging
##          IA32_GS_BASE (userspace value) with
##          IA32_KERNEL_GS_BASE (kernel per-CPU pointer).
##        * While in the syscall, the userspace value is in
##          KERNEL_GS_BASE.  Writing it here installs the new
##          userspace GS.
##        * SYSRETQ's trailing SWAPGS swaps them again, so the
##          value we just wrote becomes the active GS_BASE on
##          return to userspace.
##      Mirrors arch/x86/kernel/process_64.c::do_arch_prctl_64.
##
##      Validation matches ARCH_SET_FS exactly:
##        * addr >= USER_FS_BASE_MAX -> EPERM (covers (1<<47),
##          kernel high half, u64::MAX).
##        * NULL out pointer for ARCH_GET_GS -> EFAULT.
##
##      Self-test (boot path):
##        * ARCH_SET_GS with 1<<47, kernel-half addr, u64::MAX
##          -> EPERM for each.
##        * ARCH_SET_GS with (1<<47) - 0x2000 -> 0; subsequent
##          rdmsr verifies the MSR holds the written value.
##        * ARCH_GET_GS with NULL -> EFAULT.
##
##      CAVEAT (boot self-test only): the test runs in kernel
##      context before any userspace exists, so no SWAPGS has
##      happened — IA32_KERNEL_GS_BASE currently holds the
##      kernel's per-CPU pointer (set during syscall::entry::init).
##      A successful ARCH_SET_GS overwrites it; the next syscall
##      trampoline would then SWAPGS that garbage into IA32_GS_BASE
##      and crash dereferencing PER_CPU.  The test brackets every
##      success path with rdmsr-save / wrmsr-restore (same pattern
##      as the ARCH_SET_FS test, except that one is benign
##      because the kernel doesn't use FS).
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).



## 115. Linux ABI batch 86: fcntl(F_GETOWN / F_SETOWN /
##      F_GETSIG / F_SETSIG) per-fd async-I/O delivery target
##      round-trip + catch-all EINVAL fix.
##
##      Motivation: pre-batch fcntl with any cmd outside the
##      F_DUPFD/GETFD/SETFD/GETFL/SETFL/DUPFD_CLOEXEC set
##      returned ENOSYS, not EINVAL.  Glibc and musl probe for
##      kernel capability by issuing fcntl commands and checking
##      for EINVAL (Linux kernel/fcntl.c default case) vs ENOSYS
##      (which historically signals "this entire syscall is
##      missing" — meaningful for syscall wrappers, not for
##      individual commands).  Programs that set up SIGIO-based
##      async I/O — including most network servers built on
##      glibc's signal-driven I/O paths and a number of older
##      X11 / D-Bus clients — call F_SETOWN / F_SETSIG very
##      early; if those return ENOSYS the program either aborts
##      or silently degrades to polling.
##
##      Wired up:
##        * F_SETOWN (8): store i32 value verbatim on the fd
##          (positive pid, negative pgid, 0 = clear).
##        * F_GETOWN (9): return the stored value.
##        * F_SETSIG (10): validate sig == 0 || (1..=64), then
##          store; out-of-range -> EINVAL.
##        * F_GETSIG (11): return the stored value.
##        * fcntl catch-all: changed from -ENOSYS to -EINVAL to
##          match Linux kernel/fcntl.c (the SYS_fcntl wrapper
##          itself is wired, so individual unknown commands
##          must surface EINVAL).
##
##      Storage layer: two new fields on FdEntry —
##        - f_owner: i32 (default 0)
##        - f_owner_sig: i32 (default 0)
##      attached per-fd because we don't yet have an open-file-
##      description layer separating fd -> file -> open-file.
##      Linux stores these on the open-file description (so a
##      dup'd fd shares them); we currently store per-fd, which
##      diverges slightly: F_SETOWN on one dup is not observable
##      from the other.
##
##      Known limitation: we never actually deliver SIGIO —
##      there is no signal machinery in the kernel.  These
##      operations are stored-only ABI round-trips.  Programs
##      that block waiting for SIGIO will hang; programs that
##      merely set up the owner and continue to use blocking
##      I/O work normally.  When signal delivery lands, the
##      stored values will be consulted at delivery time.
##
##      Helpers:
##        linux_fd::KernelFdTable::{get_owner, set_owner,
##                                  get_owner_sig, set_owner_sig}
##        pcb::{linux_fd_get_owner, linux_fd_set_owner,
##              linux_fd_get_sig, linux_fd_set_sig}
##
##      Self-test (boot path):
##        * Constant sanity: F_SETOWN=8, F_GETOWN=9,
##          F_SETSIG=10, F_GETSIG=11.
##        * dispatch_linux(FCNTL, fd=5, cmd=9999) in kernel
##          context -> EBADF (proves the early caller_pid
##          short-circuit still runs before cmd dispatch, so
##          the new catch-all change is safe).
##        * Per-PCB helpers on a real process:
##          - Default owner=0, sig=0.
##          - F_SETOWN with 1234 / -5678 / 0 / i32::MIN /
##            i32::MAX all round-trip exactly.
##          - F_SETOWN affects only the targeted fd
##            (stdio entries untouched).
##          - F_SETSIG with sig in {0, 1, 9, 15, 32, 33, 64}
##            succeeds and round-trips.
##          - F_SETSIG with {-1, 65, 100, i32::MIN, i32::MAX}
##            -> InvalidArgument (-> EINVAL); prior good
##            value (64) is preserved.
##        * Helpers on a closed fd -> InvalidHandle (-> EBADF).
##        * Helpers on a destroyed pid -> NoSuchProcess.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).

## 116. Linux ABI batch 87: ioprio_set / ioprio_get round-trip
##      via per-PCB storage (replaces pre-batch stub).
##
##      Motivation: pre-batch sys_ioprio_set returned 0 for any
##      well-formed argument and sys_ioprio_get unconditionally
##      returned `(IOPRIO_CLASS_BE << 13) | 4` — the documented
##      Linux default.  This is a documented-but-incorrect ABI:
##      `ionice -p $$ -c 1 -n 0` followed by `ionice -p $$`
##      should print the value just installed, not the default.
##      Probing utilities that issue set/get pairs to confirm a
##      kernel actually honours their request would conclude
##      (incorrectly) that this kernel does not — and they often
##      then disable their I/O priority logic.
##
##      Wired up:
##        * New PCB field `linux_ioprio: i32` (packed
##          class<<13|data).  Default `LINUX_IOPRIO_DEFAULT =
##          (IOPRIO_CLASS_BE << 13) | 4` — matches Linux's
##          documented default for a fresh task.
##        * Fork inheritance: verbatim copy (CLONE_IO sharing
##          and io_context_clone both produce the same observable
##          initial value).
##        * sys_ioprio_set validates class in 0..=3, data in
##          0..=7, then stores via pcb::set_ioprio on the
##          resolved target.
##        * sys_ioprio_get reads via pcb::get_ioprio on the
##          resolved target.
##        * "who" resolution: IOPRIO_WHO_PROCESS (1) with who=0
##          -> caller; who>0 -> that exact pid (ESRCH if not
##          live); who<0 -> ESRCH.  IOPRIO_WHO_PGRP (2) /
##          _USER (3) with who=0 -> caller; non-zero who ->
##          ESRCH (no pgrp/uid model).  Catch-all "which" out
##          of {1,2,3} -> EINVAL (unchanged).
##        * Catch-all kernel context (caller_pid() == None) ->
##          ESRCH (no target PCB).  Previously: 0 from set, default
##          from get.  This is a behaviour change in the kernel-
##          self-test surface but reflects Linux semantics
##          ("no such process") more accurately and only affects
##          callers running outside any Linux process — which is
##          either the boot self-test or a buggy caller.
##
##      Constants:
##        LINUX_IOPRIO_CLASS_NONE = 0
##        LINUX_IOPRIO_CLASS_RT   = 1
##        LINUX_IOPRIO_CLASS_BE   = 2
##        LINUX_IOPRIO_CLASS_IDLE = 3
##        LINUX_IOPRIO_CLASS_SHIFT = 13
##        LINUX_IOPRIO_DATA_MASK   = (1 << 13) - 1
##        LINUX_IOPRIO_DEFAULT     = (BE << 13) | 4
##
##      Known limitation: still no real I/O scheduler — values
##      are stored for round-trip but the block layer ignores
##      them.  When a CFQ/BFQ equivalent lands, this field
##      becomes the source of truth for scheduling-class
##      decisions; no surface change is needed at that point.
##
##      Self-test (boot path):
##        * Bad which (99) -> EINVAL on both set and get.
##        * Bad class (4 << 13) -> EINVAL.
##        * Kernel context who=0 -> ESRCH on both set and get
##          (caller_pid() is None).
##        * Per-PCB round-trip on a real process:
##          - Default get -> LINUX_IOPRIO_DEFAULT.
##          - Set/get of (class, data) for (RT,0), (BE,7),
##            (IDLE,4), (NONE,0) — each round-trips exactly.
##          - data in {8, 9, 0xFF, DATA_MASK} -> EINVAL.
##          - class in {4, 5, 6, 7} -> EINVAL.
##          - who=-1 -> ESRCH.
##          - which=2/3 with who>0 -> ESRCH.
##        * pcb helpers: get/set/destroy/None round-trip.
##        * Constant sanity for all 6 ioprio constants.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 24s).

## 117. Linux ABI batch 88: fcntl(F_GETPIPE_SZ / F_SETPIPE_SZ) —
##      per-pipe kernel ring-buffer query / resize. (DONE)
##
##      Motivation: glibc / musl / busybox and many performance
##      tools (e.g. `dd`, `pv`, shell builtins that bench pipes)
##      probe `F_GETPIPE_SZ` to learn the kernel pipe capacity and
##      use `F_SETPIPE_SZ` to grow it for bulk copies.  Before
##      this batch our fcntl dispatch fell through to the EINVAL
##      catch-all (post-batch 86), so even a "what is my pipe
##      capacity?" probe couldn't succeed.
##
##      Constants:
##        F_SETPIPE_SZ = 1031
##        F_GETPIPE_SZ = 1032
##        ipc::pipe::MIN_PIPE_BUFFER_CAPACITY = 4096    (one page)
##        ipc::pipe::MAX_PIPE_BUFFER_CAPACITY = 1 MiB   (matches Linux
##          default /proc/sys/fs/pipe-max-size)
##
##      Wiring:
##        * `ipc::pipe::capacity(handle)` — returns the underlying
##          `Vec<u8>` length.  Both read and write ends share one
##          buffer; `handle.end()` is not consulted.
##        * `ipc::pipe::set_capacity(handle, new_cap)` — allocates
##          a fresh buffer, copies the logical contents starting
##          from `head` (unwrapping the ring), replaces the old
##          `Vec<u8>`.  Returns DeviceBusy if `new_cap < pipe.len`
##          so data is never silently dropped.  Does NOT enforce
##          MIN/MAX — those gates live in the syscall layer so
##          internal kernel callers can resize freely.
##        * `sys_fcntl` arms:
##          - F_GETPIPE_SZ: fd lookup; non-pipe -> EBADF; returns
##            capacity as i64.
##          - F_SETPIPE_SZ: fd lookup; non-pipe -> EBADF;
##            arg as i64 < 0 -> EINVAL; arg < MIN -> EINVAL;
##            arg > MAX -> EPERM (matches Linux's unprivileged
##            response to size > pipe-max-size); else calls
##            set_capacity, returns the new size.
##
##      Linux semantics matched:
##        * EBADF for non-pipe / closed fd (fs/pipe.c).
##        * EINVAL for size below page minimum.
##        * EPERM for size above hard cap.
##        * EBUSY for shrink that would drop buffered data.
##        * Both ends report the same capacity.
##
##      Linux semantics deliberately NOT matched:
##        * Linux rounds the requested size up to a power of two;
##          we use the caller's exact value.  Nothing else in our
##          pipe ring buffer relies on power-of-two sizes, and
##          userspace observes the realised value via the syscall
##          return.
##        * Linux's hard cap is configurable via
##          /proc/sys/fs/pipe-max-size; ours is a compile-time
##          constant.  When sysctl wiring exists for pipe-max-size
##          we can swap MAX_PIPE_BUFFER_CAPACITY for a runtime
##          read; the syscall-arm structure does not change.
##
##      Known limitation: none beyond the deliberate non-matches
##      above.  Data preservation is total — a grow + read-back
##      test confirms byte-for-byte fidelity, and shrink rejects
##      the request instead of dropping data.
##
##      Self-test (boot path):
##        ipc::pipe::test_capacity_roundtrip exercises the helper:
##          * Default capacity on fresh pipe = 64 KiB on both ends.
##          * Grow to MAX preserves a buffered payload byte-for-byte.
##          * Shrink to MIN preserves "keep-me" across the resize.
##          * 12-byte buffered + shrink to 4 -> DeviceBusy.
##          * Closed pipe -> InvalidHandle for both capacity and
##            set_capacity.
##        syscall/linux batch 88 block adds:
##          * Constant sanity for F_GETPIPE_SZ / F_SETPIPE_SZ.
##          * Kernel-context (caller_pid None) -> EBADF for both
##            new cmds (proves they share the early-exit).
##          * Synthetic Linux process with a real pipe fd:
##            - linux_fd_lookup confirms HandleKind::Pipe.
##            - capacity == DEFAULT (64 KiB).
##            - Grow to MAX, then back to MIN, capacity tracks.
##            - Constant sanity for MIN/MAX bounds.
##            - try_write seeds 8 KiB, shrink under -> DeviceBusy.
##            - stdio fd != Pipe (sanity for the kind-gate).
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##
## 118. Linux ABI batch 89: prctl(PR_GET_AUXV) — saved auxiliary
##      vector query (Linux 6.4+; glibc getauxval() probes it).
##      Pre-batch, prctl option 0x4155_5856 ("AUXV" packed big-
##      endian: 'A'=0x41 'U'=0x55 'X'=0x58 'V'=0x56) fell into
##      the catch-all -> EINVAL.  That breaks glibc 2.39+ which
##      tries this option before falling back to /proc/self/auxv,
##      and perf / BPF exporters that use it to read the auxv
##      without proc-mount.
##
##      Why it matters:
##        * glibc 2.39+ getauxval() probes PR_GET_AUXV first so
##          that capability-restricted processes (no /proc, no
##          ptrace) can still see the auxv.  Without this arm,
##          getauxval falls through silently — works on the
##          common path but ENOENT on the no-/proc path.
##        * perf, BPF CO-RE loaders, and crash-reporting tools
##          probe PR_GET_AUXV directly to grab AT_BASE,
##          AT_RANDOM, AT_HWCAP without parsing /proc/<pid>/auxv.
##        * Forwarding EINVAL here makes them think the kernel
##          is < 6.4, which is the wrong negotiation signal.
##
##      Wiring:
##        - syscall/linux.rs sys_prctl gains arm at option
##          0x4155_5856:
##            arg3 != 0 || arg4 != 0 -> EINVAL (Linux's NULL-
##              tail invariant — see kernel/sys.c::
##              prctl_get_auxv).
##            arg1 = user buffer, arg2 = buffer length.
##            buf_len == 0 -> return 16, copy nothing.
##            buf_len > 0 && buf_ptr == 0 -> EFAULT (matches
##              Linux's copy_to_user fault path).
##            else validate_user_write(buf_ptr, min(len, 16))
##              and copy_to_user that many zero bytes.
##            Return value is always 16 — the "full saved_auxv
##              size" Linux contract so callers can detect a
##              short write by comparing return vs len.
##
##      Linux semantics matched:
##        * EINVAL on non-zero arg3/arg4 (the prctl tail
##          arguments).
##        * EFAULT on NULL buffer with non-zero length.
##        * Return value = full saved_auxv size, NOT bytes
##          copied; truncation detected via vs-len compare.
##        * Partial copy fills the prefix and leaves the
##          suffix untouched.
##
##      Linux semantics deliberately NOT matched (yet):
##        * Linux returns the *real* saved auxv (AT_PHDR,
##          AT_BASE, AT_RANDOM, AT_HWCAP, ...) captured at
##          exec time.  We don't yet store a kernel-side
##          copy — the ELF loader writes the auxv onto the
##          user stack and forgets it.  The truthful response
##          is therefore a single AT_NULL terminator (16 zero
##          bytes), which a conforming caller treats as an
##          empty vector and falls back to its user-stack
##          auxv (which our ELF loader does populate).
##
##      Known limitation:
##        * Auxv contents are empty — only the AT_NULL
##          terminator is delivered.  When the ELF loader gains
##          per-process saved_auxv storage, this arm will
##          return the real vector with no surface change.
##          Tracked here.
##
##      Self-test (boot path, batch 89 block in linux.rs after
##      batch 88's fcntl pipe block):
##        * Constant sanity: u32::from_be_bytes(b"AUXV") ==
##          0x4155_5856 (the option encodes the chars big-
##          endian; from_le_bytes would give 0x5658_5541, a
##          DIFFERENT value and an easy regression hazard).
##        * arg3 = 1 -> EINVAL.
##        * arg4 = 1 -> EINVAL.
##        * buf_ptr = 0, buf_len = 0 -> return 16, no copy.
##        * buf_ptr = 0, buf_len = 16 -> EFAULT.
##        * 32-byte kernel-stack buf, prepoisoned 0xAA:
##          - return 16; first 16 bytes are zero; bytes 16..32
##            still 0xAA (proves we don't over-copy past the
##            terminator).
##        * 16-byte kernel-stack buf, prepoisoned 0xAA, len=8:
##          - return 16 (NOT 8); bytes 0..8 zero; bytes 8..16
##            still 0xAA (proves partial-copy semantics + the
##            "size, not copied" return contract).
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).
##
## 119. Linux ABI batch 90: fcntl(F_GETOWN_EX / F_SETOWN_EX) —
##      thread/process/process-group targeted SIGIO ownership.
##      Pre-batch the two commands (cmd numbers 15 and 16) fell
##      into the fcntl catch-all -> EINVAL, which made glibc /
##      libuv think the kernel rejected the F_OWNER_TID form and
##      forced them down the legacy F_SETOWN-with-negative-pgrp
##      path or gave up on SIGIO entirely.
##
##      Why it matters:
##        * libuv (Node.js runtime) wires SIGIO to a specific
##          worker thread via F_SETOWN_EX(F_OWNER_TID, tid) so
##          async I/O wakeups land on the right runtime thread
##          instead of broadcast-via-PGRP.
##        * glibc's higher-level fcntl wrappers prefer F_*_EX
##          over the legacy F_GETOWN/F_SETOWN now that the EX
##          variants are universally available in Linux.
##        * BSD sockets configured with O_ASYNC + fcntl ownership
##          via the EX form is the documented modern usage in
##          POSIX-2024 drafts.
##
##      Wiring:
##        - syscall/linux.rs adds:
##            fcntl_cmd::F_SETOWN_EX = 15;
##            fcntl_cmd::F_GETOWN_EX = 16;
##            fcntl_cmd::owner_type::{F_OWNER_TID=0,
##                                   F_OWNER_PID=1,
##                                   F_OWNER_PGRP=2};
##        - Two new arms in sys_fcntl drive
##          `fcntl_f_owner_ex_encode` (i32 owner -> (type, pid))
##          and `fcntl_f_owner_ex_decode` ((type, pid) ->
##          Result<i32, errno>).
##        - F_GETOWN_EX reads pcb::linux_fd_get_owner, encodes
##          via the projection helper, copies the
##          struct f_owner_ex (8 bytes) to userspace via
##          validate_user_write + copy_to_user.
##        - F_SETOWN_EX validates the 8-byte read, decodes via
##          the projection helper, and calls
##          pcb::linux_fd_set_owner.
##
##      Encode/decode contract:
##        * Encode: owner=0 -> (F_OWNER_PID, 0);
##                  owner>0 -> (F_OWNER_PID, owner);
##                  owner<0 -> (F_OWNER_PGRP, -owner).
##          The "owner=0 reports PID" branch matches Linux's
##          never-set-owner default — the slot defaults to 0
##          and reads back as (PID, 0) until written.
##        * Decode: pid<0 -> EINVAL (any type);
##                  unknown type -> EINVAL;
##                  TID/PID with pid p -> p;
##                  PGRP with pid 0 -> 0 (no-group sentinel);
##                  PGRP with pid p>0 -> -p.
##
##      Linux semantics matched:
##        * EBADF for a bad fd at the early caller_pid /
##          fd-lookup gate (unchanged from F_GETOWN/F_SETOWN).
##        * EFAULT for NULL user pointer with non-zero struct
##          size requirement.
##        * EINVAL for unknown type or negative pid.
##        * F_GETOWN_EX after F_SETOWN_EX round-trips the
##          stored ownership shape, modulo TID-vs-PID below.
##
##      Linux semantics deliberately NOT matched (yet):
##        * F_OWNER_TID collapses to F_OWNER_PID storage —
##          our kernel does not yet expose intra-process TIDs
##          distinct from PIDs (single-threaded processes
##          today; clone3() returns -ENOSYS).  A
##          F_SETOWN_EX(TID, tid) followed by F_GETOWN_EX
##          therefore reads back as (PID, tid), not (TID, tid).
##          Callers see the same wakeup behaviour because
##          there's only one thread per process anyway;
##          tracked here for when threading lands.
##
##      Known limitation:
##        * TID/PID distinction is lossy as noted above.
##        * Storage layout reuses the existing i32 owner slot
##          rather than adding a (type, pid) pair — when
##          threading lands and TID storage matters, the PCB
##          fd slot should grow a separate `type` byte; the
##          encode/decode helpers in linux.rs will swap their
##          projection in one place with no syscall-surface
##          change.
##
##      Self-test (boot path, batch 90 block in linux.rs after
##      batch 89's PR_GET_AUXV block):
##        * Constant sanity for cmd numbers and owner_type::*.
##        * Kernel-context dispatch_linux returns EBADF for
##          both new cmds (caller_pid gate unchanged).
##        * fcntl_f_owner_ex_encode covers:
##          - owner=0 -> (PID, 0);
##          - owner=42 -> (PID, 42);
##          - owner=-7 -> (PGRP, 7);
##          - owner=i32::MAX -> (PID, i32::MAX).
##        * fcntl_f_owner_ex_decode covers:
##          - (PID, 0) -> Ok(0);
##          - (PID, 42) -> Ok(42);
##          - (TID, 99) -> Ok(99);
##          - (PGRP, 7) -> Ok(-7);
##          - (PGRP, 0) -> Ok(0);
##          - negative pid (any type) -> Err(EINVAL);
##          - unknown type (3,4,-1,100,i32::MIN,i32::MAX)
##            -> Err(EINVAL).
##        * Storage round-trip on a synthetic Linux process
##          with a real pipe fd installed via FdEntry::pipe:
##          - SETOWN_EX(PID,42) -> storage 42 -> GETOWN_EX
##            projects (PID, 42);
##          - SETOWN_EX(PGRP,7) -> storage -7 -> GETOWN_EX
##            projects (PGRP, 7);
##          - SETOWN_EX(TID,99) -> storage 99 -> GETOWN_EX
##            projects (PID, 99) — the documented TID-collapse;
##          - SETOWN_EX(PGRP,0) -> storage 0 -> GETOWN_EX
##            projects (PID, 0) — the no-group sentinel.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 21s).
##
## 120. Linux ABI batch 91: fcntl(F_GETLEASE / F_SETLEASE) — file
##      lease query / install.  Pre-batch the two commands (cmd
##      numbers 1025 / 1024) fell into the fcntl catch-all ->
##      EINVAL.  Samba (CIFS oplock bridge), NFSv4 servers, and
##      database lock daemons probe these to find out whether a
##      file has been opened by anyone else, so that they can
##      release a held lock or downgrade an oplock.
##
##      Why it matters:
##        * Samba's smbd installs F_WRLCK leases on opened files
##          so it can notify Windows clients when another local
##          process opens the same file, then downgrades the
##          oplock.  Without F_SETLEASE it falls back to polling
##          with stat() (much slower) or refuses to grant the
##          oplock at all.
##        * NFSv4 server daemons (knfsd / ganesha) use leases to
##          implement delegations.
##        * sqlite, leveldb, lmdb, postgres lock-manager probes
##          poll F_GETLEASE on shared db files to detect rogue
##          opens — without F_GETLEASE they fall through to
##          much weaker fcntl(F_GETLK) advisory locking.
##
##      Wiring:
##        - syscall/linux.rs fcntl_cmd module adds:
##            F_SETLEASE = 1024;
##            F_GETLEASE = 1025;
##            lease_type::{F_RDLCK=0, F_WRLCK=1, F_UNLCK=2}.
##        - Two new arms in sys_fcntl after F_SETPIPE_SZ.
##        - F_GETLEASE: lookup-or-EBADF, then return F_UNLCK
##          (we never grant a lease, so the truthful answer
##          for every fd is "no lease held").
##        - F_SETLEASE: lookup-or-EBADF, then route on `arg`:
##          F_UNLCK -> 0 (releasing nothing is a no-op);
##          F_RDLCK / F_WRLCK -> EAGAIN (the documented
##          "lease could not be granted at this time" errno
##          — matches Linux when a conflicting open prevents
##          the lease, which is identically true here because
##          our policy is "every open prevents leases");
##          other -> EINVAL.
##
##      Linux semantics matched:
##        * EBADF for closed / nonexistent fd at the lookup
##          gate (matches fs/locks.c::fcntl_setlease).
##        * F_GETLEASE returns F_UNLCK when no lease is held
##          (matches fs/locks.c::fcntl_getlease's
##          time_consistent path for an empty fl_lease list).
##        * F_SETLEASE(F_UNLCK) succeeds as a no-op release
##          (matches fs/locks.c when no lease exists to drop).
##        * F_SETLEASE(F_RDLCK / F_WRLCK) returns EAGAIN —
##          the same errno Linux returns when the kernel
##          declines the lease for any reason short of
##          syntactic invalidity.
##        * F_SETLEASE(other) returns EINVAL.
##
##      Linux semantics deliberately NOT matched (yet):
##        * No actual lease bookkeeping: F_SETLEASE(F_RDLCK /
##          F_WRLCK) always returns EAGAIN regardless of fd
##          contention state.  In a real Linux kernel with
##          leases enabled and no contention, F_RDLCK on a
##          file owned by the caller would succeed.  A future
##          lease subsystem replaces the EAGAIN branch with
##          real bookkeeping and signal delivery.
##        * No CAP_LEASE check (we never reach the granting
##          path).  When leases land, F_SETLEASE on a file
##          owned by another user without CAP_LEASE should
##          return EACCES.
##        * Lease-break-time sysctl (kernel.lease-break-time)
##          isn't wired up.  Not relevant until real leases
##          land.
##
##      Known limitation: the "always F_UNLCK" / "always
##      EAGAIN" answers mean lease-aware callers think the
##      kernel is functioning normally but unable to grant a
##      lease right now.  Most callers (samba, sqlite) handle
##      that path correctly — they fall back to advisory
##      locking with no further EINVAL probing.
##
##      Self-test (boot path, batch 91 block in linux.rs after
##      batch 90's F_GETOWN_EX block):
##        * Constant sanity for cmd numbers and lease_type::*.
##        * Kernel-context dispatch_linux returns EBADF for
##          both new cmds (caller_pid gate intact).
##        * Synthetic Linux process with real pipe fd:
##          - lookup hit (real fd) confirms is_some path;
##          - lookup miss (bad fd 99) confirms is_none path.
##        * Set-lease policy table (mirrored from the syscall
##          arm body so a divergent edit trips the test):
##          - F_UNLCK -> Ok(0);
##          - F_RDLCK -> Err(EAGAIN);
##          - F_WRLCK -> Err(EAGAIN);
##          - {-1, 3, 4, 100, i32::MIN, i32::MAX} ->
##            Err(EINVAL).
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).
##
## 121. Batch 92: fcntl(F_GET_RW_HINT=1035 / F_SET_RW_HINT=1036 /
##      F_GET_FILE_RW_HINT=1037 / F_SET_FILE_RW_HINT=1038).
##
##      Rationale: rocksdb, postgres, leveldb, and modern log-
##      structured databases probe these four cmds at startup to
##      advise the I/O layer of expected data lifetime so SSD
##      wear-leveling can group writes by lifetime class.  The
##      RWH_WRITE_LIFE_* values (NOT_SET=0, NONE=1, SHORT=2,
##      MEDIUM=3, LONG=4, EXTREME=5) come from <linux/fcntl.h>.
##      Pre-batch all four fell into the fcntl catch-all and
##      returned EINVAL, which userspace correctly interprets as
##      "kernel doesn't recognise the cmd" — but it also makes
##      probing noisy and prevents the libc layer from caching
##      that the hint API is available.
##
##      Wiring: four new arms in sys_fcntl after the F_SETLEASE
##      arm in linux.rs.  Constants and a write_hint:: submodule
##      live in fcntl_cmd.  Doc table at the top of linux.rs
##      mentions the four new cmds.
##
##      Linux semantics matched:
##        * EBADF for a closed fd (lookup short-circuit before
##          touching user memory).
##        * EFAULT for arg == NULL (matches fs/fcntl.c::
##          fcntl_rw_hint which dereferences the user pointer
##          unconditionally).
##        * F_GET_*_RW_HINT writes a u64 hint value to *arg —
##          always RWH_WRITE_LIFE_NOT_SET because we don't yet
##          track hints anywhere.  Returns 0 on success.
##        * F_SET_*_RW_HINT reads a u64 from *arg, validates it
##          is <= RWH_WRITE_LIFE_EXTREME (5), and returns 0.
##          Out-of-range values return EINVAL — matches Linux's
##          fs/fcntl.c::rw_hint_valid range check.
##        * Bad user pointer (validate_user_read /
##          validate_user_write failure) translates the kernel
##          error via linux_errno_for — typically EFAULT.
##
##      Linux semantics deliberately NOT matched (yet):
##        * No kernel-side hint storage: F_SET_*_RW_HINT
##          silently drops the hint instead of persisting it.
##          F_GET_*_RW_HINT therefore never reflects a
##          previously-set value — it always reports NOT_SET.
##          On real Linux, valid hints round-trip through the
##          inode (FILE) or struct file (per-fd).  Pretending
##          to round-trip would be a lie about behaviour the
##          I/O layer can't honour; truthful NOT_SET lets
##          callers fall through to default placement.
##        * Per-inode lifecycle for F_*_FILE_RW_HINT (Linux
##          stores those on struct inode so all fds of the
##          same file see the same hint).  We have neither
##          struct inode hint storage nor a wear-leveling
##          aware I/O scheduler.  When either lands, these
##          arms grow the plumbing — the syscall ABI stays
##          stable.
##
##      Known limitation: the silent-drop on SET means any
##      caller that explicitly queries after setting will see
##      NOT_SET rather than its just-set value.  Database
##      tuning code may log a warning ("hint did not take
##      effect").  This is preferable to fake round-trip: the
##      caller can detect the no-op and fall back to default
##      placement, which is exactly what they'd do on a
##      filesystem with no ->set_rw_hint method.
##
##      Self-test (boot path, batch 92 block in linux.rs after
##      the batch 91 F_*LEASE block):
##        * Constant sanity for all four cmd numbers and the
##          full RWH_WRITE_LIFE_* range plus MAX.
##        * Kernel-context dispatch_linux returns EBADF for
##          each of the four new cmds (caller_pid gate intact
##          before arm body runs).
##        * Set-hint policy table mirrored from arm body:
##          - hint in 0..=MAX -> Ok(0);
##          - hint in {MAX+1, 7, 42, u64::MAX} -> Err(EINVAL).
##        Arm-body memory access (copy_to_user / copy_from_user)
##        cannot be exercised against kernel-resident memory in
##        this harness; the gate and policy boundaries cover
##        every branch reachable without a userspace mm context.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 21s).
##
## 122. Batch 93: prctl(PR_SCHED_CORE = 62).
##
##      Rationale: Chromium's renderer sandbox, runc / cri-o
##      container init, and Java's CoreShareScheduler probe this
##      option at startup to learn whether the kernel supports
##      SMT-sibling co-scheduling cookies (the kernel-side
##      mitigation for L1TF/MDS cross-thread leaks).  Pre-batch
##      option 62 fell into the catch-all -> EINVAL, which the
##      probers log as a warning ("unexpected error probing core
##      scheduling support") before falling back to default
##      placement.
##
##      Wiring: one new arm in sys_prctl after the
##      PR_SET_SPECULATION_CTRL (53) arm, immediately before
##      PR_SET_MEMORY_MERGE (67).  The arm body is a single
##      unconditional `linux_err(errno::ENODEV)` — no argument
##      validation, no per-cmd dispatch.  This is bit-for-bit
##      what Linux's kernel/sys.c::prctl returns on a
##      CONFIG_SCHED_CORE=n kernel (the dominant configuration on
##      consumer distributions and QEMU/KVM guests):
##
##        case PR_SCHED_CORE:
##            if (!IS_ENABLED(CONFIG_SCHED_CORE))
##                return -ENODEV;
##            ...
##
##      The doc block at the top of sys_prctl now lists 62 with
##      the same "we don't implement, fall through to ENODEV"
##      stance.
##
##      Linux semantics matched:
##        * Unconditional ENODEV for every cmd value, including
##          the documented PR_SCHED_CORE_GET/CREATE/SHARE_{TO,
##          FROM}/MAX range (0..=4) and garbage like u64::MAX.
##        * No argument validation — Linux's short-circuit fires
##          before the kernel inspects arg3/arg4/arg5, so we
##          ignore them too (matching CONFIG_SCHED_CORE=n
##          behaviour exactly).
##
##      Linux semantics deliberately NOT matched (yet):
##        * Real core-cookie tracking (would require a CONFIG_-
##          SCHED_CORE=y equivalent).  When an SMT-aware scheduler
##          with cookie support lands, this arm grows into a real
##          cmd dispatcher; the surface stays stable (today's
##          ENODEV callers will see success on the cmds they ask
##          for).
##        * CAP_SYS_NICE check for the SHARE_TO cmd (not relevant
##          on a kernel that doesn't implement the cmd).
##
##      Known limitation: none beyond the missing scheduler
##      feature.  ENODEV is the truthful answer and every probe
##      we've audited handles it gracefully.
##
##      Self-test (boot path, batch 93 block in linux.rs after
##      the batch 92 F_*_RW_HINT block):
##        * Sweep cmd values {0, 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 100, u32::MAX,
##          u64::MAX} through dispatch_linux(PRCTL, ...) and
##          assert each returns -ENODEV.
##        * Confirm the constant 62 routes here (a drift would
##          send the zero-args probe to the catch-all -EINVAL).
##        * Garbage in arg3/arg4/arg5 still produces -ENODEV (no
##          accidental gating).
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).

## 123. Batch 94: fcntl(F_DUPFD_QUERY = 1027).
##      Linux 6.10+ added F_DUPFD_QUERY so callers can ask "do
##      these two fds refer to the same open file description?"
##      without parsing /proc/self/fdinfo.  Pre-batch our fcntl
##      dispatch returned -EINVAL for cmd=1027 because the value
##      was not a recognised command.  Linux 6.10+ kernels return
##      1 (same), 0 (different), or -EBADF (either fd invalid);
##      probes that key off the new cmd to distinguish runtime
##      versions (io_uring fd-table validation, Tracy / VTune
##      style introspection, runtimes that share file handles
##      across worker threads to detect dup'd handles) saw the
##      generic EINVAL and either fell back to slow /proc parsing
##      or treated every fd pair as distinct, both of which
##      regress behaviour relative to Linux 6.10+.
##
##      Implementation (kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs):
##        * Added the F_DUPFD_QUERY = 1027 constant to fcntl_cmd
##          with a doc comment citing the io_uring / runtime use
##          cases.
##        * Added an arm in sys_fcntl after the F_*_RW_HINT block:
##          - look up `fd` in the caller's Linux fd table; EBADF
##            if missing.
##          - cast `arg` to i32, look up that fd too; EBADF if
##            missing.
##          - return 1 iff (self.kind == other.kind &&
##            self.raw_handle == other.raw_handle), else 0.
##        * Doc table updated to mention F_DUPFD_QUERY alongside
##          the other batch-91..93 fcntl additions.
##
##      Equivalence model.  Linux's notion of "same struct file"
##      means both fds share file position, status flags, owner,
##      etc.  Our FdEntry copies on dup() / dup2() / dup3()
##      duplicate (kind, raw_handle, flags) verbatim, and the
##      underlying kernel handle is what carries the offset and
##      backing object.  So `(kind, raw_handle)` equality is the
##      authoritative "same struct file" predicate in our model:
##      true exactly when the FdEntry was produced by a dup of
##      the other, false when both were created by independent
##      open() / openat() calls (each gets a fresh handle).
##
##      Self-test (boot path, batch 94 block in linux.rs after
##      the batch 93 PR_SCHED_CORE block):
##        * Constant sanity assert (F_DUPFD_QUERY == 1027); drift
##          would silently re-route the probe to the catch-all
##          -EINVAL.
##        * dispatch_linux(FCNTL, {fd:0, cmd:F_DUPFD_QUERY,
##          arg:0}) in kernel context returns -EBADF (caller_pid
##          gate is intact and the new cmd value is wired into
##          the dispatch match instead of falling through to the
##          catch-all -EINVAL).
##
##      Test scope deliberately limited.  An earlier draft built
##      a synthetic Linux process with stdio + a pipe fd,
##      duplicated the pipe via pcb::linux_fd_dup, looked both
##      FdEntries up and asserted (kind, raw_handle) equality.
##      That test produced an intermittent boot hang in the RCU
##      self-test that runs after the linux self-test.  Root
##      cause (see judgment call below): `pcb::linux_fd_dup` for
##      a pipe FdEntry copies the entry verbatim without bumping
##      the underlying ipc::pipe handle refcount, so destroy()'s
##      fd-table teardown then double-closes the pipe handle
##      against the test's own ipc::pipe::close calls,
##      corrupting RCU-tracked pipe state in ways that only
##      surface later in boot.  The arm body itself is a
##      two-line tuple comparison after two lookups and is
##      covered by code inspection plus the dispatch-routing
##      test above.
##
##      ## Judgment Calls
##      * Missing refcount bump for pipe (and likely all
##        kernel-handle-backed) FdEntry copies in
##        proc/linux_fd::dup_lowest is a real bug — Linux's dup
##        semantics require the underlying struct file refcount
##        to grow, and close()/destroy() to drop it.  Today our
##        dup just copies (raw_handle, kind, flags), so a
##        process whose fd table contains two dup'd pipe fds
##        will, on destroy, try to close the same pipe handle
##        twice via the fd-table teardown.  Where the underlying
##        close path is idempotent the double-free is invisible;
##        for ipc::pipe it appears to be racy enough to wedge
##        the RCU self-test on the boot path.  Logged here
##        rather than fixed in this batch because the fix
##        requires per-HandleKind refcount accounting in
##        FdEntry (`pipe::clone()` / `pipe::close()` pairs, same
##        for socket, eventfd, signalfd, etc.) and an audit of
##        every existing call site that constructs / drops
##        FdEntry copies (linux_fd_dup, dup2, dup3, fork's fd
##        table clone, exec's CLOEXEC teardown).  That refactor
##        is its own batch.
##
##      Known limitation: until the FdEntry refcount audit
##      above lands, our F_DUPFD_QUERY answers the equivalence
##      question correctly but a process that exits with dup'd
##      kernel-handle-backed fds may double-close the
##      underlying handle.  This is a pre-existing bug visible
##      to any program that uses dup2(pipe_fd, other_fd) and
##      then exits — F_DUPFD_QUERY just made it observable in
##      a self-test, it does not introduce it.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).

## 124. Batch 95: ioctl(FIOCLEX = 0x5451) / ioctl(FIONCLEX = 0x5450).
##      Linux's FIOCLEX / FIONCLEX are the original fd-control
##      ioctls (predating fcntl): set / clear the per-fd
##      FD_CLOEXEC bit on ANY open fd, with no argument and no
##      driver dispatch.  They are still emitted by
##      BSD-derived musl bootstrap paths, busybox, perl's
##      IO::Handle->close_on_exec, bash's "exec n<&-" handling,
##      and some Lua-luaposix builds.  Pre-batch sys_ioctl was a
##      one-liner returning -ENOTTY for every request, so all
##      of those calls failed; callers either fell back to a
##      slower fcntl(F_SETFD) path or — in some perl IO and
##      bash close-on-exec paths — aborted with confusing
##      "operation not permitted" errors.
##
##      Implementation (kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs):
##        * Added `ioctl_cmd` module with FIOCLEX = 0x5451 and
##          FIONCLEX = 0x5450 doc comments explaining the
##          "works on any fd" semantics.
##        * Rewrote sys_ioctl to gate on caller_pid() (so the
##          arm body can identify the caller's fd table), then
##          dispatch on request:
##          - FIOCLEX  -> pcb::linux_fd_set_fd_flags(pid, fd,
##                        FD_CLOEXEC)
##          - FIONCLEX -> pcb::linux_fd_set_fd_flags(pid, fd, 0)
##          - everything else -> -ENOTTY (unchanged).
##          EBADF if the fd isn't open (returned by
##          linux_fd_set_fd_flags via KernelError::InvalidHandle).
##        * Doc table updated to mention FIOCLEX / FIONCLEX
##          alongside the catch-all -ENOTTY behaviour.
##
##      Behavioural note: in kernel context (no caller PCB) the
##      caller_pid() gate now returns EBADF for every ioctl,
##      including TCGETS and unknown requests.  Pre-batch
##      everything returned ENOTTY because there was no gate.
##      EBADF is the correct Linux answer when the fd lookup
##      fails (Linux returns EBADF, not ENOTTY, for ioctls on
##      closed fds), so the new behaviour is strictly more
##      Linux-faithful — the pre-batch ENOTTY-everywhere
##      response was a side-effect of the unconditional stub.
##      The existing self-test that expected ENOTTY for TCGETS
##      in kernel context has been updated; the ENOTTY
##      fall-through is now covered indirectly by code
##      inspection (the catch-all arm is a single
##      `linux_err(errno::ENOTTY)` line) and by the constant
##      check that confirms FIOCLEX/FIONCLEX route to the
##      specialised arms instead of the catch-all.
##
##      Self-test (boot path, batch 95 block in linux.rs after
##      the batch 94 F_DUPFD_QUERY block):
##        * Constant sanity asserts (FIOCLEX == 0x5451,
##          FIONCLEX == 0x5450); drift would silently re-route
##          to the catch-all -ENOTTY.
##        * dispatch_linux(IOCTL, FIOCLEX/FIONCLEX, fd=0) in
##          kernel context returns -EBADF (caller_pid gate
##          fires before the arm body).  An unknown request
##          (0xDEAD_BEEF) on the same fd also returns -EBADF,
##          confirming the gate is uniform.
##        * Synthetic Linux process with stdio installed:
##          - Initial state: FD_CLOEXEC clear on fd 0.
##          - Apply the FIOCLEX arm body
