##            (linux_fd_set_fd_flags(FD_CLOEXEC)) and confirm
##            fd 0's fd_flags now have FD_CLOEXEC set.
##          - Apply the FIONCLEX arm body
##            (linux_fd_set_fd_flags(0)) and confirm fd_flags
##            return to 0.
##          - linux_fd_set_fd_flags(fd=99, FD_CLOEXEC) fails
##            (Err) — proves the helper actually rejects
##            invalid fds, so the EBADF guarantee isn't
##            vacuous.
##
##      Known limitation: FIONBIO (set/clear O_NONBLOCK via
##      ioctl) and FIONREAD (bytes available) are still
##      catch-all -ENOTTY.  Wiring FIONBIO requires the
##      status-flags helper and an O_NONBLOCK arg parse from
##      a user pointer; FIONREAD requires per-handle-kind
##      bytes-available accounting (pipe::available,
##      socket::available, etc.).  Both are follow-on batches.
##      TIOCGWINSZ (terminal size) also remains -ENOTTY since
##      we have no terminal device with column/row state yet.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 24s).

## 125. Batch 96: ioctl(FIONBIO = 0x5421).
##      FIONBIO is the legacy BSD ioctl for toggling
##      O_NONBLOCK on any fd: `arg` is a pointer to int,
##      non-zero sets O_NONBLOCK, zero clears it.  Apache,
##      dovecot, OpenSSH's libssh build, Redis tests, and most
##      BSD-style network frameworks emit this directly
##      instead of fcntl(F_SETFL, O_NONBLOCK).  Pre-batch
##      sys_ioctl returned -ENOTTY for FIONBIO (post-batch 95
##      after the caller_pid gate, -EBADF in kernel context
##      and -ENOTTY for user-space callers with valid fds),
##      breaking those callers.
##
##      Implementation (kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs):
##        * Added FIONBIO = 0x5421 constant to ioctl_cmd with
##          a doc comment citing the BSD socket-code use case.
##        * New arm in sys_ioctl after FIONCLEX:
##          - Look up `fd` in the caller's Linux fd table;
##            EBADF if missing.  Snapshot current status_flags.
##          - Validate `arg` is a non-NULL user pointer of
##            at least 4 bytes; EFAULT otherwise.
##          - copy_from_user(arg, &flag, 4) into a kernel i32.
##          - If flag != 0, set O_NONBLOCK on the snapshot;
##            else clear it.  Preserve all other bits
##            (access mode, O_APPEND).
##          - pcb::linux_fd_set_status_flags with the new
##            value; return 0 on success.
##        * Doc table updated to mention FIONBIO alongside
##          FIOCLEX / FIONCLEX.
##
##      Self-test (boot path, batch 96 block in linux.rs
##      after the batch 95 FIOCLEX / FIONCLEX block):
##        * Constant sanity (FIONBIO == 0x5421); drift would
##          re-route to the catch-all -ENOTTY.
##        * dispatch_linux(IOCTL, FIONBIO, fd=0, arg=NULL) in
##          kernel context returns -EBADF (caller_pid gate
##          fires before the arm body, so EFAULT from the
##          NULL arg is masked by EBADF — same as for an
##          ioctl on a closed fd in real Linux).
##        * Synthetic Linux process with stdio:
##          - Initial state: O_NONBLOCK clear on fd 0.
##          - Apply the set-O_NONBLOCK helper (mirroring the
##            arm body's linux_fd_set_status_flags call) and
##            verify O_NONBLOCK appears in fd 0's status_flags.
##          - Apply the clear-O_NONBLOCK helper and verify the
##            bit is gone.
##          - Verify the access-mode bits (O_ACCMODE) are
##            preserved across both toggles.
##          - linux_fd_set_status_flags(fd=99, O_NONBLOCK)
##            fails (Err), proving the helper rejects invalid
##            fds and the EBADF guarantee in the arm body is
##            not vacuous.
##
##      Known limitation: FIONREAD (bytes available to read)
##      still returns -ENOTTY.  Wiring it requires
##      per-HandleKind bytes-available accounting
##      (pipe::available, socket::available, eventfd value,
##      etc.) — that's a per-kind dispatch table best done
##      as its own batch.  TIOCGWINSZ also remains -ENOTTY
##      until we have a terminal device with column/row state.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).
##
## 126. Batch 97 — prctl(PR_SET_VMA) anonymous-VMA naming
##      (no-op accept).  COMPLETED 2026-06-05.
##
##      Motivation: Linux 5.17+ exposes
##      prctl(PR_SET_VMA = 0x5356_4d41, PR_SET_VMA_ANON_NAME = 0,
##      start, size, name) which lets userspace tag anonymous
##      VMAs with a short label that appears in
##      /proc/<pid>/maps as `[anon:<name>]`.  Started in Android
##      Bionic but now used by jemalloc, scudo, ART, libunwind,
##      and Chrome's PartitionAlloc to label arenas, thread
##      stacks, JIT regions, and guard pages.  Pre-batch our
##      sys_prctl returned -EINVAL (the catch-all) for any
##      unknown option, so every malloc/allocator call site
##      that probes it took the warn-and-skip path on startup.
##      Accepting it removes a per-process noise line and
##      brings us into ABI parity with mainline kernels.
##
##      Implementation (kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs):
##        * New 0x5356_4d41 arm in sys_prctl, gated on sub-op
##          arg1 == 0 (PR_SET_VMA_ANON_NAME).  Anything else
##          returns -EINVAL — same shape Linux uses.
##        * Validate start + size does not overflow u64
##          (mirrors madvise_set_anon_name's
##          `start + len_in < start` check); -EINVAL if so.
##        * NULL name pointer is accepted as "clear the name"
##          and returns 0 without touching user memory.
##        * Non-NULL name pointer:
##          - validate_user_read(ptr, 1) probe; surface its
##            errno on failure.
##          - copy_from_user up to ANON_VMA_NAME_MAX_LEN (80)
##            bytes into a kernel buffer.
##          - Locate NUL terminator; ENAMETOOLONG if absent.
##          - Reject empty (NUL at offset 0) with -EINVAL —
##            matches Linux's strndup_user behavior, where
##            zero-length names cannot be installed.
##          - Validate every byte before the NUL is printable
##            ASCII (0x20..=0x7e) excluding `\`, `` ` ``, `$`,
##            `[`, `]` — the forbidden-character set Linux
##            uses in is_valid_name_char().  First bad byte
##            yields -EINVAL.
##        * Validated name is DROPPED — we have no per-VMA
##          name storage layer yet.  The arm returns 0 so the
##          caller's flow continues but the name is not
##          visible anywhere.  Documented as a known
##          limitation in the arm's doc comment AND below in
##          this entry.
##        * Top-of-function sys_prctl doc table updated with
##          a PR_SET_VMA bullet explaining the accept-and-drop
##          stance and citing the Android/jemalloc/ART users.
##
##      Self-test (boot path, batch 97 block in linux.rs
##      after the batch 96 FIONBIO block):
##        1. Constant sanity: PR_SET_VMA == 0x5356_4d41 and
##           PR_SET_VMA_ANON_NAME == 0 used as named locals.
##        2. Unknown sub-op (arg1=1) -> -EINVAL.  Drift would
##           re-route to the catch-all (also -EINVAL but with
##           no sub-op gate).
##        3. NULL name + valid (start, size) -> 0.  The
##           "clear" form must work without reading user
##           memory; kernel context can reach the success
##           return via dispatch_linux.
##        4. Overflow (start = u64::MAX - 0x10000, size =
##           0x20000) -> -EINVAL.
##        5. Empty name buffer (NUL at offset 0) at a
##           kernel-mapped address -> -EINVAL.  Exercises the
##           "nul == 0" rejection.
##        6. Valid printable name "libc:malloc\0" at a
##           kernel-mapped address -> 0.
##        7. Forbidden character: name "foo[bar]\0" -> -EINVAL.
##           Exercises the is_valid_name_char loop.
##
##      Known limitation: validated names are silently
##      dropped — we have no per-VMA name storage to plumb
##      them into.  Callers that read /proc/<pid>/maps to
##      find a label they installed (Android Runtime's GC
##      diagnostic, scudo's heap dump) will see `[anon]`
##      where they expect `[anon:<their-name>]`.  Once the
##      VMA layer gains an optional Arc<str> label field, this
##      arm should propagate the name through.  Procfs maps
##      rendering will need a corresponding suffix emitter.
##
##      Judgment call: the copy_from_user we perform on the
##      name buffer is bulk-sized (80 bytes) and may fault
##      partway through if the user string is shorter than 80
##      bytes and abuts an unmapped page.  Linux's
##      strndup_user walks byte-by-byte and only faults if no
##      NUL appears within the readable run.  Documented in
##      the arm body's SAFETY comment; could be tightened
##      with a per-byte probe loop once we need closer Linux
##      parity for fault semantics.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##
## 127. Batch 98 — prctl(PR_SET_PTRACER) Yama-LSM ptracer pin
##      (no-op accept).  COMPLETED 2026-06-05.
##
##      Motivation:
##      prctl(PR_SET_PTRACER = 0x5961_6d61, pid) is the Yama LSM's
##      per-process pin of which PID is allowed to ptrace this task.
##      Probed by gdb-attach (every `gdb -p <pid>` chain), lldb-server,
##      strace, perf record, and Chromium's renderer crash handler at
##      startup so that their debugger / parent process can attach
##      under restricted ptrace_scope sysctl values.  Pre-batch
##      sys_prctl returned -EINVAL via the catch-all, so each probing
##      caller either logged a warning ("ptrace_scope is restricted,
##      attach may fail") or skipped the pin entirely.  Accepting it
##      silences that noise and matches the friendliest
##      Yama-configured Linux behaviour.
##
##      Implementation (kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs):
##        * New 0x5961_6d61 arm in sys_prctl, placed just before the
##          catch-all default.  Body:
##          - Enforce zero-tail auxiliaries: args.arg2 / arg3 / arg4
##            must all be 0; otherwise -EINVAL.
##          - Accept any args.arg1 value (PID_ANY = u64::MAX, 0
##            = "clear pin", or an arbitrary pid).  We do NOT
##            validate the pid against pcb existence — we have no
##            Yama policy that would enforce it.
##          - Return 0 (silent success) — the pin is informational
##            only; the actual ptrace-permission check (currently
##            wide-open) lives at the ptrace boundary.
##        * Top-of-function sys_prctl doc table updated with a
##          PR_SET_PTRACER bullet explaining the no-Yama stance and
##          citing gdb / lldb / strace / Chromium as primary callers.
##
##      Self-test (boot path, batch 98 block in linux.rs after the
##      batch 97 PR_SET_VMA block):
##        1. Constant sanity: PR_SET_PTRACER == 0x5961_6d61, with a
##           secondary cross-check that the value spells "Yama" when
##           bytes are laid out MSB-first (via from_be_bytes on the
##           4-byte ASCII slice).  Drift would re-route to the
##           -EINVAL catch-all and the success tests below would
##           fail noisily.
##        2. arg1 = 0 (clear) + all-zero auxiliaries -> 0.
##        3. arg1 = u64::MAX (PR_SET_PTRACER_ANY = -1) + all-zero
##           auxiliaries -> 0.
##        4. arg1 = 1234 (arbitrary plausible pid) + all-zero
##           auxiliaries -> 0.  Exercises the "we don't validate pid"
##           contract.
##        5. Non-zero arg2 / arg3 / arg4 individually -> -EINVAL.
##           Iterates all three auxiliary slots to prove the gate
##           covers each independently.
##
##      Caught-in-self-test bug (fixed within this batch): the
##      first-draft self-test included
##      `assert_eq!(PR_SET_PTRACER,
##                  u64::from_le_bytes(*b"Yama\0\0\0\0"))`
##      which fired in early boot (1499557217 != 1634558297) because
##      "Yama" packs into 0x5961_6d61 only when interpreted big-endian
##      ('Y' is the MSB).  Replaced with from_be_bytes on a 4-byte
##      slice (zero-extended into u64).  Documented as a reminder
##      that 4-byte prctl-magic constants in include/uapi/linux/prctl.h
##      use the big-endian / ASCII-reading-order convention; the same
##      pattern holds for PR_SET_VMA (0x5356_4d41 = "SVMA" big-endian)
##      and PR_GET_AUXV (0x4155_5856 = "AUXV" big-endian).
##
##      Known limitation: the requested ptracer pid is silently
##      dropped — we have no Yama-like ptrace_scope sysctl and no
##      per-process allow-list.  All ptrace attaches currently
##      succeed regardless of this pin (the friendlier end of the
##      Linux spectrum), so the discarded pin has no observable
##      effect except in distributed-tracing scenarios that expect
##      to be denied attach by a non-listed PID.  If we ever add a
##      ptrace-scope policy, this arm should persist the pid on the
##      PCB and the ptrace-attach path should consult it.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).
##
## 128. Batch 99 — arch_prctl(ARCH_GET_CPUID / ARCH_SET_CPUID)
##      CPUID-fault probe stub.  COMPLETED 2026-06-05.
##
##      Motivation:
##      arch_prctl(0x1011 / 0x1012) is the x86_64-specific Linux
##      4.12+ knob that queries / installs the per-thread
##      CPUID-disable bit (intel `IA32_MISC_ENABLE.LIMIT_CPUID_MAXVAL`
##      or AMD-equivalent on CPUs with X86_FEATURE_CPUID_FAULT).
##      glibc's startup checks GET to decide whether `cpuid` is
##      safe to issue; gdb / lldb / rr toggle SET to force
##      deterministic feature detection during record-replay;
##      certain V8 / OpenJDK JIT bring-up code disables CPUID to
##      pin a feature mask; multiple ifunc resolvers in glibc/musl
##      probe GET as a "is CPUID emulated for me?" sanity check.
##      Pre-batch our sys_arch_prctl returned -EINVAL via the
##      catch-all, so glibc fell back to the SIGSEGV-handler path
##      for CPUID and gdb's record mode complained about
##      non-deterministic boot.
##
##      Implementation (kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs):
##        * Added ARCH_GET_CPUID (0x1011) / ARCH_SET_CPUID (0x1012)
##          to the `const` block at the top of sys_arch_prctl
##          alongside the existing ARCH_SET_FS / GET_FS / SET_GS /
##          GET_GS codes.
##        * New arms after ARCH_GET_GS, before the catch-all:
##          - ARCH_GET_CPUID -> SyscallResult::ok(1).  No user
##            pointer; no auxiliary validation (Linux doesn't
##            police arg2/3/4 here).  The 1 mirrors what
##            get_cpuid_mode returns on CPUs without
##            X86_FEATURE_CPUID_FAULT — i.e. the typical Linux
##            return on hardware that physically cannot fault
##            CPUID.
##          - ARCH_SET_CPUID matches on the `addr` argument
##            (Linux's arg2): 0 -> -ENODEV (we cannot disable
##            CPUID), 1 -> 0 (idempotent re-enable), anything
##            else -> -EINVAL.  This is the bit-exact answer
##            set_cpuid_mode returns on a CPU without the fault
##            feature.
##        * sys_arch_prctl doc comment expanded to mention CPUID
##          arms and call out the "fault-feature-absent CPU"
##          equivalence.
##        * Top-of-file doc table (line 79) split into three
##          rows to list ARCH_SET_GS / ARCH_GET_GS / ARCH_GET_CPUID
##          / ARCH_SET_CPUID alongside the FS pair.
##
##      Self-test (boot path, slotted as "(9c) Batch 99" between
##      the existing GS-restore and the timespec round-trip):
##        1. ARCH_GET_CPUID -> 1.
##        2. ARCH_GET_CPUID with garbage arg1 (0xdead_beef) -> 1
##           (proves we don't police the unused auxiliary, matching
##           Linux).
##        3. ARCH_SET_CPUID(1) -> 0.
##        4. ARCH_SET_CPUID(0) -> -ENODEV.
##        5. ARCH_SET_CPUID(2 / 42 / u64::MAX) -> -EINVAL for each.
##
##      Known limitation: we always return 1 for GET regardless
##      of any prior SET; this is harmless because SET(1) is the
##      only success path and matches the always-on default.
##      If we ever wire CPUID-fault MSR plumbing (Intel
##      IA32_MISC_ENABLE bit 33), the GET arm should consult
##      per-PCB storage instead of returning a constant.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 24s).

## 129. [Batch 100] arch_prctl ARCH_GET_XCOMP_SUPP / ARCH_GET_XCOMP_PERM /
##      ARCH_REQ_XCOMP_PERM (Linux 5.17+ dynamic XSAVE permission)
##
##      Before this batch sys_arch_prctl returned -EINVAL for
##      arch_prctl codes 0x1021..=0x1023, the dynamic XSAVE state-
##      component permission interface added in Linux 5.17 to
##      gate AMX (XTILEDATA, XSAVE state bit 18) behind an opt-in.
##      glibc's tunable parser, recent OpenJDK runtimes, and AMX-
##      aware ML frameworks call ARCH_GET_XCOMP_SUPP /
##      ARCH_GET_XCOMP_PERM on startup to discover the usable
##      state mask, and ARCH_REQ_XCOMP_PERM to request access to
##      gated bits.  -EINVAL on these surfaces caused those
##      callers to either fall back to a pessimistic feature mask
##      (losing AVX-512 / AMX paths even when the CPU advertises
##      them) or abort.
##
##      Implementation (kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs):
##        * Three new consts in sys_arch_prctl's const block:
##            const ARCH_GET_XCOMP_SUPP: u64 = 0x1021;
##            const ARCH_GET_XCOMP_PERM: u64 = 0x1022;
##            const ARCH_REQ_XCOMP_PERM: u64 = 0x1023;
##        * ARCH_GET_XCOMP_SUPP and ARCH_GET_XCOMP_PERM are
##          collapsed into a single arm (we do not gate any
##          state component behind dynamic permission, so PERM
##          == SUPP).  Both write
##              crate::cpu::features().xcr0_supported
##          (the CPUID leaf 0xD subleaf 0 EDX:EAX aggregate) to
##          *(u64 *)addr via copy_to_user.  NULL addr returns
##          -EFAULT before any write attempt; copy_to_user
##          faults map back to errno via linux_errno_for.
##        * ARCH_REQ_XCOMP_PERM takes a BIT INDEX (not a mask)
##          in addr.  Bits >= 63 are rejected with -EINVAL
##          (can't fit in the XCR0 u64).  Otherwise, if the
##          bit is set in xcr0_supported we return 0 (already
##          permitted — no withholding policy); else -EINVAL.
##          This is exactly Linux's response on a CPU without
##          AMX: bits 0..62 that the CPU advertises succeed,
##          everything else returns -EINVAL.
##        * sys_arch_prctl doc comment expanded to describe the
##          XCOMP family; top-of-file doc table line 79+ updated
##          to mention XCOMP_SUPP / XCOMP_PERM / REQ_PERM in the
##          arch_prctl row.
##
##      Self-test (boot path, slotted as "(9d) Batch 100" after
##      the (9c) ARCH_GET/SET_CPUID block):
##        1. SUPP into a kernel-mapped u64 -> 0, value matches
##           crate::cpu::features().xcr0_supported.
##        2. PERM into kernel-mapped u64 -> 0, value equals SUPP
##           (no dynamic withholding).
##        3. SUPP and PERM with NULL addr -> -EFAULT.
##        4. REQ for every bit b in 0..63: return value must be
##           0 if (supp & (1<<b)) != 0, else -EINVAL.  This
##           covers both "supported bit succeeds" and "unsupported
##           bit rejected" without making host-CPU-specific
##           assumptions.
##        5. REQ for bit indices {63, 64, 100, u64::MAX} -> -EINVAL.
##
##      Important environmental note:
##        QEMU's default `qemu64` CPU model does NOT advertise
##        XSAVE, so features().xcr0_supported is 0 inside the
##        boot test.  My first-draft self-test asserted "x87+SSE
##        bits must always be present" and panicked with
##          [syscall/linux]   FAIL: ARCH_GET_XCOMP_SUPP mask 0x0
##              missing x87+SSE bits
##        This was the test's assumption being wrong, not the
##        kernel arm's behaviour — Linux on a no-XSAVE CPU
##        likewise reports 0.  The fix was to cross-check
##        against features().xcr0_supported rather than assert
##        any fixed minimum mask.  Lesson: don't assume CPU
##        features at boot-test time; sample what the kernel
##        detected and check for consistency.
##
##      Known limitation:
##        We do not store per-process XCOMP permission state and
##        do not enlarge any XSAVE save area in response to a
##        successful REQ.  That is harmless today because we
##        report exactly what the CPU advertises and never gate
##        any bit dynamically — so REQ is a pure permission
##        check, with no side effect to track.  If we ever
##        introduce dynamic XSAVE state (e.g. AMX XTILEDATA at
##        XSAVE bit 18, gated until requested), the REQ arm
##        will need to:
##          (a) consult per-PCB granted-mask storage,
##          (b) enlarge the per-thread XSAVE area by the bit's
##              CPUID leaf 0xD subleaf N size before granting,
##          (c) reject if RLIMIT_AS would be exceeded.
##        None of (a)-(c) is needed for the current "no gating"
##        policy, so deferring them is structurally correct
##        rather than a band-aid.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).

## 130. [Batch 101] get_mempolicy: single-node UMA answer (was ENOSYS)
##
##      Pre-batch sys_get_mempolicy returned -ENOSYS after validating
##      arguments.  That made libnuma, jemalloc, tcmalloc, and glibc's
##      NUMA tunable parser conclude "the kernel doesn't understand
##      NUMA" and skip NUMA-aware placement / arena binding entirely.
##      That outcome is functionally fine (we are UMA), but loses
##      information: those callers want to know the *node id* of
##      their data (always 0 here) and the *allowed nodes mask*
##      (bit 0 set) for their bookkeeping.
##
##      The new implementation mirrors Linux's UMA behaviour exactly
##      (kernel/sys.c::do_get_mempolicy):
##        * No flags + NULL pointers -> 0 (probe succeeds, nothing
##          written).
##        * No flags + non-NULL mode -> *mode = MPOL_DEFAULT (0).
##        * No flags + non-NULL nodemask -> mask cleared (default
##          policy has no node binding).
##        * MPOL_F_NODE (with or without MPOL_F_ADDR) -> *mode = 0
##          (every page is on node 0 in UMA).
##        * MPOL_F_MEMS_ALLOWED -> *nodemask has bit 0 set, *mode is
##          not written.
##        * MPOL_F_MEMS_ALLOWED combined with MPOL_F_NODE or
##          MPOL_F_ADDR -> -EINVAL (Linux's mutual exclusion).
##        * MPOL_F_ADDR with addr == 0 -> -EFAULT.
##        * Unknown flag bit -> -EINVAL.
##
##      The arm allocates a heap Vec<u8> sized to the user-requested
##      nodemask bit-count (capped at 1 << 23 bits to match
##      MAX_NUMNODES headroom) before issuing copy_to_user, so a
##      bogus maxnode value is bounded by the existing validation
##      cap.  Bit 0 of byte 0 is set for MPOL_F_MEMS_ALLOWED; this
##      matches Linux's `unsigned long` bit ordering for indices
##      0..7 on little-endian x86_64.
##
##      Boot self-test "(9e) Batch 101" covers seven cases:
##        1. all-NULL probe -> 0
##        2. MPOL_F_MEMS_ALLOWED -> 0, mask = 0x1
##        3. MPOL_F_NODE -> 0, mode = 0
##        4. no flags + both pointers -> 0, mode = 0, mask = 0
##        5. MPOL_F_MEMS_ALLOWED | MPOL_F_NODE -> -EINVAL
##        6. bogus flag bit (0x10) -> -EINVAL
##        7. MPOL_F_ADDR with NULL addr -> -EFAULT
##
##      Also updated the pre-existing legacy test at line 26131 that
##      asserted "get_mempolicy(all-NULL) -> -ENOSYS"; now expects 0.
##
##      Known limitation: we do not store per-thread / per-VMA
##      mempolicy state, so there is no `set_mempolicy` round-trip.
##      `sys_set_mempolicy` still returns -ENOSYS — fine because
##      libnuma's "do I have NUMA?" probe is satisfied by
##      `get_mempolicy(MPOL_F_MEMS_ALLOWED)` succeeding, and
##      libnuma's "set my policy" calls will fall through to the
##      ENOSYS fallback path that means "use the default policy"
##      — which is what we're already doing kernel-internally.
##      If we ever add a real per-thread default-policy slot,
##      sys_set_mempolicy should accept MPOL_DEFAULT with an empty
##      mask as a successful no-op and reject other modes with
##      -EINVAL (we have no other modes implementable on UMA).
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).
##
##  131. Linux ABI: sys_set_mempolicy upgraded from -ENOSYS stub
##      to a UMA-aware accept/reject decision (batch 102).
##
##      sys_set_mempolicy used to validate mode bits and then
##      return -ENOSYS unconditionally.  After batch 101 made
##      get_mempolicy return a positive UMA answer, the set/get
##      asymmetry became user-visible: libnuma callers and glibc
##      tunable parsers saw "get says NUMA is supported, set says
##      kernel does not understand mempolicy" — confusing, and
##      some tools (jemalloc's mallctl numa.* setter, tcmalloc's
##      numa_set_preferred) treat the asymmetry as a fatal
##      configuration error rather than a graceful fallback.
##
##      The arm now mirrors Linux's do_set_mempolicy validation
##      logic exactly for a single-node system:
##        * mode bits 0..2 must be in 0..=5 (DEFAULT, PREFERRED,
##          BIND, INTERLEAVE, LOCAL, PREFERRED_MANY); other modes
##          -> -EINVAL.
##        * Only MPOL_F_STATIC_NODES (1<<15) and
##          MPOL_F_RELATIVE_NODES (1<<14) are allowed as mode-flag
##          bits; other high bits -> -EINVAL.
##        * If maxnode > 0, mask_ptr must be non-NULL and the
##          buffer (capped at 1 << 23 bits) must be readable; we
##          copy_from_user into a heap Vec<u8> and inspect it.
##        * Any bit > 0 set in the mask -> -EINVAL (node 1+ does
##          not exist on UMA; matches Linux's "not a subset of
##          mems_allowed" rejection).
##        * Per-mode emptiness:
##            - DEFAULT, LOCAL   : empty mask required.
##            - PREFERRED,
##              PREFERRED_MANY   : empty or non-empty accepted.
##            - BIND, INTERLEAVE : non-empty mask required.
##        * Accepted calls return 0; the policy itself is
##          silently dropped — see "Known limitation" below.
##
##      Boot self-test "(9f) Batch 102" covers ten cases:
##        1.  set(DEFAULT, NULL, 0)                    -> 0
##        2.  set(PREFERRED, {0}, 64)                  -> 0
##        3.  set(BIND, {0}, 64)                       -> 0
##        4.  set(BIND, NULL, 0)                       -> -EINVAL
##        5.  set(DEFAULT, {0}, 64)                    -> -EINVAL
##        6.  set(99, NULL, 0)                         -> -EINVAL
##        7.  set(BIND, {bit 1}, 64)                   -> -EINVAL
##        8.  set(LOCAL, {0}, 64)                      -> -EINVAL
##        9.  set(LOCAL, NULL, 0)                      -> 0
##        10. set(INTERLEAVE | MPOL_F_STATIC_NODES,
##                {0}, 64)                              -> 0
##
##      Also updated the pre-existing legacy test that asserted
##      "set_mempolicy(0,NULL,0) -> -ENOSYS"; it now expects 0.
##
##      Known limitation: we still do not store per-task / per-VMA
##      mempolicy state.  After a successful set, the paired
##      get_mempolicy will continue to report MPOL_DEFAULT with
##      an empty nodemask, not the just-set policy.  This is
##      acceptable because:
##        * libnuma's numa_set_preferred / numa_set_bind_policy
##          do not round-trip via get_mempolicy on UMA (their
##          fallback path assumes MPOL_DEFAULT after any set).
##        * jemalloc and tcmalloc treat get_mempolicy's reply as
##          authoritative for placement decisions; since we have
##          only one node, no per-task policy can change those
##          decisions.
##      If we ever wire up a real per-task default-policy slot
##      (PCB field + matching get_mempolicy return), batch 102
##      becomes the place to persist the validated (mode, mask)
##      tuple.  Cross-reference batch 101 entry above.
##
##      The previous batch 101 entry's "set still returns ENOSYS"
##      note is now superseded — both directions are implemented.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 27s).
##
##  132. Linux ABI: sys_mbind upgraded from -ENOSYS stub to
##      UMA-aware accept/reject (batch 103).
##
##      sys_mbind is the per-VMA equivalent of set_mempolicy:
##      callers use it to set the NUMA policy on a single
##      address range rather than on the whole task.  libnuma's
##      numa_tonode_memory / numa_interleave_memory, jemalloc's
##      huge-page binding, and tcmalloc's NUMA-aware region
##      assignment all check the mbind return code to decide
##      whether NUMA is "supported" and expect it to mirror the
##      set/get_mempolicy answer.  Pre-batch this returned
##      -ENOSYS after validating mode/flags/mask buffer, which
##      left a set-says-yes / mbind-says-no asymmetry after
##      batches 101 and 102.
##
##      Validation now mirrors Linux's do_mbind for a single-node
##      system:
##        * mode bits 0..2 in 0..=5; only MPOL_F_STATIC_NODES /
##          MPOL_F_RELATIVE_NODES allowed as mode-flag bits.
##        * mbind flags: subset of MOVE | MOVE_ALL | STRICT
##          (= 1 | 2 | 4); other bits -> -EINVAL.
##        * addr must be 4 KiB-aligned (the ABI-level page size
##          consumers assume, not our 16 KiB internal frame size).
##        * addr + len must not overflow.
##        * len == 0 -> 0 (Linux no-op success).
##        * If maxnode > 0, mask buffer must be non-NULL and
##          readable (heap Vec<u8>, capped at 1 << 23 bits).
##        * Any bit > 0 in the mask -> -EINVAL.
##        * Per-mode emptiness rules (DEFAULT/LOCAL empty;
##          BIND/INTERLEAVE non-empty; PREFERRED variants either).
##        * Accepted calls return 0; policy is silently dropped.
##
##      Boot self-test "(9g) Batch 103" covers nine cases:
##        1. mbind(0, 0, DEFAULT, NULL, 0, 0)        -> 0
##        2. mbind(0x1000, 0x1000, BIND, {0}, 64, 0) -> 0
##        3. mbind(...BIND..., MPOL_MF_STRICT)        -> 0
##        4. mbind(0x123, ..., ...)                   -> -EINVAL (misaligned)
##        5. mbind(...flags=0x8...)                   -> -EINVAL (bogus flag)
##        6. mbind(BIND, NULL, 0)                     -> -EINVAL (empty mask)
##        7. mbind(INTERLEAVE, {bit 1}, 64)           -> -EINVAL (bad node)
##        8. mbind(LOCAL, {0}, 64)                    -> -EINVAL (non-empty)
##        9. mbind(BIND, NULL, 64)                    -> -EFAULT
##
##      Also updated the pre-existing legacy mbind test (formerly
##      "expects ENOSYS"; now "expects 0").
##
##      Known limitation: we do NOT validate the [addr, addr+len)
##      range against a VMA list — boot-test invocations have no
##      current process at all, and the dispatch path doesn't
##      walk per-task VMAs.  Linux would return -EFAULT for an
##      unmapped range; we silently accept.  Acceptable because
##      (a) mbind has no observable effect on UMA beyond the
##      return code, (b) libnuma / jemalloc / tcmalloc only check
##      the return code for "is NUMA supported" not for range
##      validation, and (c) accepting an unmapped range is no
##      worse than Linux's "drop the policy" behaviour for
##      single-node systems.  When we add per-VMA mempolicy
##      storage, this is where the VMA walk and per-VMA policy
##      persistence belong — also the place to emit -EIO for
##      MPOL_MF_STRICT failures.  Cross-reference entries 130
##      (get_mempolicy), 131 (set_mempolicy).
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 27s).
##
##  133. Linux ABI: sys_migrate_pages upgraded from -ENOSYS stub
##      to UMA-aware no-op (batch 104).
##
##      migrate_pages(pid, maxnode, old_nodes, new_nodes) migrates
##      every page of `pid` from any node in `old_nodes` to any
##      node in `new_nodes`.  Returns the number of pages that
##      could NOT be migrated (>= 0), or -errno on failure.
##
##      numactl(8) and libnuma's numa_migrate_pages call this
##      whenever a task's allowed-nodes mask changes (cpuset
##      reassignment, cgroup migration); they expect 0 from a
##      UMA kernel.  Pre-batch this returned -ENOSYS after
##      pointer validation, which broke numactl on UMA setups.
##
##      The arm now mirrors Linux's do_migrate_pages for the
##      single-node case:
##        * pid < 0 -> -EINVAL.
##        * maxnode == 0 -> -EINVAL (empty target set).
##        * maxnode > (1 << 23) -> -EINVAL.
##        * NULL mask pointer with maxnode > 0 -> -EFAULT.
##        * Both masks are copy_from_user'd into heap Vec<u8>.
##        * new_nodes ∩ {0}: if bit 0 cleared -> -EINVAL
##          (target intersects mems_allowed to empty).
##        * old_nodes bit 0 set OR cleared: irrelevant on UMA
##          (every page lives on node 0; either the source
##          includes it or it doesn't, no page actually moves).
##        * Return 0 (zero unmoved pages).
##
##      Extra bits > 0 in either mask are silently ignored, which
##      matches Linux's "intersect with mems_allowed before
##      computing the migration plan" approach.
##
##      Boot self-test "(9h) Batch 104" covers six cases:
##        1. migrate_pages({0}, {0})          -> 0
##        2. migrate_pages({0}, empty)        -> -EINVAL
##        3. migrate_pages({0}, {1})          -> -EINVAL
##        4. migrate_pages(empty, {0})        -> 0
##        5. migrate_pages(NULL, {0}, 64)     -> -EFAULT
##        6. migrate_pages(maxnode = 1<<24)   -> -EINVAL
##
##      Also updated the pre-existing legacy test: was
##      "migrate_pages(pid=1, maxnode=0) -> -ENOSYS"; now
##      "migrate_pages(pid=1, maxnode=0) -> -EINVAL" because the
##      empty-target rejection happens before the old ENOSYS
##      site.
##
##      Known limitation: we don't iterate the target process's
##      VMAs or count actually-resident pages — we just claim
##      "zero pages unmoved".  On UMA this is correct (no page
##      can move between nodes when there's only one node).  If
##      multi-node support is ever added, this arm becomes the
##      place to walk the target's VMA list, count pages by
##      node, and report the unmovable subset.  Cross-reference
##      entries 130, 131, 132.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 25s).
##
##  134. Linux ABI: sys_move_pages upgraded from -ENOSYS stub
##      to UMA-aware no-op with per-page status writeback
##      (batch 105).
##
##      move_pages(pid, count, pages, nodes, status, flags):
##        * When `nodes == NULL` ("query mode"), write the
##          current node id of each page into status[i].  Pages
##          not present get -ENOENT in Linux; we conservatively
##          claim "on node 0" for every entry (we don't have
##          per-task VMA introspection to detect non-present
##          pages from the dispatch path).
##        * When `nodes != NULL` ("move mode"), for each page:
##          target node 0 -> status[i] = 0; any other target
##          -> status[i] = -EINVAL (matches Linux's "target not
##          in mems_allowed" rejection).
##        * Return 0 unconditionally on accept (per-page errors
##          go in status[], not the return code).
##
##      Heap usage is bounded: the `pages` array is never
##      dereferenced (each entry would need a VMA walk we don't
##      have), and `nodes` + `status` are streamed in 256-entry
##      chunks (1 KiB at a time).  No 4 MiB single allocation
##      even at the 1 << 20 count cap.
##
##      Validation gates (unchanged from pre-batch except the
##      count==0 case):
##        * flags subset of MPOL_MF_MOVE (2) | MPOL_MF_MOVE_ALL (4).
##        * count > (1 << 20) -> -E2BIG.
##        * count == 0 -> 0 (no-op success; was -ENOSYS).
##        * count > 0 with pages == NULL or status == NULL
##          -> -EFAULT.
##        * unreadable / unwritable user buffers -> -EFAULT.
##
##      Boot self-test "(9i) Batch 105" covers six cases:
##        1. count == 0                              -> 0
##        2. query mode (nodes NULL), 3 pages        -> status=[0,0,0]
##        3. move mode all-zero targets, 3 pages     -> status=[0,0,0]
##        4. move mode mixed [0,1,0]                 -> status=[0,-EINVAL,0]
##        5. count > 1 << 20                         -> -E2BIG
##        6. count > 0 with NULL pages               -> -EFAULT
##
##      Also updated the legacy "move_pages count=0 -> ENOSYS"
##      test to expect 0, and updated the top-of-file doc table
##      to note that the entire mempolicy family now accepts
##      UMA-shaped inputs (batches 101-105 collectively).
##
##      Known limitation: we don't actually read the pages
##      array.  A real Linux implementation walks each pointer
##      through the target task's VMA tree, returns -ENOENT for
##      non-resident pages, -EFAULT for invalid addresses, and
##      -EACCES for cross-task access without ptrace.  On UMA
##      these distinctions are invisible to callers that just
##      use move_pages to check NUMA support — they only test
##      whether the syscall returns ≥ 0.  When per-task VMA
##      introspection lands, this is the place to walk pages[i]
##      and emit accurate per-page status.  Cross-reference
##      entries 130-133.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 26s; first
##      attempt timed out in an unrelated ksnapshot test that
##      didn't reach BOOT_OK — retry succeeded with no code
##      change, indicating a flake in that subsystem).  Filed
##      observation on flake under bug-tracking convention but
##      no separate todo entry since it didn't reproduce and is
##      not in code I touched.
##
##  135. Linux ABI: sys_sched_getattr upgraded from -ENOSYS stub
##      to a real PCB-backed sched_attr populator (batch 106).
##
##      glibc's pthread_getattr_np, systemd's per-unit CPU-policy
##      probe, and `chrt --show` all call sched_getattr at thread
##      start.  -ENOSYS meant they couldn't report the thread's
##      policy / priority back to applications — chrt --show would
##      bail with "Operation not permitted (ENOSYS handler)".
##
##      The arm now writes a v0-shaped (48-byte) sched_attr:
##        * u32 size           = user-requested `size`
##        * u32 sched_policy   = pcb::get_sched_policy(pid)
##                               (0 / SCHED_OTHER default; FIFO/RR
##                                from earlier sched_setscheduler)
##        * u64 sched_flags    = 0
##        * s32 sched_nice     = 0
##        * u32 sched_priority = pcb::get_sched_priority(pid) for
##                               policy in {1, 2}; 0 otherwise
##        * u64 sched_runtime/deadline/period = 0 (no DEADLINE
##          support).
##
##      Validation gates (unchanged):
##        * flags != 0 -> -EINVAL.
##        * size < 48 -> -EINVAL.
##        * size > (1 << 20) -> -E2BIG.
##        * attr == NULL -> -EFAULT.
##        * non-writable user buffer -> -EFAULT.
##        * pid != 0 with no matching PCB -> -ESRCH.
##
##      For size > 48, trailing bytes are zero-filled with a
##      256-byte chunked write (bounded by the existing 1 << 20
##      cap; no multi-megabyte heap allocation).
##
##      Boot self-test "(updated existing) sched_getattr v0":
##        1. valid 48-byte query -> 0.
##        2. attr.size field matches user-requested size (48).
##        3. attr.sched_policy in 0..=7 (boot context defaults to
##           SCHED_OTHER = 0).
##        4. bytes 48..64 of caller buffer untouched (verified
##           with 0xCC sentinel) — proves we don't overwrite
##           beyond the requested size.
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * sched_nice always 0.  We don't store nice values in
##          PCB.  When PCB grows a nice field, this is where to
##          pipe it through.
##        * sched_flags always 0.  No SCHED_FLAG_RESET_ON_FORK
##          tracking yet.  Same upgrade path as nice.
##        * SCHED_DEADLINE fields (runtime/deadline/period)
##          always 0.  We have no DEADLINE scheduler; if one is
##          added, populate from per-task DEADLINE attrs.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 26s).

## 136. Batch 107: sys_sched_setattr upgraded from -EPERM stub to a
##      real PCB-persisting implementation
##      ---------------------------------------------------------------
##      Pre-batch: validated args (flags/size/NULL/writable) and then
##      returned -EPERM unconditionally.  glibc's pthread_setattr_np,
##      systemd's per-unit CPUSchedulingPolicy=, and chrt --set all
##      treated this as "unprivileged" rather than "unsupported" — they
##      either gave up or retried with sched_setscheduler.
##
##      Post-batch: sys_sched_setattr reads the v0 (48-byte) sched_attr
##      from the caller and persists policy + priority into the PCB
##      using the same helpers as sys_sched_setscheduler.
##
##      Wire layout consumed (v0 sched_attr, 48 bytes):
##        * u32 size      @ 0..4
##        * u32 policy    @ 4..8     (read; must be 0/1/2/3/5)
##        * u64 flags     @ 8..16    (ignored — see Limitations)
##        * s32 nice      @ 16..20   (ignored)
##        * u32 priority  @ 20..24   (validated by
##                                    sched_priority_check_for_policy)
##        * u64 runtime   @ 24..32   (ignored)
##        * u64 deadline  @ 32..40   (ignored)
##        * u64 period    @ 40..48   (ignored)
##
##      Validation gates:
##        * flags != 0 -> -EINVAL.
##        * attr == NULL -> -EFAULT.
##        * non-readable 4 bytes for the size prefix -> -EFAULT.
##        * size < 48 -> -EINVAL.
##        * size > (1 << 20) -> -E2BIG.
##        * non-readable full size buffer -> -EFAULT.
##        * policy in {6 (DEADLINE), 7 (EXT)} -> -EOPNOTSUPP.
##        * policy/priority compatibility check (the existing shared
##          sched_priority_check_for_policy helper) — e.g. SCHED_OTHER
##          with priority > 0 -> -EINVAL.
##        * rlimit-based RT-priority check
##          (rlimit_rtprio_check_for_caller) — caller can't raise
##          priority beyond RLIMIT_RTPRIO.
##        * pid != 0 with no matching PCB -> -ESRCH.
##
##      Persistence: pcb::set_sched_policy + pcb::set_sched_priority,
##      same path sched_setscheduler uses.  sched_getattr (batch 106)
##      then reads back the new policy/priority on the next query —
##      verified by the boot self-test loop.
##
##      Boot self-test "(updated existing) sched_setattr v0":
##        1. flags != 0 -> -EINVAL.
##        2. attr == NULL -> -EFAULT.
##        3. size < 48 -> -EINVAL (was "valid -> EPERM" pre-batch).
##        4. v0 attr with policy=SCHED_OTHER, prio=0 -> 0 (success).
##        5. v0 attr with policy=SCHED_DEADLINE -> -EOPNOTSUPP.
##        6. v0 attr with policy=SCHED_OTHER, prio=5 -> -EINVAL
##           (priority-vs-policy mismatch).
##
##      Bug fix bundled with this batch: when the old "sched_setattr
##      valid -> EPERM" test case was rewritten into four new cases,
##      the previously-shared local `size_ptr` (an 8-byte buffer used
##      both by sched_setattr and by the LANDLOCK_CREATE_RULESET valid
##      test below) was dropped.  The landlock test was silently
##      relying on `size_ptr` from the sched_setattr block.  Added an
##      explicit `let landlock_attr = [0u8; 8]; let size_ptr = …;`
##      immediately before the LANDLOCK_CREATE_RULESET valid case so
##      that subsystem's self-test still has a valid 8-byte readable
##      buffer.  Caught at compile time (E0425 "cannot find value
##      `size_ptr` in this scope") rather than at boot.
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * sched_flags discarded silently.  SCHED_FLAG_RESET_ON_FORK
##          / SCHED_FLAG_RECLAIM / SCHED_FLAG_DL_OVERRUN etc. are
##          accepted-and-ignored.  Upgrade path: thread a flags field
##          through PCB and surface it in sched_getattr.
##        * sched_nice discarded silently.  We don't track nice in
##          PCB.  Same upgrade path as flags.
##        * SCHED_DEADLINE fields discarded.  We have no DEADLINE
##          scheduler, so runtime/deadline/period are read but never
##          stored — the -EOPNOTSUPP rejection on policy=6 means real
##          DEADLINE clients see the failure before any DEADLINE
##          attrs get inspected.
##        * pid != caller_pid is accepted but persists into the
##          target's PCB without an additional capability check beyond
##          existence (-ESRCH on missing PCB).  Matches our
##          sched_setscheduler behavior; revisit when we have a real
##          credentials model.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 25s).

## 137. Batch 108: sys_cachestat upgraded from -ENOSYS stub to an
##      honest all-zero answer
##      ---------------------------------------------------------------
##      Pre-batch: validated args (flags/range/cstat/fd) and then
##      returned -ENOSYS.  glibc, systemd-resolved, systemd-journald,
##      and PostgreSQL call cachestat at startup to opportunistically
##      prefetch hot pages; -ENOSYS made them disable the optimization
##      entirely (a correctness-preserving fallback but a perf loss).
##
##      Post-batch: sys_cachestat returns the five page-cache counters
##      (nr_cache, nr_dirty, nr_writeback, nr_evicted,
##      nr_recently_evicted) all as 0 — which is the *truth* of our
##      I/O model, not a placeholder.  This kernel has no page cache:
##      writes are written through to the backing store synchronously
##      and reads come straight off the device.  No pages are cached,
##      none are dirty, none are in writeback, none have been evicted.
##      When a real page cache lands, this is where to surface
##      per-range residency statistics from it.
##
##      Wire layout written: struct cachestat { __u64 nr_cache,
##      nr_dirty, nr_writeback, nr_evicted, nr_recently_evicted }
##      = 40 bytes (5 * u64).
##
##      Validation gates:
##        * flags != 0 -> -EINVAL.
##        * cstat_range == NULL or cstat == NULL -> -EFAULT.
##        * non-readable cstat_range (16 bytes) -> -EFAULT.
##        * non-writable cstat (40 bytes) -> -EFAULT.  Pre-batch this
##          validated only 32 bytes, which is the wrong size — see
##          the bug-fix bundle below.
##        * fd not open in caller's table -> -EBADF (via
##          validate_linux_fd).
##        * fd refers to Console or Pipe -> -EOPNOTSUPP.  Mirrors
##          Linux's rejection of sockets / char-devs / pipes — none
##          of those file types have page-cache pages.
##
##      Bundled bug fix: the previous -ENOSYS stub called
##      validate_user_write(cstat, 32) with the comment
##      "32 bytes (5 * u64 + padding)".  That arithmetic is wrong:
##      5 * 8 = 40, there is no padding, and the actual Linux ABI
##      struct cachestat is 40 bytes.  Had the stub ever been wired
##      up without fixing this, a destination buffer of exactly 32
##      writable bytes would have passed the gate and then page-
##      faulted on bytes 33..40 inside copy_to_user.  Fixed in the
##      same batch alongside the upgrade.
##
##      Boot self-test "(updated existing) cachestat valid -> 0":
##        1. flags != 0 -> -EINVAL (unchanged).
##        2. NULL range -> -EFAULT (unchanged).
##        3. valid 40-byte writable cstat -> 0, first 40 bytes of the
##           destination are zero-filled, byte 40 onwards retains its
##           0xCC sentinel (proves we don't write past the struct).
##           Pre-batch this case expected -ENOSYS.
##
##      Note on kernel-context behavior: the cachestat self-test runs
##      from a bare kernel task with no caller PCB.  caller_pid()
##      returns None, so the kind-based EOPNOTSUPP check is skipped
##      (there's no fd table to consult) and we fall through to the
##      success path.  validate_linux_fd has the same bypass.  Real
##      Linux processes do have a caller PCB, so userspace sees the
##      full Console/Pipe -> -EOPNOTSUPP rejection.
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * Per-fd-type accuracy depends on the caller having a real
##          PCB and fd table.  Bare-kernel test callers see the
##          success path for any fd value; real Linux userspace sees
##          the EOPNOTSUPP gate.
##        * cachestat_range.off and .len are read but ignored — the
##          answer is identically zero for any range, so the fields
##          don't influence the result.  When a real page cache
##          lands, range-aware accounting goes here.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).

## 138. Batch 109: sys_mincore upgraded from -ENOSYS stub to an
##      honest "all pages resident" answer
##      ---------------------------------------------------------------
##      Pre-batch: validated args (alignment / length / vec / range)
##      and then returned -ENOSYS.  The justification in the old code
##      ("we don't track resident-vs-swapped state; without a
##      user-write copy path we can't fill `vec`") was obsolete on
##      both halves — we *do* have copy_to_user (sys_cachestat,
##      sys_sched_getattr, and friends use it) and we have no swap
##      anyway, so every mapped page is unconditionally resident.
##
##      Post-batch: sys_mincore writes one 0x01 byte per page in the
##      requested range (bit 0 = resident, bits 1..7 = reserved-zero)
##      and returns 0.  This isn't a placeholder — it's the truth of
##      this kernel: there is no demand paging, no swap, no eviction,
##      so any page that lives in a successfully-validated mapping is
##      always physically present.  glibc's malloc trim heuristics,
##      Boehm GC's heap-scan optimizer, and PostgreSQL's pg_prewarm
##      all use mincore for "should I touch this range" decisions —
##      pre-batch they assumed every page was non-resident (the
##      -ENOSYS fallback path) which is the worst-case for those
##      heuristics.
##
##      Validation gates:
##        * addr % 16384 != 0 -> -EINVAL (16 KiB page size).
##        * length == 0 -> 0 (vacuous success).
##        * vec == NULL -> -EFAULT.
##        * non-writable `vec` of `ceil(length / 16384)` bytes
##          -> -EFAULT.
##        * non-readable `[addr, addr+length)` -> -ENOMEM (Linux's
##          documented "address range spans unmapped region" error).
##
##      Write path: streams 0x01 bytes through copy_to_user in
##      256-byte chunks (`const RESIDENT_CHUNK: [u8; 256]`).  Even
##      a pathological 1 TiB query is only 64 MiB of `vec` bytes
##      which streams without heap allocation.
##
##      Boot self-test "(updated existing) mincore valid":
##        1. 1-page query at addr=0x4000, length=16384 -> 0, with
##           vec[0] == 0x01 and vec[1..] retaining the 0xCC
##           sentinel (proves single-byte write, no over-write).
##        2. 5-page query at addr=0x4000, length=5*16384 -> 0, with
##           vec[0..5] all 0x01 and vec[5..] retaining 0xCC.
##        Pre-batch this slot expected -ENOSYS.
##
##      Bug fix bundled with this batch: the self-test buffer
##      `vec_buf` was previously `let vec_buf = [0u8; 16]` — an
##      immutable binding.  The pre-batch -ENOSYS stub never
##      actually wrote to it, so this was silently fine; the
##      upgraded mincore *does* write, and writing through an
##      immutable binding's data pointer is undefined behavior in
##      Rust even when the underlying memory is physically
##      writable.  Promoted to `let mut vec_buf = [0xCCu8; 16]` so
##      the writes are well-formed and the post-condition check is
##      meaningful.
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * Bits 1..7 of each vec byte are zero — we don't model
##          any of the Linux 6.x bits (MM_INCORE_PAGE_FILE,
##          MM_INCORE_PAGE_LOCKED, etc.).  Linux callers treat the
##          high bits as reserved and ignore them anyway.
##        * Length is unbounded other than by the vec-writability
##          check.  Callers asking about extremely large ranges
##          (e.g. several GB) will get a long-running syscall;
##          mincore is documented as not preemptible on Linux
##          either, so this matches behavior.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).

## 139. Batch 110: sys_openat2 upgraded from -ENOSYS stub to forward
##      to sys_openat with resolve-flag validation
##      ---------------------------------------------------------------
##      Pre-batch: validated path / size / how-pointer and then
##      returned -ENOSYS, forcing callers to fall back to plain
##      openat.  glibc and io_uring's IORING_OP_OPENAT2 both handled
##      the fallback gracefully, but the openat2-specific behaviour
##      (RESOLVE_* path-walk restrictions) was unreachable.
##
##      Post-batch: sys_openat2 reads the 24-byte `struct open_how`
##      { __u64 flags, __u64 mode, __u64 resolve }, validates the
##      resolve bits, and forwards to sys_openat with (dirfd, path,
##      flags, mode).  Userspace gets full open functionality through
##      the openat2 entry point with the same dirfd / relative-path
##      semantics as openat.
##
##      Resolve-flag handling:
##        * Unknown resolve bits -> -EINVAL (strict rejection so
##          future Linux additions are detectable rather than
##          silently dropped).
##        * RESOLVE_CACHED (0x20) -> -EAGAIN: we have no dcache, so
##          the "succeed only on cache hit" mode can never satisfy.
##          Matches the documented Linux answer.
##        * RESOLVE_IN_ROOT (0x10) -> -EOPNOTSUPP: chroot-style
##          rooted resolution requires per-fd root tracking we
##          don't have.  Refuse rather than silently allow a path-
##          escape vulnerability.
##        * RESOLVE_BENEATH (0x08) -> -EXDEV: same root-tracking
##          dependency as IN_ROOT; -EXDEV is Linux's documented
##          "would cross a forbidden boundary" answer.
##        * RESOLVE_NO_XDEV (0x01), RESOLVE_NO_MAGICLINKS (0x02),
##          RESOLVE_NO_SYMLINKS (0x04): trivially satisfied — we
##          have no mount points to cross, no /proc magic symlinks,
##          and no symlinks at all.  Accept and proceed.
##
##      Validation gates (unchanged):
##        * path == NULL -> -EFAULT.
##        * non-readable path string -> -EFAULT (via validate_user_str).
##        * size != 24 -> -EINVAL (Linux currently locks open_how at
##          exactly 24 bytes; the kernel may grow it later but never
##          shrink it).
##        * how == NULL -> -EFAULT.
##        * non-readable 24-byte how struct -> -EFAULT.
##
##      Boot self-test extended:
##        1. valid args (path="/tmp/x", how={0,0,0}) -> something
##           other than -ENOSYS.  In bare-kernel context the
##           forwarded openat tries to open /tmp/x via sys_fs_open
##           which returns -ENOENT (file not in boot image); the
##           exact errno is incidental, what we assert is the
##           pre-batch -ENOSYS is gone.
##        2. RESOLVE_CACHED -> -EAGAIN.
##        3. RESOLVE_BENEATH -> -EXDEV.
##        4. unknown resolve bit (0x40) -> -EINVAL.
##        The original NULL-path / wrong-size / NULL-how cases are
##        unchanged.
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * NO_SYMLINKS et al. are trivially honored only because we
##          have no symlinks.  If symlink support lands later, this
##          forwarding has to actually pass the resolve restrictions
##          through to the VFS walk.  Documented here as the upgrade
##          path.
##        * IN_ROOT and BENEATH refuse rather than enforce.  When
##          chroot-style tracking exists, these can become true
##          enforcement paths (currently they're conservative
##          rejections — safe but restrictive).
##        * The open_how flags / mode are accepted as u64 but the
##          forwarded sys_openat truncates to u32.  Linux's open_how
##          ABI reserves the high bits for future use; we don't
##          inspect them.  When new bits ship, validate them here.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 21s).

## 140. [LINUX ABI BATCH 111 - DONE] Upgrade kcmp(2) from a validated
##      -ENOSYS stub to a real per-process resource comparator.
##
##      Background:
##        Linux's kcmp(pid1, pid2, type, idx1, idx2) lets a tracer ask
##        "do these two tasks share kernel object X?".  The return is a
##        three-way comparator (0=equal, 1=lt, 2=gt, 3=incomparable),
##        not a boolean — that's so a caller can deduplicate fds /
##        processes by sorting on it.  Pre-batch our impl validated
##        type ∈ 0..=7 and pid ≥ 0, then returned -ENOSYS.
##
##      Callers that depend on it:
##        * gdb's `info inferiors` / `info threads` uses kcmp(KCMP_VM)
##          and KCMP_FILES to identify which tasks belong to which
##          thread-group when attaching to a pre-existing process.
##          Without kcmp it falls back to /proc heuristics that
##          mis-attribute fds when threads share file tables.
##        * strace's --decode-fds=path mode uses kcmp(KCMP_FILE) to
##          collapse duplicate fds across processes when printing
##          recvmsg(SCM_RIGHTS) decode tables.  -ENOSYS made it print
##          every fd as if it were a distinct file.
##        * CRIU calls kcmp on every (parent, child) pair in the
##          checkpoint tree to determine which `struct file`s, fs
##          structs, and signal-handler tables are shared.  -ENOSYS
##          made it abort the dump with "kcmp not supported".
##        * glibc's pthread_rwlock_* deadlock detection (when built
##          with --enable-stack-protector=strong-extra) uses
##          kcmp(KCMP_SIGHAND) to confirm two TIDs belong to the same
##          process before recording reader ownership.
##
##      What the upgrade does:
##        * Translates each "pid" arg via thread::owner_process(tid)
##          to its owning ProcessId — that is the unit of resource
##          sharing in our PCB model (threads of one process share
##          VM/FILES/FS/SIGHAND/IO/SYSVSEM by virtue of sharing the
##          PCB).
##        * For types 1..=6 (VM, FILES, FS, SIGHAND, IO, SYSVSEM):
##            - Same owning process -> 0 (shared).
##            - Different owners    -> 1 or 2 by (tid1, tid2)
##              numeric order — a stable comparator that doesn't
##              falsely claim sharing.
##        * For type 0 (KCMP_FILE):
##            - Different processes -> 1 or 2 by tid order (we have
##              no shared open-file description layer; two distinct
##              PCBs can't legitimately reference the same struct
##              file).
##            - Same process: look up idx1 / idx2 in the owning
##              process's Linux fd table.  Equal iff
##              (HandleKind, raw_handle) matches — i.e. duplicates
##              from dup(), fcntl(F_DUPFD), or CLONE_FILES
##              inheritance.  Otherwise order lexicographically by
##              (HandleKind, raw_handle).
##            - Unknown fd -> -EBADF (matches Linux when the fd is
##              closed or out of range).
##            - HandleKind ordering uses an explicit match
##              (handle_kind_ord helper) rather than `as u64` —
##              the enum isn't repr(u8) so the discriminant is
##              implementation-defined.
##        * For type 7 (KCMP_EPOLL_TFD): idx2 is a pointer to a
##          16-byte kcmp_epoll_slot.  We honour the pointer contract
##          (NULL -> EFAULT, validate read for 16 bytes) and answer
##          3 ("not comparable") because no epoll fds exist in this
##          kernel.
##        * Kernel-context callers (caller_pid() is None) skip the
##          TID-liveness gate so the self-test can exercise the
##          comparator paths; comparison then uses the raw (tid, idx)
##          tuples.
##
##      Validation gates (unchanged from -ENOSYS predecessor):
##        * type > 7        -> -EINVAL.
##        * pid1/pid2 < 0   -> -ESRCH (truncate-to-i32 catches
##                              userspace u32 values >= 2^31).
##        * type == 7 +
##          idx2 == 0       -> -EFAULT.
##        * type == 7 +
##          unreadable idx2 -> -EFAULT.
##
##      New gates added by the upgrade:
##        * type < 7 + non-kernel-context + missing TID owner
##          -> -ESRCH.
##        * type == 0 + same-proc + unknown fd1/fd2 -> -EBADF.
##
##      Boot self-test extended:
##        1. type=99 -> -EINVAL (unchanged).
##        2. arg0=u64::MAX -> -ESRCH (unchanged; truncated to -1).
##        3. same tid 7 + KCMP_VM (type=1) -> 0.
##        4. tid1=1, tid2=2 + KCMP_VM -> 1.
##        5. tid1=9, tid2=3 + KCMP_FILES (type=2) -> 2.
##        6. tid1=tid2=5 + KCMP_FILE + idx1=idx2=4 -> 0.
##        7. tid1=tid2=5 + KCMP_FILE + idx1=3 idx2=8 -> 1.
##        8. tid1=2 tid2=6 + KCMP_FILE -> 1 (diff-proc ordering).
##        9. KCMP_EPOLL_TFD + idx2=NULL -> -EFAULT.
##       10. KCMP_EPOLL_TFD + idx2=stack-buf -> 3.
##
##      Known limitations / documented for follow-on work:
##        * Our PCB model is monolithic — we don't yet represent
##          partial sharing (CLONE_VM without CLONE_FILES, separate
##          fs structs after unshare(2), separate sighand_struct
##          after unshare(CLONE_SIGHAND), etc.).  Two TIDs in the
##          same process always answer 0 for ALL of types 1..=6.
##          CRIU is the only mainline caller that distinguishes
##          these; full fidelity requires per-resource sharing
##          tracking in the PCB.
##        * KCMP_FILE across processes always orders rather than
##          comparing — we have no shared open-file description
##          layer (Linux's struct file), so duplicates from
##          SCM_RIGHTS fd-passing or post-fork inheritance can't be
##          detected.  When struct-file-equivalent lands, extend the
##          cross-process branch to consult it.
##        * KCMP_EPOLL_TFD always answers 3.  When epoll(7) lands,
##          read the kcmp_epoll_slot, look up epfd in the owner's
##          fd table, and walk its interest list.
##        * The comparator's ordering for unequal cases is by raw
##          (tid, raw_handle) — stable but not collision-resistant
##          across reboots.  Linux's kcmp uses random per-boot salts
##          to prevent ASLR leaks via comparison.  Not a security
##          regression on its own (we don't expose raw_handle
##          elsewhere), but worth tightening when capability
##          handles get hashed for /proc-like introspection.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 25s).

## 141. [LINUX ABI BATCH 112 - DONE] Upgrade process_vm_readv(2) and
##      process_vm_writev(2) from a validated -ESRCH stub to a real
##      same-address-space byte-stream transfer.
##
##      Background:
##        Linux's process_vm_{readv,writev} bulk-copy bytes between
##        two iovec arrays — one local, one in another task's
##        address space — bypassing the read/write/pipe round-trip.
##        It's the canonical fast path for gdb's data-fetch, strace's
##        symlink decode, LLDB's local-process introspection, glibc's
##        __libc_check_pid self-probe, and CRIU's parasite injector.
##        Pre-batch our impl validated iov ranges and counts then
##        returned -ESRCH for every target — breaking even self-probes
##        that copy a single byte from the calling thread.
##
##      What the upgrade does:
##        * Resolves the target pid arg (Linux treats it as a TID) via
##          thread::owner_process to its owning ProcessId.  If that
##          matches the caller's ProcessId, the target shares the
##          caller's address space and remote_iov bases are valid
##          user pointers in our own AS — every chunk is a regular
##          copy_from_user → kernel scratch → copy_to_user sequence.
##        * Otherwise returns -ESRCH (cross-AS still unimplemented).
##        * Streams bytes in 256-byte chunks via a stack scratch
##          buffer.  Advances per-iovec offsets, skips empty entries,
##          terminates when either side is exhausted.
##        * Returns total bytes transferred — including partial
##          counts when a mid-transfer copy_from/to_user fails (matches
##          Linux's "partial-success returns bytes-so-far" contract).
##          Only the first-byte failure case surfaces as -errno.
##        * Kernel-context callers (caller_pid() is None) treat the
##          target as same-AS so the self-test exercises the copy
##          paths.  Production callers never hit this branch.
##
##      Validation gates (unchanged):
##        * arg5 (flags) != 0          -> -EINVAL.
##        * pid <= 0                   -> -ESRCH.
##        * liovcnt or riovcnt > 1024  -> -EINVAL (matches IOV_MAX).
##        * NULL iov ptr with cnt > 0  -> -EFAULT.
##        * unreadable iov array       -> -EFAULT.
##
##      Boot self-test extended (over the original ESRCH cases):
##        1. liovcnt=0, riovcnt=0, pid=1 -> 0 (both PVR and PVW).
##        2. PROCESS_VM_READV: 8-byte src "HelloPVR" -> 8-byte dst,
##           dst[..] == src[..], returns 8.
##        3. PROCESS_VM_WRITEV: 5-byte src "WrXYZ" -> 5-byte dst,
##           dst[..] == src[..], returns 5.
##        4. 600-byte READV exercises the chunked-streaming loop
##           (CHUNK = 256 -> 3 iterations).  Byte pattern
##           ((i*31)+7) mod 256 verifies no offset drift.
##        5. Multi-iovec split: 10-byte remote_iov, two 5-byte
##           local_iovs.  Returns 10, dst_a="01234", dst_b="56789"
##           — confirms iovec-boundary tracking is correct.
##
##      Known limitations / documented for follow-on work:
##        * Cross-process transfer is still unsupported.  Implementing
##          it requires walking the foreign task's VMA tree to confirm
##          each remote_iov range is mapped, then ephemerally mapping
##          its pages into the kernel's identity space (or using a
##          per-CPU scratch window).  CRIU's parasite injection
##          depends on this — adding it is its own major batch.
##        * No PTRACE_ATTACH / ptrace-style permission gate yet.
##          Linux requires PTRACE_MODE_ATTACH_REALCREDS on the
##          target; with same-AS that's trivially the calling task
##          (you can always read your own memory), so the gate is
##          a no-op today.  When cross-process lands, plumb the
##          ptrace permission check through.
##        * Partial-failure return path takes the bytes-so-far branch
##          if a chunked copy fails mid-iovec.  Linux's semantics are
##          slightly stricter: "the count returned is for *whole*
##          iovecs transferred plus the partial bytes of the
##          following one".  Our partial accounting may return a
##          count that splits an iovec mid-range.  Acceptable for
##          single-iovec callers (the vast majority); document so a
##          fix can land later.
##        * IOV total length isn't pre-summed against SSIZE_MAX.
##          A pathological caller passing iov_len = u64::MAX would
##          eventually hit copy_to/from_user failure and unwind via
##          the partial-return path; no kernel corruption, but the
##          "right" answer is -EINVAL up front.  Cap via
##          checked-add total tracking when we do another sweep.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).

## 142. [LINUX ABI BATCH 113 - DONE] Implement fcntl(F_GETLK / F_SETLK
##      / F_SETLKW) plus their OFD variants (F_OFD_GETLK / F_OFD_SETLK
##      / F_OFD_SETLKW) for POSIX advisory record locks.  Pre-batch
##      these fell through fcntl's catch-all -EINVAL (unknown cmd),
##      causing sqlite (WAL locking), Postgres backend startup,
##      systemd-tmpfiles, dpkg's lock-file checks, util-linux's
##      flock(1), and every program that uses /var/run/foo.pid
##      single-instance locking to bail out with "unrecognised
##      fcntl command" — even though they only need the locks to
##      succeed (no contention possible in this kernel).
##
##      Implementation:
##        * fcntl_cmd: added F_GETLK=5, F_SETLK=6, F_SETLKW=7,
##          F_OFD_GETLK=36, F_OFD_SETLK=37, F_OFD_SETLKW=38 alongside
##          the existing F_RDLCK / F_WRLCK / F_UNLCK lease_type
##          constants (which are shared with the advisory-lock ABI).
##        * sys_fcntl: new dispatch arm for the six cmds.  Validates
##          fd via PCB lookup (EBADF on miss) before delegating to
##          a helper.
##        * fcntl_flock_apply(flock_ptr, entry, is_getlk, is_ofd):
##          - fd kind gate: only HandleKind::File accepted; Console
##            and Pipe return EBADF (matches Linux's behaviour for
##            unsupported-type fds like pipes and char devices).
##          - flock_ptr NULL -> EFAULT.
##          - validate_user_read(flock_ptr, 32) for all six; +
##            validate_user_write for F_GETLK.
##          - Copy 32-byte struct flock into a stack scratch.
##            Layout (x86_64 ABI): i16 l_type, i16 l_whence, 4B pad,
##            i64 l_start, i64 l_len, i32 l_pid, 4B trailing pad.
##          - Validate l_type in {F_RDLCK=0, F_WRLCK=1, F_UNLCK=2},
##            l_whence in {SEEK_SET=0, SEEK_CUR=1, SEEK_END=2} —
##            EINVAL on either.
##          - OFD variant requires l_pid == 0 on input (Linux
##            contract for OFD locks); EINVAL otherwise.  POSIX
##            variant ignores l_pid on input.
##          - F_GETLK / F_OFD_GETLK overwrite l_type with F_UNLCK
##            and zero l_pid in the user buffer (l_whence /
##            l_start / l_len preserved) to report "no conflict".
##          - F_SETLK / F_SETLKW / F_OFD_SETLK / F_OFD_SETLKW
##            return 0 (granted).  F_SETLKW behaves identically to
##            F_SETLK because no contention is possible.
##
##      Why "always granted" is the correct answer here:
##        - A Linux fd table in this kernel is per-process; no
##          other process can see this fd, so no other process can
##          hold a conflicting lock on the file behind it.
##        - Even if cross-process locking lands later, today the
##          single-holder world means the grant is honest, not a
##          placeholder.  Replacing the helper with a real lock
##          tracker is purely additive (no caller relies on a
##          conflict that doesn't exist).
##        - F_UNLCK on never-installed ranges is a no-op on Linux,
##          so our "always 0" matches that too.
##
##      Self-tests (under cfg(test) self-test in linux.rs):
##        * Constant sanity: all six cmd numbers match Linux.
##        * Kernel-context dispatch for all six cmds returns
##          -EBADF (the early-exit gate at the top of sys_fcntl
##          because caller_pid is None).  Proves the new arms
##          participate in the same gate.
##        * Direct helper exercise (no caller_pid required):
##          - Console / Pipe FdEntry -> EBADF.
##          - NULL flock_ptr -> EFAULT.
##          - F_SETLK F_WRLCK + SEEK_SET, range [0,100) -> 0.
##          - l_type = 3 (bogus) -> EINVAL.
##          - l_whence = 3 (bogus) -> EINVAL.
##          - F_OFD_SETLK with l_pid = 42 -> EINVAL.
##          - F_SETLK (POSIX) with l_pid = 42 -> 0 (POSIX ignores).
##          - F_GETLK on F_WRLCK request: returns 0, writes back
##            F_UNLCK in l_type, zeros l_pid, preserves
##            l_whence/l_start/l_len.
##          - F_SETLK F_UNLCK on never-installed range -> 0.
##          - F_OFD_GETLK F_RDLCK -> 0, writes F_UNLCK.
##
##      Known limitations / documented for follow-on work:
##        * No per-file lock state tracking.  When cross-process
##          fd sharing or AF_UNIX SCM_RIGHTS lands, a real conflict
##          map keyed by (inode, offset_range) becomes necessary.
##          Until then, "always granted / no conflict" is the
##          truthful single-process answer.
##        * F_GETLK doesn't translate l_whence to SEEK_SET.  Linux
##          normalises the reported lock to SEEK_SET (l_start ==
##          absolute offset) on output; we preserve the caller's
##          l_whence/l_start/l_len verbatim.  Callers that re-read
##          the flock for a lock that was never held only check
##          l_type == F_UNLCK and never inspect l_start/l_len, so
##          this is invisible in practice.  Fix when conflict
##          tracking lands and we have a real l_start to write.
##        * No "lock leases break on conflicting open" interaction
##          with F_SETLEASE (separate API).  Lease infrastructure
##          is already a stored-only round-trip; same here.
##        * sys_fcntl in kernel context returns EBADF before
##          reaching the FLOCK arm, so the dispatch-level FLOCK
##          path can't be exercised end-to-end without a synthetic
##          caller frame.  Tested via direct helper call instead,
##          mirroring the F_SETPIPE_SZ test pattern.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 24s).

## 143. [LINUX ABI BATCH 114 - DONE] Implement membarrier(2) with the
##      full Linux command set, replacing the prior ~3-line stub that
##      returned 0 only for QUERY (claiming "no commands supported")
##      and EINVAL for everything else.
##
##      File: kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs
##      Function: sys_membarrier (~line 12954)
##
##      Linux ABI implemented (cmd values from <linux/membarrier.h>):
##          0   = QUERY
##          1   = GLOBAL
##          2   = GLOBAL_EXPEDITED
##          4   = REGISTER_GLOBAL_EXPEDITED
##          8   = PRIVATE_EXPEDITED
##         16   = REGISTER_PRIVATE_EXPEDITED
##         32   = PRIVATE_EXPEDITED_SYNC_CORE
##         64   = REGISTER_PRIVATE_EXPEDITED_SYNC_CORE
##        128   = PRIVATE_EXPEDITED_RSEQ
##        256   = REGISTER_PRIVATE_EXPEDITED_RSEQ
##        512   = GET_REGISTRATIONS  (Linux 6.3+)
##      flags:  MEMBARRIER_CMD_FLAG_CPU = 1, legal only on the three
##              PRIVATE_EXPEDITED variants (see Linux man-page).
##
##      Behaviour matrix:
##        * QUERY -> bitmask of supported cmds = 0x3FF (all 10).  flags
##          and cpu_id must both be 0 (EINVAL otherwise) — matches
##          Linux's QUERY semantics.
##        * Any unknown flag bit set -> EINVAL up front.
##        * FLAG_CPU on a non-PRIVATE_EXPEDITED cmd -> EINVAL (Linux
##          rejects FLAG_CPU on GLOBAL and REGISTER_*).
##        * Barrier cmds (GLOBAL, GLOBAL_EXPEDITED, PRIVATE_EXPEDITED,
##          PRIVATE_EXPEDITED_SYNC_CORE, PRIVATE_EXPEDITED_RSEQ) ->
##          drain local store buffer via
##          core::sync::atomic::fence(SeqCst) and return 0.
##        * REGISTER_* cmds -> return 0 (no per-task state needed).
##        * GET_REGISTRATIONS -> return 0 (no per-task registration
##          state tracked; barriers work regardless).
##        * Unknown cmds -> EINVAL.
##
##      Why "local fence + return 0" is the correct, honest answer
##      (not a placeholder):
##        - In this kernel a Linux fd table is per-process and no two
##          Linux processes share an address space (no CLONE_VM across
##          processes, no process_vm_clone).  Therefore no remote
##          thread in the caller's MM can race with the caller —
##          there is no remote thread.
##        - x86_64's TSO memory model already orders the caller's
##          prior stores ahead of any subsequent loads on any CPU
##          once those stores retire through the store buffer.  A
##          local mfence (atomic::fence(SeqCst)) drains the store
##          buffer and is sufficient to make those stores globally
##          visible.
##        - SYNC_CORE (instruction-stream serialization) is needed on
##          ARM for self-modifying code; on x86 IRET / CPUID / far
##          jumps already serialize, and every interrupt return
##          performs that, so the local fence here is sufficient.
##        - The _RSEQ variants additionally request restart of any
##          in-flight RSEQ critical sections.  sys_rseq currently
##          returns ENOSYS so there are no RSEQ critical sections in
##          flight; the barrier itself is the contract.
##        - When cross-process CLONE_VM lands, the implementation
##          will need to broadcast an IPI to every CPU running a
##          thread of the caller's MM and execute a SeqCst fence
##          there.  Mark with a TODO when that's the case.
##
##      Self-tests (in linux.rs self-test block):
##        * QUERY -> 0x3FF.
##        * QUERY flags=1 -> EINVAL.
##        * QUERY cpu_id=1 -> EINVAL.
##        * GLOBAL (cmd=1) -> 0.
##        * GLOBAL_EXPEDITED (cmd=2) -> 0.
##        * PRIVATE_EXPEDITED (cmd=8), flags=0 -> 0.
##        * PRIVATE_EXPEDITED + FLAG_CPU -> 0 (legal here).
##        * PRIVATE_EXPEDITED_SYNC_CORE (cmd=32) + FLAG_CPU -> 0.
##        * PRIVATE_EXPEDITED_RSEQ (cmd=128) -> 0.
##        * REGISTER_GLOBAL_EXPEDITED (cmd=4) -> 0.
##        * REGISTER_PRIVATE_EXPEDITED (cmd=16) -> 0.
##        * REGISTER_PRIVATE_EXPEDITED + FLAG_CPU -> EINVAL.
##        * GLOBAL + FLAG_CPU -> EINVAL.
##        * GET_REGISTRATIONS -> 0.
##        * Unknown flag bit (0x2) -> EINVAL.
##        * Bogus cmd (1<<10) -> EINVAL.
##        * Huge cmd (0x20_000) -> EINVAL.
##
##      Known limitations / documented for follow-on work:
##        * GET_REGISTRATIONS always reports the empty mask because
##          we do not track per-task registration state.  When real
##          cross-MM threads exist and the barrier path branches on
##          "did this thread register," REGISTER_* needs to set a
##          per-task bit and GET_REGISTRATIONS needs to read it
##          back.  Today every barrier just executes the fence
##          unconditionally, so the missing state is invisible.
##        * No per-CPU targeting: cpu_id is accepted on the three
##          PRIVATE_EXPEDITED variants (matching Linux's flag
##          validation) but the fence is local-only.  When SMP
##          adds cross-MM threads, the EXPEDITED path needs to
##          IPI either the specified CPU (FLAG_CPU set) or every
##          CPU running a thread of the caller's MM.
##        * No RSEQ critical section restart.  Wire into sys_rseq
##          when RSEQ lands; today sys_rseq returns ENOSYS so
##          there is nothing to restart.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).

## 144. [LINUX ABI BATCH 115 - DONE] Two truth-of-the-kernel errno
##      upgrades that close the gap between this file's section
##      header comments and the actual code:
##
##        (a) pkey_alloc(2): ENOSYS -> ENOSPC.
##            File: kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs, sys_pkey_alloc
##            (~line 13984).
##            The section header at ~line 13907 already documents
##            "pkey_alloc returns ENOSPC (no keys available) which
##            is the documented 'PKU disabled' answer", but the code
##            still returned ENOSYS with an inline TODO ("we don't
##            have ENOSPC defined yet").  ENOSPC has been defined at
##            line 935 for a while, so the discrepancy is resolved.
##            ENOSPC is the truthful answer: PKU is not initialised
##            in this kernel (no PKU CR4 bit, no per-process PKRU
##            save/restore), so there are no keys to allocate; on
##            Linux ENOSPC is the documented "no keys available"
##            answer that glibc / libpkey treat as "MPK unavailable,
##            fall back to plain mprotect."
##
##        (b) set_mempolicy_home_node(2): ENOSYS -> 0 (success).
##            File: kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs,
##            sys_set_mempolicy_home_node (~line 14113).
##            The kernel has a single NUMA node (node 0).  After
##            the existing validation (home_node == 0, flags == 0,
##            len > 0, 16 KiB-aligned start), the requested policy
