##            -- pin the home node of [start, start+len) to node 0
##            -- is necessarily already in effect because no other
##            node exists.  Linux returns 0 for this call on a
##            single-node system once the range has a valid MBIND
##            policy (default MPOL_DEFAULT covers all anonymous /
##            file mappings).  Returning 0 is therefore the truthful
##            "applied, no-op" answer, not a placeholder.  The
##            previous ENOSYS forced glibc numa_set_localalloc()
##            callers to log a "kernel lacks home-node API" warning
##            even though our single-node topology means they got
##            exactly the behaviour they asked for.  Section header
##            comment at ~line 13918 also updated to reflect the
##            new "validate and return 0" semantics.
##
##      Self-tests (extend the existing blocks in linux.rs):
##        * pkey_alloc valid (flags=0, rights=0) -> -ENOSPC.
##        * pkey_alloc with PKEY_DISABLE_WRITE rights -> -ENOSPC.
##        * pkey_alloc bad flags -> -EINVAL (unchanged).
##        * pkey_alloc bad rights -> -EINVAL (unchanged).
##        * pkey_free in-range key -> -EINVAL (unchanged; no
##          allocated keys exist).
##        * set_mempolicy_home_node valid (home=0, range aligned,
##          len > 0) -> 0.
##        * Idempotent (same call again) -> 0.
##        * Different valid range -> 0.
##        * Non-zero node, flags, len=0, misaligned start: all
##          unchanged at -EINVAL.
##
##      Known limitations / documented for follow-on work:
##        * pkey_mprotect with a non-default key still returns
##          -EINVAL because no keys can be allocated; this matches
##          a real Linux on a non-PKU CPU.  When PKU is wired into
##          the page tables, pkey_alloc switches to handing out
##          real keys [0..15] and pkey_free / pkey_mprotect gain
##          real meaning.
##        * set_mempolicy_home_node doesn't actually record the
##          policy because there is no MBIND policy storage yet
##          (the existing mbind syscall is also a validate-and-0
##          stub).  When MBIND lands, both sites will need to read
##          back the recorded policy when get_mempolicy is called;
##          today neither read path exists, so the missing storage
##          is invisible.
##        * sys_pkey_alloc still ignores the access_rights bits
##          beyond validation.  When PKU is wired in, the bits
##          will need to be programmed into the initial PKRU.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 24s).

## 145. [LINUX ABI BATCH 116 - DONE] Implement poll(2) and ppoll(2)
##      with a real readiness check over Console / File / Pipe fds,
##      replacing the prior validate-and-ENOSYS stubs.
##
##      File: kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs
##      New code: mod poll_bits, poll_revents_from_entry,
##                poll_compute_revents, poll_core,
##                sys_poll (rewritten), sys_ppoll (rewritten)
##      (~lines 10921-11270 region)
##
##      Readiness semantics per HandleKind:
##        * File: regular files are always reported readable and
##          writable.  Reads past EOF return 0 bytes (not block),
##          and writes never block on a regular file in this kernel,
##          so POLLIN|POLLRDNORM and POLLOUT|POLLWRNORM are always
##          set.  Matches what Linux's per-fs poll callback does
##          for ext4/tmpfs.
##        * Pipe: dispatches to crate::ipc::pipe::poll_status which
##          already returns the same bits Linux uses
##          (POLLIN=0x01 / POLLOUT=0x04 / POLLERR=0x08 /
##          POLLHUP=0x10).  POLLRDNORM / POLLWRNORM are mirrored
##          from POLLIN / POLLOUT so glibc's poll wrapper sees what
##          it expects.
##        * Console: O_WRONLY/O_RDWR -> always writable
##          (POLLOUT|POLLWRNORM).  O_RDONLY (stdin) -> no POLLIN,
##          because there is no Linux-fd-visible keyboard input
##          path yet; matches the behaviour Linux exhibits on a
##          tty with no input source.  When keyboard -> Linux fd
##          plumbing lands, the stdin-readable check becomes a
##          real input-queue-nonempty query.
##
##      Always-implicit bits: POLLERR, POLLHUP, POLLNVAL are reported
##      in revents regardless of whether the caller put them in
##      `events`.  fd < 0 entries report revents = 0 (Linux ignores
##      negative fds).  Unknown fds report POLLNVAL = 0x20.
##
##      Blocking model:
##        * timeout_ms == 0 -> instantaneous check, return count.
##        * timeout_ms > 0 -> poll once; if no fds ready, sleep in
##          10 ms slices via crate::sched::sleep_ms re-polling
##          between each slice, until any fd becomes ready or the
##          deadline expires.  10 ms is small enough to catch pipe
##          state changes from another task without busy-waiting.
##        * timeout_ms < 0 -> wait forever in 10 ms slices.  A
##          state change in any polled fd ends the wait.  When
##          signal delivery lands, EINTR will also end it.
##
##      ppoll(2) translates timespec -> ms (round nsec up so a 1-ns
##      timeout still represents "wait at least 1 ms"), caps at
##      i32::MAX ms, and forwards to the shared poll_core.  NULL
##      timespec means wait forever (-1 ms).  sigmask is validated
##      and ignored — we have no signal delivery, so atomic
##      swap-in/out has no observable effect.
##
##      Why the count is honest, not a placeholder:
##        - Every fd reported as ready actually is ready: regular-
##          file I/O is synchronous, write to a known-open pipe is
##          guaranteed not to block past `pipe::poll_status` saying
##          so, and console stdout/stderr writes don't block.
##        - Every fd reported as not ready actually isn't: stdin
##          has no source, and `pipe::poll_status` is computed from
##          the pipe's live `len` / `buf.capacity` / closed flags
##          under the pipe table lock.
##        - The count returned matches the number of pollfd entries
##          with non-zero revents, which is the precise Linux
##          contract.
##
##      Self-tests in linux.rs (replacing the prior ENOSYS-checking
##      tests for poll/ppoll):
##        * poll(NULL, 0, 0) -> 0 (no fds, no wait).
##        * poll absurd nfds -> EINVAL.
##        * poll nfds>0 NULL ptr -> EFAULT.
##        * poll[{fd=-1, fd=99}, timeout=0] -> count=1; revents
##          stays 0 for fd=-1 and is POLLNVAL(0x20) for fd=99
##          (kernel context = no fd table = unknown fd).
##        * poll_revents_from_entry direct exercise for File:
##            - events=POLLIN|POLLOUT -> revents=POLLIN|POLLOUT.
##            - events including POLLRDNORM|POLLWRNORM ->
##              revents=POLLIN|POLLOUT|POLLRDNORM|POLLWRNORM.
##        * Console O_RDONLY -> revents=0; O_WRONLY -> revents=
##          POLLOUT.
##        * Pipe end-to-end:
##            - empty pipe read end -> 0.
##            - empty pipe write end -> POLLOUT.
##            - write 1 byte, read end -> POLLIN.
##            - close write end, read end -> POLLIN|POLLHUP.
##        * ppoll with sigsetsize=16 -> EINVAL (must be 0 or 8).
##        * ppoll timespec{0,0} -> 0.
##        * ppoll timespec{-1,0} -> EINVAL (negative tv_sec).
##        * ppoll timespec{0, 1e9} -> EINVAL (tv_nsec out of range).
##
##      Known limitations / documented for follow-on work:
##        * No wakeup notification: when a pipe transitions
##          empty->non-empty from another task, the polling task
##          finds out on its next 10 ms re-poll, not immediately.
##          For a real userspace event loop this adds up to 10 ms
##          of latency per readiness transition.  Fix by wiring
##          pipe::write_one_byte to wake any tasks parked in
##          poll_core for that pipe handle (and by exporting a
##          ready-queue from pipe.rs).
##        * No POLLPRI / POLLRDBAND / POLLWRBAND modelling: bits
##          defined for completeness but never set.  Console and
##          pipe don't carry priority data, files don't either.
##        * Console POLLIN never set: stdin has no input source
##          yet.  Fixed when keyboard -> Linux fd plumbing lands;
##          the check goes into the HandleKind::Console arm of
##          poll_revents_from_entry.
##        * select / pselect6 / epoll_* still ENOSYS: select uses
##          fd_set bitmaps (a separate plumbing effort), and
##          epoll needs an EpollFd HandleKind.  Documented in
##          the section header comment.
##        * In kernel context (no caller PCB) poll reports
##          POLLNVAL for every non-negative fd because there is
##          no fd table to look up; this is the same EBADF gate
##          fcntl uses in batch 113.  Real fd-level testing
##          requires a real Linux process, which the boot self-
##          test doesn't construct; instead the engine is tested
##          directly via poll_revents_from_entry with synthetic
##          FdEntry values, mirroring the fcntl(F_GETLK) test
##          pattern.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).

## 146. [LINUX ABI BATCH 117 - DONE] Implement select(2) and pselect6(2)
##      on top of the readiness engine introduced in batch 116.  Both
##      were stub ENOSYS returns; with poll() now wired up there is no
##      reason for the older multiplexing APIs not to work for the same
##      File / Console / Pipe handle kinds.
##
##      Implementation (kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs):
##
##      * New helper `select_core(nfds, rd*, wr*, ex*, len, timeout_ms)`
##        — the moral equivalent of poll_core for fd_set bitmaps.  It:
##          - Quick-returns 0 when nfds == 0 or len == 0, optionally
##            sleeping for a positive timeout (Linux: no fds + finite
##            timeout = a plain timed sleep that returns 0).
##          - Snapshots three input fd_sets via copy_from_user.  A
##            NULL pointer is treated as the all-zero set (Linux
##            convention; you don't have to pass all three).
##          - Allocates three output fd_set buffers (each `len`
##            bytes), zeroed before each polling iteration so a
##            transient earlier readiness doesn't bleed through.
##          - Iterates fds in [0, nfds), synthesises a poll events
##            mask (POLLIN|POLLRDNORM for want_r, POLLOUT|POLLWRNORM
##            for want_w, POLLPRI for want_x), and dispatches through
##            the same `poll_compute_revents` engine poll/ppoll use.
##            One shared readiness oracle for both APIs — no code
##            duplication, no chance for the two to drift.
##          - Maps revents back to the three Linux fd_set categories:
##              * readable  if want_r AND
##                  revents & (POLLIN|POLLRDNORM|POLLHUP|POLLERR) != 0
##                (Linux treats HUP and ERR as "readable so the
##                 caller's next read returns EOF / the error.")
##              * writable  if want_w AND
##                  revents & (POLLOUT|POLLWRNORM|POLLERR) != 0
##                (ERR is reported writable so the caller's next
##                 write surfaces the error.)
##              * exceptional if want_x AND
##                  revents & POLLPRI != 0
##                (POLLPRI is the canonical out-of-band signal.)
##          - **EBADF semantics** — unlike poll(2), which reports
##            POLLNVAL in revents and keeps going, select(2)
##            short-circuits with EBADF the moment any fd the caller
##            asked about turns out to be invalid.  The first
##            POLLNVAL we observe terminates the call with EBADF;
##            output buffers are not written back.  This matches
##            Linux's select(2) man-page contract:
##              "EBADF  An invalid file descriptor was given in one
##                       of the sets.  (Perhaps a file descriptor
##                       that was already closed, or one on which
##                       an error has occurred.)"
##          - Same 10 ms re-poll slicing as poll_core: balances
##            responsiveness against busy-waiting, no wakeup
##            infrastructure needed.
##          - On return, copy_to_user the three output buffers —
##            but only for sets the caller actually supplied (NULL
##            input stays NULL output; we do not synthesise a
##            zero-filled buffer Linux didn't ask us to write to).
##          - Return value is the count of fds with at least one
##            output bit set, exactly like Linux.
##
##      * `sys_select(nfds, rd*, wr*, ex*, tv*)` — classic select.
##        timeval is 16 bytes (i64 tv_sec + i64 tv_usec).  NULL tv =
##        "wait forever" (-1).  Non-NULL: validate, copy_from_user,
##        reject tv_sec < 0 or tv_usec out of [0, 1_000_000) with
##        EINVAL.  Round non-zero microseconds *up* to whole ms so a
##        1us timeout still gives at least 1 ms of waiting.  Total
##        ms is capped at i32::MAX.  fd_set buffers are validated
##        for write access up front via validate_user_write so we
##        catch EFAULT before any work.
##
##      * `sys_pselect6(nfds, rd*, wr*, ex*, ts*, sigmask_arg*)` —
##        same as select but with timespec (i64 tv_sec + i64 tv_nsec,
##        nsec in [0, 1e9)) and an optional sigmask_arg pointing to
##        `struct { const sigset_t *ss; size_t ss_len; }` (16 bytes).
##        We validate sigmask_arg for read access if non-NULL but
##        ignore its contents — we have no signal delivery yet, so
##        masking signals during the wait is a no-op.  Timespec is
##        converted to ms exactly like ppoll does.
##
##      * The mapping rule "POLLNVAL -> EBADF" is also documented in
##        the readable/writable mapping comments inside select_core so
##        the next session reading the code understands why poll and
##        select diverge here.
##
##      Boot-time self-tests (kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs, replacing
##      the old ENOSYS stubs):
##
##        * select(0, NULL, NULL, NULL, &{0,0}) -> 0
##          (no fds, zero timeout = immediate return; Linux's
##           well-known "timed sleep with no fds" idiom).
##        * select(nfds < 0) -> EINVAL  (via fd_set_byte_len gate).
##        * select(nfds > 1<<20) -> EINVAL  (same gate, sanity cap
##          matching the kernel's own select(2) limit).
##        * select with timeval{-1, 0} -> EINVAL  (tv_sec < 0).
##        * select with timeval{0, 1_000_000} -> EINVAL
##          (tv_usec out of [0,1e6)).
##        * select with timeval{0, -1} -> EINVAL  (tv_usec negative).
##        * select with bit 3 set in readfds + tv={0,0} from kernel
##          context (no PCB -> no fd table -> every fd is invalid)
##          -> -EBADF.  Exercises the POLLNVAL short-circuit.
##        * pselect6(0, NULL, NULL, NULL, &{0,0}, NULL) -> 0.
##        * pselect6 with timespec{0, 1e9} -> EINVAL.
##        * pselect6 with timespec{-1, 0} -> EINVAL.
##
##      Known limitations / documented for follow-on work:
##        * Inherits the 10 ms wakeup-latency limitation from
##          poll_core — when a pipe transitions empty->non-empty
##          from another task, the polling task finds out on the
##          next re-poll slice.  Fixed when pipe::write_one_byte
##          gains a ready-queue wakeup hook.
##        * pselect6 silently ignores the supplied signal mask
##          because the kernel still has no Linux-style signal
##          delivery surface.  Once we wire up signals, the mask
##          needs to be installed for the duration of the wait
##          and restored on return (the whole reason pselect6
##          exists vs. select+sigprocmask).
##        * No POLLPRI traffic ever generated by any HandleKind,
##          so the exceptional fd_set will always come back empty
##          in practice.  This matches what poll/ppoll report and
##          is the correct answer until OOB data (console BREAK,
##          TCP urgent) gets a real source.
##        * select still uses the historical 1<<20 fd cap (128 KiB
##          per fd_set).  That's plenty for any realistic process
##          today and matches Linux's hard cap; the more usual
##          1024-fd RLIMIT_NOFILE limit applies at a higher layer.
##        * In kernel context (no caller PCB) select cannot tell
##          the difference between "really invalid fd" and "we are
##          in the kernel self-test where there is no fd table" —
##          both look like EBADF.  Real fd-level testing requires
##          a real Linux process which the boot self-test does
##          not construct; the engine itself is exercised in batch
##          116 via direct calls to poll_revents_from_entry.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).

## 147. [LINUX ABI BATCH 118 - DONE] Implement clone3(2) on top of the
##      existing clone(2) machinery.  Before this batch, clone3 returned
##      ENOSYS after a thin validation gate (size >= 88, ptr non-NULL,
##      readable).  glibc 2.34+ and modern Rust std prefer clone3 over
##      clone() when libc reports linux >= 5.3, so leaving clone3 ENOSYS
##      added a wasted syscall per thread/process spawn (the kernel says
##      ENOSYS, the libc caches the answer and falls back to clone()).
##      Wiring clone3 through to the same code path clone() uses makes
##      the modern API a first-class citizen at no marginal kernel cost.
##
##      Implementation (kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs):
##
##      * Extracted `linux_clone_inner(flags, child_stack, parent_tid,
##        child_tid, new_tls, &mut frame) -> i64` from `linux_clone` so
##        clone3 can dispatch through the same thread-creation /
##        fork-equivalent split without going through the SyscallFrame
##        arg-register convention twice.  `linux_clone` is now a 6-line
##        wrapper that just unpacks frame.arg0..arg4 and calls inner.
##      * New `validate_clone3_args(cl_args_ptr, size) -> Result<
##        ClonedArgs, errno>` shared helper.  It:
##          - Rejects NULL with EFAULT.
##          - Rejects size < 64 (CLONE_ARGS_SIZE_VER0) or > 4096 with
##            EINVAL.  Linux's PAGE_SIZE cap is 4 KiB on x86; we keep
##            the same cap even though our base page is 16 KiB.
##          - Rejects misaligned ptr or size with EINVAL (every field
##            is __u64; the struct must be 8-byte aligned).
##          - validate_user_read for `size` bytes.
##          - copy_from_user into a stack `[u64; 11]` array; fields
##            past the user-supplied size are implicitly zero, matching
##            Linux's copy_struct_from_user zero-extension semantics.
##          - Reads 11 fields: flags, pidfd_out, child_tid, parent_tid,
##            exit_signal, stack_base, stack_size, tls, set_tid,
##            set_tid_size, cgroup.
##          - Rejects exit_signal > 0xFF (Linux constrains it to the
##            CSIGNAL byte).
##          - Rejects flags with any CSIGNAL bit set (the byte lives
##            in exit_signal alone in clone3).
##          - Rejects features we don't support: CLONE_PIDFD,
##            CLONE_INTO_CGROUP, CLONE_CLEAR_SIGHAND, set_tid_size>0,
##            non-zero cgroup fd, any CLONE_NEW* namespace flag.
##          - Rejects lopsided stack (base != 0 XOR size != 0).
##          - Computes child_stack = stack_base + stack_size (clone(2)
##            ABI wants the *top* of the stack; x86_64 stacks grow
##            down).  Detects overflow with checked_add -> EINVAL.
##          - Returns ClonedArgs with the flags+exit_signal byte
##            recombined into clone(2)'s flags-word layout.
##      * New `linux_clone3(&mut frame)` calls validate_clone3_args,
##        then forwards through `linux_clone_inner` with the translated
##        args.  Wired into `dispatch_linux_with_frame` so real
##        userspace clone3 calls take this path.
##      * `sys_clone3` (the args-only dispatch entry) was rewritten to
##        call validate_clone3_args and return ENOSYS on success — it
##        has no frame to fork from, so it can't actually clone.  This
##        is the path the boot self-test hits; real Linux callers
##        never reach it because dispatch_linux_with_frame intercepts
##        nr::CLONE3 first.
##      * Added 13 new CLONE_* constants the validation needs:
##        CLONE_PIDFD (0x1000, bit 12), CLONE_NEWTIME (0x80, bit 7),
##        CLONE_NEWCGROUP/UTS/IPC/USER/PID/NET (bits 25-30), CLONE_IO
##        (bit 31), CLONE_CLEAR_SIGHAND (bit 32), CLONE_INTO_CGROUP
##        (bit 33).  Source: include/uapi/linux/sched.h.
##
##      Boot-time self-tests (kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs, expanded
##      from 3 cases to 13):
##
##        * clone3(NULL, 88) -> EFAULT.
##        * clone3(buf, 32) -> EINVAL (below VER0=64).
##        * clone3(buf, 8192) -> EINVAL (above 4 KiB cap).
##        * clone3(buf|1, 64) -> EINVAL (unaligned ptr).
##        * clone3(buf, 65) -> EINVAL (size not multiple of 8).
##        * clone3(buf, 64) all-zero -> ENOSYS (VER0 path, valid).
##        * clone3(buf, 88) all-zero -> ENOSYS (VER2 path, valid).
##        * clone3 with CLONE_PIDFD in flags -> EINVAL.
##        * clone3 with CSIGNAL bit (SIGCHLD=17) in flags -> EINVAL.
##        * clone3 with exit_signal=0x100 -> EINVAL.
##        * clone3 with stack_base set, stack_size=0 -> EINVAL.
##        * clone3 with set_tid_size=1 -> EINVAL.
##        * clone3 with CLONE_NEWPID -> EINVAL.
##
##      Known limitations / documented for follow-on work:
##        * CLONE_PIDFD requires a new HandleKind::Pidfd plus the
##          fd-allocation plumbing that goes with it.  Deferred until
##          we have a real pidfd subsystem (which also lets us implement
##          pidfd_open, pidfd_send_signal, pidfd_getfd).
##        * CLONE_INTO_CGROUP / cgroup field require a cgroup subsystem
##          which the microkernel doesn't have (and per design.txt
##          probably never will in the Linux sense — control groups are
##          a Linux-specific abstraction).  Reject with EINVAL.
##        * set_tid (manual TID assignment in a PID namespace
##          hierarchy) requires PID namespaces; deferred.
##        * CLONE_CLEAR_SIGHAND requires per-thread signal handler
##          tables independent from the PCB.  Our signal model still
##          shares handlers PCB-wide.
##        * Namespace clones (CLONE_NEW{NS,CGROUP,UTS,IPC,USER,PID,NET,
##          TIME}) all rejected — no namespace subsystem.  This is a
##          deliberate microkernel design choice (containers will be
##          built differently per design.txt).
##        * In kernel context (no frame available) sys_clone3 returns
##          ENOSYS after full validation rather than actually cloning;
##          this is the boot-self-test code path.  Real userspace
##          clone3 always goes through linux_clone3 which has the
##          frame and routes to either thread_clone::clone_thread or
##          fork::fork_process.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).

## 148. [LINUX ABI BATCH 119 - DONE] Add FUTEX_WAIT_BITSET (op=9) and
##      FUTEX_WAKE_BITSET (op=10) to the classic futex(2) syscall, on
##      top of the existing single-futex wait/wake handlers.  These two
##      ops are what glibc's pthread_cond_wait (Linux 2.6.25+) and Rust
##      std's thread parking primitives actually use — pre-batch they
##      hit the `_ => linux_err(errno::ENOSYS)` default in sys_futex
##      and glibc fell back to either a sched_yield spin (very bad on
##      a desktop) or the unmasked FUTEX_WAIT path that gives up the
##      whole point of bitsets (avoiding spurious cross-condvar
##      wakeups).  Wiring the BITSET ops makes pthread condvars and
##      Rust parking work end-to-end against the same futex bucket
##      table that batch 22 set up.
##
##      Files: kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs (sys_futex extended;
##      handlers untouched), boot-time self-tests in the same file.
##
##      Implementation:
##        * Two new op codes added inside sys_futex.  FUTEX_PRIVATE_FLAG
##          (0x80) and FUTEX_CLOCK_REALTIME (0x100) are now sampled from
##          the raw op *before* the FUTEX_CMD_MASK strip — the raw bit
##          for CLOCK_REALTIME selects the clock used to convert the
##          absolute deadline into a relative ns count for the existing
##          handlers::sys_futex_wait_timeout helper.
##        * FUTEX_WAKE_BITSET: val3 (args.arg5, low 32 bits) is the
##          mask.  Linux rejects mask==0 with -EINVAL up front; we
##          mirror that exactly.  Any non-zero mask forwards to
##          handlers::sys_futex_wake(addr, val) — we do NOT yet filter
##          per-waiter bitmasks, so the mask is best-effort (any waiter
##          on the same address may be woken).  POSIX/Linux allow
##          spurious wakeups; correct callers re-check their predicate,
##          so this is compatible with pthread_cond / Rust park.
##        * FUTEX_WAIT_BITSET: same mask validation.  Timeout is an
##          ABSOLUTE deadline on either CLOCK_REALTIME or
##          CLOCK_MONOTONIC depending on the FUTEX_CLOCK_REALTIME bit.
##          We read the timespec, compute now_ns from
##          crate::timekeeping::{clock_realtime, clock_monotonic}, take
##          saturating_sub(abs_ns, now_ns) to derive a relative ns,
##          then dispatch to handlers::sys_futex_wait_timeout.  When
##          the deadline is in the past the saturating_sub yields 0
##          which the futex layer treats as "value-compare only" — the
##          right semantics for an expired absolute timeout.  NULL
##          timeout routes to handlers::sys_futex_wait (no deadline).
##
##      Boot-time self-tests (kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs, 11 cases
##      total).  All run in the bare kernel task: there is no PCB, so
##      the mm::user::validate_user_* gates bypass.  The tests are
##      carefully crafted to NEVER block — either mask=0 short-circuits
##      with EINVAL, the wake target has no waiters (futex_wake returns
##      0), or the wait value (=1) intentionally mismatches the kernel
##      buffer's value (=0) so futex_wait/futex_wait_timeout returns
##      Ok(false) at the value-compare step *before* reaching the
##      block-current path:
##
##        * FUTEX_WAKE_BITSET with val3=0 -> EINVAL.
##        * FUTEX_WAIT_BITSET with val3=0 -> EINVAL.
##        * FUTEX_WAKE_BITSET mask=MATCH_ANY, val=0 -> 0 (no waiters).
##        * FUTEX_WAKE_BITSET mask=1, val=10 -> 0 (no waiters).
##        * FUTEX_WAIT_BITSET mask=MATCH_ANY, NULL timeout, value
##          mismatch -> 0 (Ok(false)).
##        * FUTEX_WAIT_BITSET mask=MATCH_ANY, absolute ts=(0,0)
##          (always past), value mismatch -> 0.
##        * FUTEX_WAKE_BITSET | FUTEX_PRIVATE_FLAG with val3=0 ->
##          EINVAL (verifies private flag is stripped before op match).
##        * FUTEX_WAIT_BITSET | FUTEX_CLOCK_REALTIME | FUTEX_PRIVATE_FLAG
##          with mask=MATCH_ANY, ts=(0,0), value mismatch -> 0
##          (exercises the clock_realtime() branch of the abs->rel
##          conversion).
##        * Unknown op (op=11, which would be FUTEX_LOCK_PI2) ->
##          ENOSYS — verifies the unknown-op default is preserved.
##        * Regression: FUTEX_WAKE with no waiters -> 0.
##        * Regression: FUTEX_WAIT with value mismatch -> 0.
##
##      Known limitations / documented for follow-on work:
##        * Per-waiter bitmask filtering is not implemented.  The
##          FUTEX_WAKE_BITSET mask is consulted only to reject 0; any
##          non-zero mask is treated as MATCH_ANY at the queue layer.
##          Real Linux walks the wait queue and skips waiters whose
##          stored bitmask doesn't intersect.  Fixing this requires
##          adding a `bitmask: u32` field to the Waiter struct in
##          kernel/src/ipc/futex.rs and threading it through
##          futex_wait_with_mask / futex_wake_with_mask.  Practical
##          impact is bounded: callers see at-most-spurious wakeups,
##          which they already handle for POSIX correctness.  This is
##          a follow-up batch.
##        * Pre-existing translation gap: handlers::sys_futex_wait
##          returns Ok(false) (= SyscallResult::ok(0)) on value
##          mismatch, but Linux returns -EAGAIN for the same condition.
##          linux_from_native passes 0 through unchanged because
##          value >= 0 is the success branch.  Callers that strictly
##          check for -EAGAIN to detect "the futex word changed under
##          us" will instead see a successful return and re-loop —
##          equivalent to an instant spurious wakeup.  pthread_cond
##          and Rust park both handle spurious wakeups, so this is
##          benign in practice.  Documented for a future cleanup batch
##          that adds an EAGAIN translation in the linux_from_native
##          path (or in a futex-specific result wrapper).
##        * FUTEX_REQUEUE / FUTEX_CMP_REQUEUE (used by
##          pthread_cond_broadcast for the optimized requeue-batch path)
##          still return -ENOSYS.  Implementing them needs a
##          futex_requeue helper that atomically transfers waiters from
##          one bucket to another while running the cmpval check.  The
##          futex.rs bucket layout already supports this; what's
##          missing is the requeue routine and its handler.  Deferred
##          to a future batch focused on pthread_cond_broadcast
##          throughput.
##        * FUTEX_WAKE_OP (atomic op-then-wake) still ENOSYS — used by
##          glibc's older condvar implementation but not by current
##          glibc (>= 2.25 dropped it).  Low-value to add.
##        * FUTEX_LOCK_PI / FUTEX_UNLOCK_PI / FUTEX_TRYLOCK_PI /
##          FUTEX_LOCK_PI2 / FUTEX_WAIT_REQUEUE_PI /
##          FUTEX_CMP_REQUEUE_PI: native handlers exist
##          (handlers::sys_futex_lock_pi, sys_futex_unlock_pi) but
##          require the FUTEX-encoded owner-tid invariant on the
##          uaddr word (low bits hold the owner's TID, high bit is
##          FUTEX_WAITERS).  Wiring them through sys_futex needs the
##          owner-tid layout adapter; doable but cross-cutting enough
##          to warrant its own batch.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##
## 149. Batch 120 — futex2 wake/wait routed through to legacy handlers,
##      with the FUTEX2 flag layout fixed.
##
##      The pre-existing futex2_wake / futex2_wait stubs in
##      kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs validated `flags` against
##      `const VALID: u32 = 1 | 2 | 4` and then returned -ENOSYS for
##      every conforming call.  That treatment had two problems:
##        (a) the constants treated the low two flag bits as independent
##            switches (PRIVATE/U32/NUMA).  Linux's actual FUTEX2 layout
##            uses bits [0..1] as a 2-bit size *field*
##            (FUTEX2_SIZE_MASK = 0x3) where the only implemented value
##            is FUTEX2_SIZE_U32 = 0x2.  Bit 2 is FUTEX2_NUMA = 0x4 and
##            bit 7 is FUTEX2_PRIVATE = 0x80.  Our previous "valid" mask
##            therefore accepted FUTEX2_SIZE_U8 (flags=0) and rejected
##            FUTEX2_PRIVATE (flags=0x80) — exactly inverted from real
##            Linux on every modern caller.
##        (b) returning -ENOSYS forced glibc/Rust to fall back to the
##            classic futex(2) op-with-bitset path on every wake/wait.
##            With the underlying handlers::sys_futex_wake /
##            sys_futex_wait[_timeout] already in place, the routing
##            was a 10-line plumb job — no excuse for the fallback.
##
##      Implementation (sys_futex2_wake, sys_futex2_wait):
##        * Reject unknown flag bits with -EINVAL
##          (flags & !(SIZE_MASK|NUMA|PRIVATE) != 0).
##        * Reject any size other than U32 with -EINVAL.  Matches Linux's
##          state of the world today (the other size variants are
##          syntactically defined but not implemented kernel-side).
##        * Reject NUMA with -ENOSYS.  Our futex hash is global; per-node
##          buckets would need a redesign of ipc::futex bucket sharding.
##        * Accept PRIVATE silently — we never differentiated private vs
##          shared in the classic futex either, and PRIVATE is purely an
##          optimization hint.
##        * Reject mask == 0 with -EINVAL (a zero bitmask matches nothing).
##        * Validate uaddr is 4-byte readable; return EFAULT on miss.
##
##      futex2_wake forwarding: build a SyscallArgs with arg0=uaddr,
##      arg1=nr (cast from i32, already validated >= 0), pass to
##      handlers::sys_futex_wake.  Mask is consumed and dropped — same
##      caveat as FUTEX_WAKE_BITSET from batch 119 (no per-waiter mask
##      state in the queue).
##
##      futex2_wait forwarding:
##        * clockid restricted to 0 (CLOCK_MONOTONIC) or 1
##          (CLOCK_REALTIME) per Linux's tighter check on this path.
##          Other clocks are -EINVAL.
##        * NULL timespec → indefinite wait → handlers::sys_futex_wait.
##        * Non-NULL timespec is an **absolute** deadline on the chosen
##          clock.  Read with read_timespec, convert to relative ns via
##          `abs_ns.saturating_sub(now_ns)`, then call
##          handlers::sys_futex_wait_timeout.  The saturating_sub
##          collapses "deadline already past" to rel_ns=0, which the
##          handler resolves correctly: value-compare first, then
##          ETIMEDOUT on hit (vs. Ok(false) → 0 on mismatch).
##
##      Boot self-tests (16 cases, replacing the 6 pre-batch ENOSYS
##      regression tests in the fchmodat2/futex2 block):
##        FUTEX2 wake:
##          1. NULL uaddr2 → EFAULT.
##          2. nr < 0 (u64::MAX cast to i32 → negative) → EINVAL.
##          3. Unknown flag bit (0x100) → EINVAL.
##          4. Non-U32 size (flags=0 ⇒ SIZE_U8) → EINVAL.
##          5. NUMA bit set (with U32) → ENOSYS.
##          6. mask=0 (with U32) → EINVAL.
##          7. Valid PRIVATE|U32, nr=0, mask=MAX → 0 (no waiters).
##          8. Valid U32 only, nr=10, mask=1 → 0 (no waiters).
##        FUTEX2 wait:
##          9. Bad clockid (99) → EINVAL.
##         10. clockid=2 (BOOTTIME — disallowed on futex2 path) → EINVAL.
##         11. mask=0 → EINVAL.
##         12. NUMA → ENOSYS.
##         13. Non-U32 size → EINVAL.
##         14. Value mismatch (expected=1, buffer[0]=0), no timeout → 0
##             (Ok(false) → SyscallResult::ok(0); same pre-EAGAIN
##             translation gap documented in batch 119, behaves as a
##             spurious wakeup to glibc / Rust park).
##         15. Past absolute MONOTONIC deadline (ts=(0,0)) → 0
##             (value-mismatch short-circuit beats the timeout branch).
##         16. Past absolute REALTIME deadline (clockid=1, PRIVATE|U32)
##             → 0.
##
##      Known limitations carried forward:
##        * Per-waiter bitmask filtering: futex2_wake still wakes any
##          waiter on uaddr regardless of mask.  Same upgrade path as
##          FUTEX_WAKE_BITSET — adding per-waiter mask state in
##          ipc::futex flips the behaviour with no callsite changes.
##        * EAGAIN translation: value-mismatch on the WAIT path returns 0
##          to userspace instead of -EAGAIN.  Pre-existing, documented
##          in batch 119.
##        * futex2 elements other than U32 (U8/U16/U64) are rejected
##          with EINVAL.  Matches Linux today; if Linux ever lights up
##          U64 futexes, this needs a wider WAIT handler in
##          ipc::futex (the value compare and wake bucket hash both
##          assume u32).
##        * futex2_requeue still ENOSYS.  Same blocker as
##          FUTEX_CMP_REQUEUE — needs a futex_requeue helper in
##          ipc::futex that atomically transfers waiters between
##          buckets while running the cmpval check.  Deferred to the
##          same future batch.
##        * NUMA path: returns ENOSYS rather than silently ignoring the
##          flag.  If callers fall back gracefully (Linux man page says
##          they should), this is the right answer; if they hard-fail,
##          a future revision could ignore NUMA when our hash remains
##          global.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##
## 150. Batch 121 — rseq(2) registration accepted instead of returning
##      ENOSYS at glibc startup.
##
##      glibc 2.35+ calls rseq() from every thread's startup so it can
##      use per-CPU malloc tcache slot selection without a syscall on
##      each allocation.  Before batch 121 we returned -ENOSYS, which
##      glibc tolerates (falls back to a sched_getcpu-based path) but
##      logs in diagnostic builds and degrades malloc throughput.
##
##      Implementation:
##        * New per-task store in kernel/src/proc/thread_clone.rs:
##            RSEQ: Mutex<BTreeMap<TaskId, (ptr, len, sig)>>
##          register_rseq / lookup_rseq / unregister_rseq.
##        * on_thread_exit_hook now also drops the rseq entry (after the
##          robust-list drop, before the CLEAR_CHILD_TID handling).
##          No userspace write at exit — Linux doesn't either; the
##          thread's address space is going away.
##        * kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs::sys_rseq replaces the ENOSYS
##          stub with a real register/unregister:
##            - Validate flags ∈ {0, 1=UNREGISTER}, len == 32, ptr != 0,
##              ptr & 0x1F == 0 (32-byte alignment, Linux-enforced),
##              ptr 32-byte readable AND writable.
##            - On UNREGISTER: lookup_rseq must return Some with matching
##              (ptr, len, sig); -EINVAL on mismatch or "never registered".
##              Successful unregister drops the entry and returns 0.
##            - On register (flags & UNREGISTER == 0):
##                * If already registered, -EBUSY (Linux behaviour: no
##                  idempotent re-register, userspace must unregister
##                  first).
##                * Insert the (ptr, len, sig) triple.
##                * Zero the userspace runtime fields the kernel owns:
##                  - cpu_id_start (off 0..4) = 0
##                  - cpu_id        (off 4..8) = 0
##                  - node_id       (off 20..24) = 0
##                  - mm_cid        (off 24..28) = 0
##                  Leave rseq_cs (off 8..16), flags (off 16..20), and
##                  padding (off 28..32) untouched — those are
##                  user-owned per the ABI.
##                * If any copy_to_user fails (e.g. the page got
##                  unmapped between validation and write), roll back
##                  the registration so the task is in a clean
##                  unregistered state.
##                * Return 0.
##
##      Boot self-tests (12 cases, replacing the 4 pre-batch ENOSYS
##      tests in the seccomp/ptrace/membarrier/rseq block):
##        Validation:
##          1. wrong len (16) -> EINVAL.
##          2. bad flags (0x2 not RSEQ_FLAG_UNREGISTER) -> EINVAL.
##          3. NULL ptr -> EFAULT.
##          4. unaligned ptr (ptr+1) -> EINVAL.
##          5. unregister with no prior registration -> EINVAL.
##        Round-trip:
##          6. register with sig=0x53053053 (glibc) -> 0.
##          7. Verify view of the 32-byte buffer: bytes [0..8] zeroed
##             (cpu_id_start, cpu_id), bytes [8..20] still 0xAA
##             (rseq_cs + flags, untouched), bytes [20..28] zeroed
##             (node_id, mm_cid), bytes [28..32] still 0xAA (padding).
##          8. double-register -> EBUSY.
##          9. unregister with mismatched sig (0xDEADBEEF) -> EINVAL.
##         10. unregister with matching sig -> 0.
##         11. re-register after unregister with a different sig -> 0.
##         12. cleanup unregister -> 0 (so the RSEQ table is empty at
##             the end of the rseq test block; matters because the
##             current task carries through subsequent boot tests).
##
##      Known limitations / deferred work:
##        * No per-preemption cpu_id update.  Linux's scheduler writes
##          the current CPU back into *rseq on every context switch
##          (and walks rseq_cs to check whether the section was
##          crossed, rewriting RIP to abort_ip if so).  We are
##          single-CPU today: cpu_id stays 0 forever, no abort is ever
##          warranted, and glibc's per-CPU malloc fast path always
##          "succeeds" against cpu 0.  When SMP scheduling lands this
##          hook MUST be added before correctness holds — otherwise
##          per-CPU tcache could be raced under migration.  Tracked
##          here as a hard prerequisite for SMP enablement.
##        * The `sig` field is stored and round-tripped but never
##          consulted at abort time, because we never abort (see
##          above).  When the abort hook lands it MUST verify the u32
##          immediately preceding the abort_ip matches the stored
##          sig — this is Linux's defence against attacker-supplied
##          abort handlers.
##        * The clone path does not inherit / propagate rseq across
##          thread creation.  glibc's pthread_create registers rseq
##          inside the child thread's own startup, so this is correct
##          for the common case.  fork() also doesn't carry rseq, by
##          design (Linux: rseq is per-task, child has none).
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##
## 151. Batch 122 — adjtimex(2) / clock_adjtime(2) modes=0 read path
##      returns a real struct timex snapshot instead of EPERM.
##
##      Story: chrony, ntpd, and `timedatectl status` all start by
##      issuing adjtimex(&buf) with buf.modes == 0 to read the kernel's
##      current NTP / timex state (status flags, last frequency
##      correction, the realtime epoch the kernel observed).  Our
##      previous stub returned -EPERM for any non-NULL argument,
##      which caused those tools to abort during their probe phase
##      before they ever attempted to discipline the clock.
##
##      Implementation (kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs):
##        * TIMEX_STRUCT_SIZE = 208 (matches Linux x86_64 layout).
##        * Constants:
##            TIME_ERROR  = 5    (return code when STA_UNSYNC is set)
##            STA_UNSYNC  = 0x0040
##            STA_NANO    = 0x2000
##        * adjtimex_fill_read(tx_ptr) helper:
##            - Stack-builds a zeroed 208-byte buffer.
##            - Fills the fields userspace actually inspects on a
##              modes=0 read:
##                maxerror  (off 24..32)  = 16_000_000  us
##                status    (off 40..44)  = STA_UNSYNC | STA_NANO
##                constant  (off 48..56)  = 2
##                precision (off 56..64)  = 1
##                tolerance (off 64..72)  = 32_768_000
##                time.tv_sec  (off 72..80) = realtime / 1e9
##                time.tv_usec (off 80..88) = realtime % 1e9
##                                            (STA_NANO -> nanoseconds)
##                tick      (off 88..96)  = 10_000   us (10 ms)
##            - Single copy_to_user; on EFAULT propagate the error
##              code through linux_errno_for.
##            - Returns TIME_ERROR=5 on success, matching Linux's
##              "we are not yet synchronised" answer.
##        * sys_adjtimex:
##            - NULL tx -> EFAULT.
##            - Validate 208-byte read of tx for modes.
##            - Read modes (off 0..4).  Non-zero -> EPERM (we still
##              don't implement clock discipline; userspace must
##              tolerate this).  Zero -> adjtimex_fill_read.
##        * sys_clock_adjtime:
##            - clk_id must be CLOCK_REALTIME(0) or CLOCK_TAI(11);
##              anything else -> EINVAL (matches Linux:
##              only these two are user-disciplinable).
##            - NULL tx -> EFAULT.
##            - Same modes==0 fill / modes!=0 EPERM split.
##            - CLOCK_TAI returns the same snapshot as CLOCK_REALTIME
##              because our TAI-UTC offset is currently 0 (see
##              limitations below).
##
##      Boot self-tests (8 cases, replacing the previous 2-test
##      EPERM-only block):
##         1. adjtimex(NULL) -> EFAULT.
##         2. adjtimex(modes=0) -> 5; verify status word at off 40
##            equals STA_UNSYNC|STA_NANO (0x2040); verify time.tv_sec
##            at off 72 is non-zero (realtime epoch is populated).
##         3. adjtimex(modes=1=ADJ_OFFSET) -> EPERM.
##         4. clock_adjtime(clk=5, &buf, modes=0) -> EINVAL
##            (unsupported clock).
##         5. clock_adjtime(CLOCK_REALTIME, NULL, _) -> EFAULT.
##         6. clock_adjtime(CLOCK_REALTIME, &buf, modes=0) -> 5;
##            same status / tv_sec checks as case 2.
##         7. clock_adjtime(CLOCK_TAI, &buf, modes=0) -> 5.
##         8. clock_adjtime(CLOCK_REALTIME, &buf, modes=1) -> EPERM.
##
##      Known limitations / deferred work:
##        * No actual clock discipline.  We do not run an NTP loop,
##          do not maintain a frequency offset, and never clear
##          STA_UNSYNC.  Userspace that needs a steered clock has
##          to keep stepping CLOCK_REALTIME manually via
##          clock_settime; we always advertise "unsynchronised, here
##          is the current monotonically-advancing realtime".
##        * tv_usec carries nanoseconds (STA_NANO is set).  Tools
##          that ignore the STA_NANO bit and treat the field as
##          microseconds will see absurd "fractional second"
##          values.  This is correct per the Linux ABI but worth
##          flagging if compatibility issues appear with very old
##          ntpd builds.
##        * TAI offset is 0 -> CLOCK_TAI is indistinguishable from
##          CLOCK_REALTIME.  Real Linux today carries the
##          accumulated TAI-UTC leap second delta (37 s in 2026);
##          consumers that expect CLOCK_TAI to be ahead of
##          CLOCK_REALTIME will see them equal.  When we eventually
##          ingest a leap-second table the fill path must add the
##          offset for clk == CLOCK_TAI.
##        * adjtimex returns a fixed snapshot for tick / tolerance /
##          precision rather than reading from a configurable
##          per-system timekeeping state.  When clock discipline
##          actually lands these become live fields.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##
## 152. Batch 123 — eventfd(2) / eventfd2(2) wired through to the
##      in-kernel ipc::eventfd counter (no longer ENOSYS).
##
##      Story: D-Bus, Wayland clients, libuv, Qt's QEventDispatcher,
##      glibc's pthread cancel/setxid paths, and almost every modern
##      Linux daemon that needs a kernel-mediated wakeup primitive
##      uses eventfd.  Returning ENOSYS made those paths fall back to
##      pipes — which costs an extra fd, an 8-byte buffer copy, and
##      doesn't give the counter-coalescing semantics callers rely on
##      (eventfd write+read coalesces many signals into one wakeup).
##      We already had a fully-working counter primitive in
##      kernel/src/ipc/eventfd.rs (1056 lines, used by completion
##      ports and the bench/IPC tests); the gap was that nothing
##      plumbed it into the Linux fd table.
##
##      Implementation:
##
##      1. kernel/src/proc/linux_fd.rs
##         * New HandleKind::EventFd variant.
##         * needs_kernel_close() returns true for EventFd (the entry
##           must be released from EVENTFD_TABLE on last close).
##         * FdEntry::eventfd(handle, status_flags) constructor — the
##           raw_handle field stores the EventFdHandle's u64.
##
##      2. kernel/src/ipc/eventfd.rs
##         * New pub fn poll_status(handle) -> u16, returning Linux
##           poll(2) bit values:
##             POLLIN  (0x01) if counter > 0
##             POLLOUT (0x04) if counter < MAX_COUNTER
##             POLLHUP (0x10) if closed
##             POLLERR (0x08) if handle is no longer in the table.
##
##      3. kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs
##         * close_handle: EventFd arm calls ipc::eventfd::close.
##         * dispatch_write: new EventFd arm via dispatch_eventfd_write
##           helper:
##             - count < 8                 -> EINVAL
##             - copy_from_user 8 LE bytes
##             - value == u64::MAX         -> EINVAL  (Linux sentinel)
##             - value == 0                -> no-op, returns 8
##             - O_NONBLOCK + would overflow -> EAGAIN
##             - blocking + would overflow  -> ipc::eventfd::write
##               blocks until a reader drains the counter
##             - success returns 8.
##         * dispatch_read: new EventFd arm via dispatch_eventfd_read
##           helper:
##             - cap < 8                   -> EINVAL
##             - O_NONBLOCK + empty        -> EAGAIN
##             - blocking + empty          -> ipc::eventfd::read blocks
##               until a writer signals
##             - In default mode the entire counter is drained and the
##               u64 is copied LE to userspace; in semaphore mode the
##               counter is decremented by 1 and a `1u64` is returned.
##             - success returns 8.
##         * Every other `match entry.kind` site got an EventFd arm:
##             - lseek, pread64, pwrite64, preadv, pwritev, preadv2,
##               pwritev2     -> ESPIPE (not seekable)
##             - fsync / fdatasync           -> EINVAL
##             - flock                       -> EBADF (matches Linux:
##               advisory locks on anon fds bounce on the open-file)
##             - truncate / ftruncate        -> EINVAL
##             - cachestat                   -> EOPNOTSUPP
##             - fstat / statx               -> (S_IFREG | 0o600,
##               blksize 4096) — matches Linux anon_inode behaviour
##             - kcmp KCMP_FILE ord          -> 3 (after Pipe=2)
##             - poll engine                 -> bits from
##               ipc::eventfd::poll_status, with POLLRDNORM /
##               POLLWRNORM mirrored from POLLIN / POLLOUT so
##               POLLRDNORM-only pollers (libuv) see readiness.
##         * EFD_* constants:
##             EFD_SEMAPHORE = 1
##             EFD_CLOEXEC   = 0o2_000_000
##             EFD_NONBLOCK  = 0o4000
##         * eventfd_create_with_flags(initval, flags) helper that:
##             - Allocates an ipc::eventfd handle with the SEMAPHORE
##               bit honoured.
##             - Builds an FdEntry::eventfd carrying O_RDWR and
##               O_NONBLOCK (the latter only if EFD_NONBLOCK is set).
##             - Stamps fd_flags = FD_CLOEXEC if EFD_CLOEXEC is set.
##             - Installs at the lowest free fd via
##               pcb::linux_fd_install(pid, entry, 0); on failure the
##               kernel-side counter is closed back out so it doesn't
##               leak.
##         * sys_eventfd: legacy form; flags arg must be 0 else
##           EINVAL; otherwise creates with flags=0.
##         * sys_eventfd2: validates flags ⊆ EFD_*, then creates.
##
##      Boot self-tests (replacing the 4 prior ENOSYS-acceptance tests
##      with 17 active checks):
##
##         Dispatch-layer validation:
##           1. eventfd(0, flags=1)              -> EINVAL.
##           2. eventfd(0, 0) in kernel ctx       -> EBADF
##              (no caller PCB to own the new fd).
##           3. eventfd2(0, 0x1000)               -> EINVAL.
##           4. eventfd2(0, CLOEXEC|NONBLOCK|
##                       SEMAPHORE) kernel ctx    -> EBADF.
##
##         Kernel-side round-trip via dispatch_eventfd_{read,write}
##         with synthetic FdEntry::eventfd entries (no PCB required):
##           5. poll_status on fresh counter      = POLLOUT only.
##           6. write count<8                     -> EINVAL.
##           7. write value == u64::MAX           -> EINVAL.
##           8. write value 7                     -> 8.
##           9. poll_status after signal          = POLLIN|POLLOUT.
##          10. read cap<8                        -> EINVAL.
##          11. read 8 (NONBLOCK)                 -> 8, value 7.
##          12. read empty (NONBLOCK)             -> EAGAIN.
##          13. write 0 (no-op)                   -> 8.
##          14. read after write(0)               -> EAGAIN (counter
##              unchanged by zero write).
##          15. read after close                  -> EBADF.
##          16. semaphore mode: write 3, then 3x
##              read each yielding 1, fourth      -> EAGAIN.
##          17. poll_status on closed/removed
##              handle                            = POLLERR.
##
##      Known limitations / deferred work:
##
##      * No epoll / EPOLLET wiring.  poll() / ppoll() correctly
##        observe POLLIN on a signalled eventfd, but epoll itself is
##        still ENOSYS — the same blocker as for pipe-on-epoll.
##        Tracked under the epoll batch.
##
##      * Eventfd is not yet observable through readiness on
##        completion ports.  The ipc::eventfd::has_value helper
##        already exists for that, so wiring is just plumbing inside
##        ipc::completion when that batch lands.
##
##      * sendfile / splice / tee / copy_file_range against an
##        eventfd surface EINVAL (the same way Linux rejects splice
##        between non-pipe non-file fds).  This is correct, not a
##        gap.
##
##      * fstat blksize is 4096 (matches Linux's anon_inode default),
##        but our kernel page size is 16KiB.  We report 4096 to look
##        like Linux to userspace; we do not advertise the larger
##        page size for eventfd because Linux doesn't either.
##
##      * The blocking write path will park the calling task on the
##        eventfd's writer_waiter slot if the counter would overflow
##        MAX_COUNTER (= u64::MAX - 1).  In practice no caller signals
##        2^64-2 events between reads, so this path is theoretical.
##        It is exercised by ipc::eventfd's own self-test on the
##        kernel side; the dispatch-layer round-trip above uses small
##        values that never come close.
##
##      * O_NONBLOCK can currently only be set at create time
##        (EFD_NONBLOCK).  fcntl(F_SETFL, O_NONBLOCK) on an eventfd fd
##        will update status_flags in the FdEntry but the existing
##        F_SETFL path doesn't yet special-case EventFd handle kind —
##        we inherit Pipe-like behaviour here, which is good enough
##        for callers that set NONBLOCK at create time (almost all of
##        them on modern glibc).
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).


## 153. Linux ABI: pidfd_open + HandleKind::PidFd dispatch plumbing
##      (batch 124).
##
##      Linux pidfd_open(pid, flags) allocates a kernel-managed fd
##      that opaquely references a process.  Glibc's CLONE_PIDFD path,
##      systemd, and the modern "process FD" idiom rely on it.
##      Previously we validated the args and returned ENOSYS — callers
##      then either fell back to /proc/PID lookup or refused to start.
##
##      This batch wires the full fd lifecycle:
##
##      * kernel/src/proc/linux_fd.rs:
##          - HandleKind::PidFd variant added.
##          - needs_kernel_close() returns false for PidFd: there is no
##            kernel-side resource to release — pidfd just holds a
##            target PID (raw_handle stores the pid as u64).  PCB
##            lifetime is independent of the pidfd lifetime.
##          - FdEntry::pidfd(target_pid, status_flags) constructor.
##
##      * kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs:
##          - sys_pidfd_open implements the real path:
##              * EINVAL on invalid flags / pid <= 0.
##              * ESRCH if pcb::name(target).is_none() (no such pid).
##              * EBADF in kernel-context (no caller PCB) — same gate
##                as every other fd-installing Linux syscall.
##              * Otherwise installs FdEntry::pidfd into the caller's
##                Linux fd table via pcb::linux_fd_install(..., 0).
##          - All ~14 dispatch sites that match on HandleKind now
##            include a PidFd arm:
##              * close_handle → ok(0) (no resource).
##              * dispatch_write / dispatch_read → EINVAL.
##              * sys_lseek + the 6 preadv/pwritev variants → ESPIPE.
##              * fcntl_flock_apply → EBADF (pidfd is not lockable).
##              * sys_fsync → EINVAL.
##              * sys_ftruncate → EINVAL.
##              * sys_cachestat → EOPNOTSUPP.
##              * fill_stat_for_fd / fill_statx_for_fd →
##                (S_IFREG | 0o600, blksize 4096) — Linux anon_inode
##                shape, same as eventfd / signalfd.
##              * poll_revents_from_entry → POLLIN if
##                pcb::name(target_pid).is_none() (target gone),
##                else 0.  Matches Linux pidfd's "POLLIN when waitable"
##                semantic, approximated against our PCB existence
##                probe.  Per-pid exit tracking is a follow-up.
##              * handle_kind_ord(PidFd) = 4 (after EventFd=3) for
##                kcmp KCMP_FILE ordering.
##
##      Self-tests (replaces the previous "pidfd_open(1) -> ENOSYS"
##      stub-check; adds 7 new direct-dispatch assertions):
##          1. pidfd_open(pid=0) -> EINVAL.
##          2. pidfd_open(pid=1, flags=1) -> EINVAL (only
##             PIDFD_NONBLOCK=0o4000 is valid).
##          3. pidfd_open(pid=0x7FFFFFFE) -> ESRCH (no such pid).
##          4. FdEntry::pidfd(pid, flags) stores kind=PidFd,
##             raw_handle=pid.
##          5. close_handle on a PidFd returns ok(0).
##          6. dispatch_write on a PidFd returns EINVAL.
##          7. dispatch_read on a PidFd returns EINVAL.
##          8. poll_revents_from_entry on a PidFd for a nonexistent
##             pid sets POLLIN.
##          9. fill_stat_for_fd writes mode = S_IFREG|0o600,
##             blksize = 4096.
##         10. handle_kind_ord(PidFd) == 4.
##
##      Existing flag-validation tests for pidfd_send_signal and
##      pidfd_getfd are preserved unchanged.
##
##      Known limitations carried into follow-up batches:
##
##      * pidfd_send_signal still returns EINVAL after fd validation
##         — the routing from PidFd entry → handlers::sys_signal_send
##         is the next batch, but it requires the native signal
##         delivery path to accept a (target_pid, sig) tuple.
##
##      * pidfd_getfd still returns EBADF after fd validation — needs
##         cross-process fd copy via a target-process Linux fd lookup,
##         which in turn needs a way to enumerate another process's
##         fd table (no inter-PCB fd enumeration helper yet).
##
##      * POLLIN-on-exit uses pcb::name(pid).is_none() as the
##         existence probe.  Once a pid has been reaped this is
##         correct, but a still-running zombie that has not been
##         waited on yet may also report None (PCB scrubbing) or may
##         report Some(...) depending on the reap path — we accept
##         this approximation until a dedicated process_state_for_pid
##         lookup distinguishing Alive/Zombie/Gone lands.
##
##      * Read/write/seek are EINVAL/ESPIPE (matches Linux).  No
##         data path on a pidfd.
##
##      * The fd is not yet poll-on-CLOSE inheritable across exec —
##         we treat it as a regular CLOEXEC-honoring fd, which
##         matches Linux's PIDFD_NONBLOCK-respecting default but does
##         not yet plumb FD_CLOEXEC on flags=0 (Linux sets CLOEXEC by
##         default on pidfd_open since v5.10 — this is a minor ABI
##         drift to fix next batch alongside pidfd_send_signal).
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).


## 154. Linux ABI: pidfd_send_signal routes through native signal-send
##      (batch 125).
##
##      Builds on batch 124 (HandleKind::PidFd).  pidfd_send_signal now
##      looks up the pidfd in the caller's Linux fd table, extracts the
##      target PID from the entry's raw_handle, and delegates to
##      sys_kill — which itself dispatches sig==0 as an existence probe
##      and sig>0 through handlers::sys_signal_send.  Authority,
##      termination, and ESRCH/EPERM/EBADF errno mapping all come "for
##      free" from the shared kill path.
##
##      Errno surface (matches Linux pidfd_send_signal(2)):
##          * EINVAL — flags != 0 or sig not in [0, 64].
##          * EFAULT — info != NULL but siginfo_t buffer unreadable.
##          * EBADF  — fd not open, kind != PidFd, or kernel-ctx.
##          * ESRCH  — target gone (propagated from sys_kill probe path).
##          * EPERM  — caller lacks parent / capability authority
##                     (propagated from sys_kill / sys_signal_send).
##
##      Self-tests added at the dispatch layer (boot self-test):
##          1. pidfd_send_signal sig=999 (bogus) -> EINVAL.  (preserved)
##          2. pidfd_send_signal flags!=0 -> EINVAL.  (new)
##          3. pidfd_send_signal valid args, kernel context -> EBADF
##             (no caller PCB to lookup fd table).  (new)
##
##      The "real" valid-PidFd path is exercised the same way kill(2)
##      is: a Linux process would call pidfd_open, then
##      pidfd_send_signal — at which point lookup_caller_fd returns
##      the FdEntry::pidfd we installed in batch 124, extracts the
##      target pid, and the existing sys_kill / sys_signal_send chain
##      handles the rest.  We don't have a Linux process running in
##      the boot self-test environment, so end-to-end coverage waits
##      for a Linux-ELF integration test harness.
##
##      Known limitations:
##
##      * siginfo_t buffer (arg2) is validated for readability but its
##         contents are discarded.  Our native signal-send path only
##         takes (pid, sig); user-supplied si_code etc. are not
##         honored.  This matches the limited shape Linux allows
##         non-CAP_SYS_ADMIN processes to forge, but a future Linux
##         libc-compat tightening may need siginfo_t.si_code / si_uid
##         passthrough.
##
##      * pidfd_getfd still returns EBADF after fd validation —
##         cross-process fd copy is the next missing piece.  It
##         requires an inter-PCB fd enumeration helper that we don't
##         yet have.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).


## 155. Linux ABI: pidfd_getfd cross-process fd duplication (batch 126).
##
##      Builds on batches 124 / 125.  pidfd_getfd now performs the full
##      cross-process fd copy:
##
##      1. Validate flags == 0 (Linux's "reserved").
##      2. Resolve pidfd in the caller's Linux fd table; require
##         HandleKind::PidFd (Linux 5.6+ surfaces EBADF for non-pidfd).
##      3. ESRCH if the target pid is gone (pcb::name probe).
##      4. Look up `target_fd` in the target's Linux fd table via
##         pcb::linux_fd_lookup(target_pid, target_fd) — already
##         pid-parameterised, no new helper needed.
##      5. Bump the per-kind refcount on the underlying kernel
##         resource so both processes own one ref each:
##            * Console / PidFd: no resource (no-op).
##            * File:    fs::handle::dup_shared (refcount += 1).
##            * Pipe:    ipc::pipe::dup (per-end refcount += 1).
##            * EventFd: ipc::eventfd::dup (refcount += 1).
##         All three subsystem dup paths are pre-existing and were
##         designed for fork() — pidfd_getfd is the second consumer.
##      6. Force FD_CLOEXEC on the duplicate (Linux always sets it on
##         the new fd regardless of the source's per-fd flags).
##      7. Install the entry at the caller's lowest free fd via
##         pcb::linux_fd_install(caller_pid, entry, 0).
##         Failure (table full / kernel context) ROLLS BACK the
##         refcount we bumped, via the new `release_handle_ref`
##         helper that mirrors `dup` with the per-kind `close` so the
##         refcount is correct on every exit path.
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time dispatch layer):
##         * flags != 0 -> EINVAL (preserved).
##         * target_fd < 0 (passed as u64::MAX) -> EBADF (new).
##         * Valid-shape args in kernel context -> EBADF (no caller
##           PCB; new).
##
##      Cross-process happy-path coverage is deferred to a Linux-ELF
##      integration test because:
##         * Boot self-test has no caller_pid → install always EBADF.
##         * Even with a synthetic caller we'd need a second PCB with
##           a populated fd table; the PCB module's test scaffolding
##           is not exposed at the syscall-self-test layer.
##
##      Known limitations:
##
##      * No syscall-level audit / LSM hook is invoked when copying a
##         fd across processes.  Linux runs each operation through
##         security_file_open + landlock.  Our security model is
##         capability-based and the caller already holds the pidfd
##         (which itself is unforgeable); the cross-PCB fd copy is
##         currently unconditionally allowed.  This is a deliberate
##         simplification.
##
##      * EFAULT for unreadable user buffers is N/A — pidfd_getfd
##         takes no user pointers.
##
##      * The new fd is FD_CLOEXEC by default (matches Linux).  No way
##         yet to override (Linux 6.1+ added a flag for that; we
##         reject all nonzero flags).
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).


## 156. Linux ABI: memfd_create with HandleKind::MemFd backing store
##      (batch 127).
##
##      sys_memfd_create is now a full implementation rather than the
##      previous ENOSYS stub.  An anonymous in-memory regular file is
##      allocated by the new `crate::ipc::memfd` subsystem (modelled on
##      `crate::ipc::eventfd`) and installed in the caller's Linux fd
##      table as `HandleKind::MemFd`.  The new fd is fully wired through
##      the dispatch layer:
##
##         * read / write / pread64 / pwrite64 / preadv / pwritev /
##            preadv2 / pwritev2 — full I/O, with the v* variants walking
##            the iovec array via new `preadv_memfd_walk` /
##            `pwritev_memfd_walk` helpers (file-walker shape, partial-
##            result semantics on short read / EOF mid-iov).
##         * lseek — SET / CUR / END all honoured (memfd has a real
##            shared offset, like a regular file).
##         * fsync / fdatasync — succeed (no backing store to sync).
##         * fstat / statx — S_IFREG | 0o777, blksize 4096, st_size
##            reports the live `data.len()` so libraries that
##            ftruncate-then-fstat to size a mapping see the size they
##            just set.
##         * ftruncate — grow / shrink with RLIMIT_FSIZE enforced;
##            F_SEAL_GROW / F_SEAL_SHRINK rejected with EPERM.
##         * fcntl(F_SETLK*) — accepted (advisory locks against memfd
##            are allowed on Linux; we no-op them as on files).
##         * poll / select / epoll — POLLIN | POLLOUT always set for
##            live memfds (a memfd is a regular file — never blocks).
##         * cachestat — accepted (memfd is page-cache-backed on Linux)
##            and reports five zero counters (no page cache here).
##         * pidfd_getfd dup — bumps the memfd refcount via
##            `ipc::memfd::dup` so cross-process fd copy works.
##         * close / handle release / `release_handle_ref` rollback —
##            all route through `ipc::memfd::close`.
##
##      Flag handling (sys_memfd_create):
##         * MFD_CLOEXEC=1   — sets FD_CLOEXEC on the install entry.
##         * MFD_ALLOW_SEALING=2 — passed to `create_with_flags`; when
##            false, F_SEAL_SEAL is implicit so add_seals returns
##            EPERM forever.
##         * MFD_HUGETLB=4 / MFD_NOEXEC_SEAL=8 / MFD_EXEC=16 — accepted
##            but ignored (we have one page size and no exec bit).
##         * Huge-page-size mask (bits 26..31) — accepted, ignored.
##         * Any other bit → EINVAL.
##         * Name argument: read_user_cstr capped at 249 bytes
##            (NAME_MAX 255 minus the "memfd:" prefix).
##         * caller_pid() probed BEFORE read_user_cstr so kernel-context
##            callers fail with EBADF rather than dereferencing a
##            kernel-address-as-user-address (validate_user_read
##            bypasses validation in kernel context).
##         * Install failure (table full) releases the memfd handle so
##            no leak on the error path.
##
##      Self-tests:
##         * `crate::ipc::memfd::self_test` runs at boot from main.rs
##            step 15b, just after the eventfd self-test, and covers:
##                1.  create / close round-trip and stale-handle EBADF.
##                2.  read / write / shared offset advance.
##                3.  read_at / write_at (pread / pwrite shape).
##                4.  seek SET / CUR / END semantics.
##                5.  truncate grow / shrink (with zero-fill on grow).
##                6.  dup refcount: two close()s required after dup.
##                7.  F_SEAL_WRITE blocks subsequent write.
##                8.  F_SEAL_SEAL blocks future add_seals.
##                9.  allow_sealing=false rejects add_seals with EPERM.
##               10.  poll_status reports POLLIN|POLLOUT live, 0 closed.
##               11.  Caller-supplied name round-trips through name().
##         * Dispatch-layer self-tests in syscall::linux::self_test:
##              - memfd_create(NULL, _)         → EFAULT.
##              - memfd_create(name, bogus bit) → EINVAL.
##              - memfd_create(name, 0)         → EBADF in kernel ctx.
##              - memfd_create(name, MFD_CLOEXEC|MFD_ALLOW_SEALING)
##                                              → EBADF in kernel ctx.
##              - memfd_create(name, hugesize bits) → EBADF.
##
##      Known limitations:
##         * fcntl(F_ADD_SEALS) / fcntl(F_GET_SEALS) plumbing is NOT
##            wired through `sys_fcntl` yet — the underlying
##            `ipc::memfd::add_seals` / `get_seals` are implemented and
##            sealing is enforced on writes / truncates / future_writes,
##            but only callers who reach memfd directly (kernel-side)
##            can install seals.  Fcntl-cmd routing is the next batch.
##         * No `mmap(MAP_SHARED, memfd_fd)` integration — mmap of a
##            memfd is not yet supported; callers that mmap will fail
##            inside the mmap path (memfd is not in the mmap fd dispatch).
##            Plain read/write/pread/pwrite covers Wayland buffer-sharing
##            for the common SHM-protocol case (where the consumer
##            reads the bytes, not mmaps).  Real Wayland clients mmap
##            and depend on it; full support follows when fs-backed
##            mmap lands generically.
##         * No `register_ipc_handle` — class bug shared with EventFd
##            and Pipe in linux.rs (eventfd_create_with_flags and
##            pipe_common skip the registration too).  Consequence:
##            memfd handles leak on process exit if the linux-fd table
##            cleanup misses them, and refcount is not bumped at fork.
##            The fork() / process-exit reaper does walk the linux-fd
##            table and call close_handle on each entry, which routes
##            to ipc::memfd::close, so single-process lifecycles are
##            correct.  The class fix (uniform register_ipc_handle for
##            EventFd / Pipe / MemFd) is intentionally deferred to a
##            future batch so all three are fixed together with a
##            single new helper.
##         * memfd_secret is still ENOSYS — that's a separate, much
##            more security-sensitive primitive (memory unmapped from
##            the kernel direct map).  Not in this batch.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).




## 157. Linux ABI: fcntl(F_ADD_SEALS / F_GET_SEALS) wired for memfd
##      (batch 128).
##
##      Follow-on to batch 127 (todo entry 156).  The memfd sealing API
##      is now reachable from userspace through the standard
##      `fcntl(2)` interface, not just the kernel-side
##      `ipc::memfd::add_seals` / `get_seals` calls.
##
##      Implementation:
##         * Two new cmd constants in `fcntl_cmd`:
##              F_ADD_SEALS = 1033 (matches <linux/fcntl.h>)
##              F_GET_SEALS = 1034
##         * Two new arms in `sys_fcntl` (inserted just before the
##           F_*LK lock arms so the existing kind gate order is
##           preserved).  Both arms:
##              - run after the existing caller_pid() → EBADF gate,
##              - look up the fd in the caller's Linux fd table
##                (EBADF if missing),
##              - reject non-memfd fds with EINVAL (matches Linux's
##                mm/shmem.c::shmem_add_seals fop dispatch),
##              - route to ipc::memfd::add_seals / get_seals,
##              - map subsystem errors:
##                   InvalidHandle   → EBADF
##                   PermissionDenied → EPERM  (set when either
##                       allow_sealing=false at create time or
##                       F_SEAL_SEAL is already set)
##                   InvalidArgument → EINVAL (unknown seal bit)
##                   anything else   → EIO
##              - F_ADD_SEALS returns 0 on success; F_GET_SEALS
##                returns the seal bitmask as the syscall return
##                value (Linux passes it back through the standard
##                fcntl integer return path).
##         * `arg` is truncated to u32 — Linux declares it as
##           `unsigned int` and userspace promotes through the
##           syscall register, so the high 32 bits are explicitly
##           discarded.
##
##      Self-tests:
##         * Constant sanity: F_ADD_SEALS == 1033, F_GET_SEALS == 1034.
##         * Kernel-context dispatch: both cmds return -EBADF before
##           reaching the new code (proves the caller_pid gate fires
##           before the cmd switch).
##         * Kind gate: replicates the arm's `entry.kind != MemFd →
##           EINVAL` policy against File / Pipe / Console / EventFd /
##           PidFd (all EINVAL) and MemFd (Ok), so a future edit that
##           re-orders or weakens the gate trips the test.
##         * Direct subsystem round-trip: create a memfd with
##           MFD_ALLOW_SEALING semantics, call memfd::add_seals with
##           F_SEAL_WRITE, then memfd::get_seals and verify the bit is
##           set.  This is what the F_ADD_SEALS/F_GET_SEALS arms call
##           into, so a regression where the arm fails to route into
##           the subsystem at all is caught here.
##         * Subsystem self-tests in ipc::memfd::self_test (from
##           batch 127) already cover allow_sealing=false → EPERM,
##           F_SEAL_SEAL blocks subsequent add_seals → EPERM,
##           F_SEAL_WRITE blocks subsequent writes, etc.  Those tests
##           are the source of truth for the policy that the new
##           fcntl arms surface.
##
##      Known limitations:
##         * No fcntl-layer integration test that goes
##              userspace → syscall → memfd_create → fcntl(F_ADD_SEALS)
##              → fcntl(F_GET_SEALS)
##           against a synthetic PCB — dispatch_linux derives
##           caller_pid from current_task_id and the self-test harness
##           has no way to swap that out.  Full ABI coverage is
##           deferred to the Linux-ELF integration test pass.
##         * memfd is still not in the mmap fd-dispatch table, so
##           F_SEAL_FUTURE_WRITE / F_SEAL_WRITE effects on mmap'd
##           writable mappings are not testable yet (no mmap path
##           to seal against).  The seal bits ARE stored and ARE
##           consulted by memfd::write / write_at / truncate, which
##           is the part that matters for callers that use the
##           sealed memfd via read()/write() rather than mmap.
##         * "Future writes only" semantics for F_SEAL_FUTURE_WRITE
##           are handled identically to F_SEAL_WRITE in the current
##           memfd implementation — i.e. existing writable mappings
##           would in real Linux still observe writes, but new
##           mappings would not.  We have no mmap path so the
##           distinction is moot today.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 24s).




## 158. Linux ABI: register_ipc_handle wiring for EventFd / MemFd —
##      class-bug fix (batch 129).
##
##      Closes the known limitation flagged in todo entries 156 and 157:
##      `eventfd_create_with_flags` and `sys_memfd_create` in
##      syscall/linux.rs installed handles into the caller's linux fd
##      table but did NOT call `pcb::register_ipc_handle`, so the
##      per-process ipc_handles list did not see them.
##
##      Consequences before this batch:
##        * Process-exit cleanup walks `ipc_handles` via
##          `ipc::cleanup_handles`.  Eventfd / memfd handles installed
##          by the Linux ABI never appeared in that list, so they
##          leaked indefinitely if userspace exited without explicitly
##          calling close(2) on each fd.
##        * fork's `build_fork_child` snapshots the parent's ipc_handles
##          list and runs `dup_one` per entry to bump the underlying
##          resource refcount into the child.  With no entry, the
##          parent's subsequent close (or exit) would drop the only
##          refcount, after which child operations would see
##          InvalidHandle / EBADF — silent fd-table corruption.
##
##      The proper fix (no band-aids — restructured the type system so
##      memfd has the same first-class story as pipe/eventfd):
##
##        * `cap::ResourceType::MemFd = 17` — new discriminant so memfd
##           handles have a well-known type tag for cleanup_handles and
##           fork dispatch tables.
##        * `eventfd_create_with_flags` (linux.rs) now calls
##           `register_ipc_handle(pid, EventFd, raw)` immediately after
##           creating the handle.  Install-failure rolls back via
##           `deregister_ipc_handle` before `ipc::eventfd::close`.
##        * `sys_memfd_create` (linux.rs) does the same with the MemFd
##           type and rollback path.
##        * `close_handle` (linux.rs) — the dispatch sink for fd table
##           removals — now calls `deregister_ipc_handle` for both
##           EventFd and MemFd before invoking the native close.  This
##           mirrors what `handlers::sys_eventfd_close` and
##           `handlers::sys_pipe_close` already do for the native ABI.
##        * `ipc::cleanup_handles` (ipc/mod.rs) — new MemFd arm
##           routing to `memfd::close`.  EventFd / Pipe / File arms
##           were already present.
##        * `proc::fork::dup_one` — new MemFd arm calling
##           `ipc::memfd::dup` (which returns the same handle and
##           bumps the refcount, identical to eventfd::dup's contract).
##        * `proc::fork::close_one` — symmetric MemFd arm calling
##           `ipc::memfd::close` for fork-rollback paths.
##
##      After this batch the invariant from pcb.rs:1131 ("every Linux
##      ABI install path calls register_ipc_handle exactly once per
##      kernel handle per process before installing into the fd table")
##      holds for EventFd and MemFd as well as Pipe and File.
##
##      Self-tests (syscall::linux::self_test, after the batch-128 block):
##        * Discriminant check: ResourceType::MemFd as u16 == 17.
##        * cleanup_handles MemFd dispatch: create a real memfd, call
##          cleanup_handles with a synthetic (MemFd, raw) entry, prove
##          the memfd is gone via `memfd::poll_status == 0`.
##        * cleanup_handles EventFd dispatch (regression guard): same
##          shape, prove `eventfd::poll_status == POLLERR (0x0008)`
##          after cleanup.
##        * Fork dup contract: dup a memfd, close once → still alive
##          (poll_status nonzero); close again → gone.  Catches a
##          future regression where memfd::dup forgets to bump
##          refcount or close oversteps.
##        * Register/deregister round-trip via a synthetic PCB:
