##          register a fabricated MemFd entry, snapshot ipc_handles,
##          verify presence, deregister, verify absence.  Catches
##          tuple-keying regressions in deregister_ipc_handle.
##
##      Known limitations / follow-ups:
##        * `register_ipc_handle` runs unconditionally — it does not
##          check if the caller already has an entry for the same
##          (kind, raw).  That's correct for the create paths covered
##          here (each create mints a fresh handle), but a future
##          patch that calls `register_ipc_handle` from a duplicating
##          path (e.g. a future `socketpair`-equivalent that returns
##          two refcounted ends sharing one entry) must take care to
##          register exactly the right number of entries to match the
##          refcount bump pattern.  The pipe install path already
##          documents this discipline (handlers::sys_pipe_create
##          registers BOTH ends because pipe::create returns two
##          handles with distinct refcounts).
##        * pidfd is intentionally NOT registered because it has no
##          per-fork dup_one path (it carries a pid value, not a
##          refcounted kernel resource).  Process exit cleanup just
##          drops the pid value with the fd table.
##        * The fix does not retroactively close memfd / eventfd
##          handles that were leaked in earlier kernels.  Reboot
##          flushes everything; we don't carry persistent state
##          across boots.
##        * memfd_secret remains ENOSYS as in batch 127.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).




## 159. Linux ABI: register_ipc_handle for sys_pidfd_getfd duplicated
##      handles — class-bug fix (batch 130).
##
##      Same shape of class bug as batch 129 (entry 158), applied to
##      the cross-process fd-duplication path.  Before this batch
##      `sys_pidfd_getfd` performed the per-kind dup
##      (`fs::handle::dup_shared`, `ipc::pipe::dup`,
##      `ipc::eventfd::dup`, `ipc::memfd::dup`) and installed the
##      duplicated FdEntry in the *caller's* linux_fd_table, but it
##      did NOT call `pcb::register_ipc_handle` to add the caller's
##      reference to its `ipc_handles` list.
##
##      Consequences before this batch:
##        * Process-exit cleanup walks `ipc_handles` via
##          `ipc::cleanup_handles`.  Handles obtained through
##          `pidfd_getfd` never appeared in that list, so the
##          per-process reference leaked: the underlying resource's
##          refcount stayed elevated until reboot.
##        * Fork's `build_fork_child` snapshots ipc_handles and
##          calls `dup_one` per entry to refcount-bump into the
##          child.  Without an entry, the caller's fork-inherited fd
##          had a dangling reference: parent's subsequent close (or
##          exit) would drop the only refcount and child operations
##          would see InvalidHandle / EBADF.
##
##      The fix (analogous to batches 127–129):
##        * Between the per-kind dup and `linux_fd_install`,
##          register the duplicated handle in the caller's
##          ipc_handles list under the matching ResourceType.
##          File / Pipe / EventFd / MemFd each have a kernel-side
##          refcount; Console and PidFd have no kernel resource and
##          are intentionally skipped (matches the per-kind dup arm
##          and `release_handle_ref`'s no-op cases).
##        * Install-failure rollback now also deregisters before
##          calling `release_handle_ref`.  The `release_handle_ref`
##          path for File routes through `handlers::sys_fs_close`
##          which itself deregisters; that second deregister is a
##          safe no-op because `deregister_ipc_handle` swap_removes
##          on the first matching tuple and finds nothing on the
##          second call.
##
##      After this batch every Linux-ABI install path that adds a
##      refcounted reference to the caller's process now also
##      registers an `ipc_handles` entry.  The remaining install
##      sites in syscall::linux are:
##        * `open_common` / `open_kernel_path_install` — register via
##          `handlers::sys_fs_open` (already correct pre-batch).
##        * `pipe_common` — registers via
##          `handlers::sys_pipe_create` (already correct pre-batch).
##        * `eventfd_create_with_flags` — fixed in batch 129.
##        * `sys_memfd_create` — fixed in batch 129.
##        * `sys_pidfd_open` — pidfd has no kernel resource, intentionally
##          unregistered (it carries a pid value, not a refcounted
##          handle).
##        * `sys_pidfd_getfd` — fixed in this batch.
##
##      Self-tests (syscall::linux::self_test, after the batch-129 block):
##        * Kind→ResourceType mapping table: assert each HandleKind
##          maps to the expected ResourceType (or None).  Catches a
##          future edit where a new HandleKind variant is added
##          without extending the new match.
##        * Per-resource register/deregister round-trip on a
##          synthetic PCB.  Walks {File, Pipe, EventFd, MemFd}, each
##          with a fabricated handle id.  Verifies snapshot reflects
##          presence after register and absence after deregister.
##          Catches tuple-keying regressions and ResourceType discriminant
##          conflicts.
##        * Kernel-context dispatch: `pidfd_getfd(0, 0, 0)` returns
##          -EBADF.  Guards against the new register call being
##          accidentally moved above the caller_pid() check (which
##          would mint phantom ipc_handles entries for kernel-context
##          callers).
##
##      Known limitations / follow-ups:
##        * No integration test for the full userspace round-trip
##          (caller A opens fd; caller B pidfd_open(A) + pidfd_getfd;
##          A closes its fd; verify B can still operate).  Same
##          limitation as batches 127–129: the boot self-test harness
##          can't synthesize two distinct PCBs both reachable from
##          dispatch_linux.  Deferred to the Linux-ELF integration
##          test pass.
##        * `release_handle_ref` could be deduplicated with
##          `close_handle` (both close a per-kind handle, both have
##          the same set of arms).  Left for a future refactor — the
##          two callers have slightly different rollback semantics
##          (release_handle_ref doesn't deregister; close_handle now
##          does) and merging them would require new flags.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).

## 160. Linux ABI: futex_waitv(2) nr=1 fast path (batch 131).
##
##      Status: DONE.
##
##      Pre-batch shape: `sys_futex_waitv` (linux.rs:15737) validated
##      every argument shape Linux requires — nr range, flags==0,
##      user-pointer readability for the waitv array, timespec
##      readability when non-NULL, clockid in [0, 1] — and then
##      always returned ENOSYS.  This is correct in the literal sense
##      (we don't multiplex on multiple futex keys), but the
##      overwhelmingly common caller pattern is `nr == 1`: glibc /
##      musl / Rust std use futex_waitv as a "wait with one of
##      CLOCK_REALTIME or CLOCK_MONOTONIC selectable" primitive on a
##      single futex word, since the legacy SYS_FUTEX FUTEX_WAIT op
##      ties you to CLOCK_MONOTONIC.  Falling through to ENOSYS in
##      that case prevented these runtimes from ever making forward
##      progress and forced a fallback to the legacy futex API (or
##      worse, a hard syscall error).
##
##      Fix: implement the nr=1 case as a single FUTEX_WAIT_BITSET-
##      style wait on the one entry, using the same handler routing
##      and timeout semantics as `sys_futex`'s FUTEX_WAIT_BITSET arm
##      (linux.rs:19010).  The implementation:
##
##        1. Reads the single `struct futex_waitv` (24 bytes) from
##           userspace via `copy_from_user`.  The validate_user_read
##           call upstream of the dispatch already proved the range
##           readable; copy_from_user re-validates under SMAP.
##        2. Rejects non-zero `__reserved` with EINVAL (matches Linux).
##        3. Validates per-entry flags:
##             - FUTEX2_SIZE_MASK = 0x03; only FUTEX2_SIZE_U32 (=2)
##               is implemented.  U8/U16/U64 sizes return EINVAL.
##             - FUTEX2_NUMA (0x04) is rejected (we don't expose NUMA
##               keys).
##             - FUTEX2_PRIVATE (0x80) is accepted as a no-op hint
##               (all our futexes are process-private; we don't
##               support cross-process named futexes yet).
##             - Any other bit -> EINVAL (matches Linux's FUTEX2_MASK
##               gate).
##        4. SIZE_U32 means the futex word is 32 bits, so only the
##           low 32 bits of the 64-bit `val` field participate in the
##           value compare (cast `entry.val as u32`).
##        5. If `args.arg3 == 0` (no timespec), dispatches to
##           `handlers::sys_futex_wait`.  Otherwise reads the absolute
##           timespec on the selected clock, computes
##           `rel_ns = abs_ns.saturating_sub(now_ns)`, and dispatches
##           to `handlers::sys_futex_wait_timeout`.  This mirrors the
##           FUTEX_WAIT_BITSET pattern exactly so the underlying
##           futex behavior stays identical.
##        6. Maps the native return into the Linux futex_waitv
##           contract:
##             - Ok(1) → SyscallResult::ok(0)  (index of woken waiter;
##               for nr=1 that's always 0).
##             - Ok(0) → -EAGAIN  (value mismatch, per Linux).
##             - Native error → mapped via linux_from_native
##               (TimedOut → -ETIMEDOUT, InvalidAddress → -EFAULT,
##               etc.).
##
##      For `nr > 1` the implementation still returns ENOSYS — we
##      need a futex waitqueue that can park on N keys simultaneously
##      and atomically unpark from any one of them.  Our futex
##      subsystem (`crate::futex`) only supports one key per parker.
##      Multi-key wait is tracked here as a known limitation.
##
##      Self-tests (syscall::linux::self_test, in the existing
##      futex_waitv block circa line 33227):
##
##        Updated existing test: the old "nr=1 valid -> ENOSYS"
##        assertion (against an all-zero waitv buffer) was wrong
##        post-fix.  An all-zero waitv has `flags=0` which decodes as
##        FUTEX2_SIZE_U8 — unsupported — so the new expected result
##        is EINVAL.  Updated accordingly.
##
##        New: `nr=2` against the same all-zero waitv -> ENOSYS.
##        Verifies the multi-key path still returns the honest
##        unsupported answer.
##
##        New: six nr=1 fast-path gates using stack-allocated
##        `WaitvEntry` structs:
##          (a) flags=1 (SIZE_U16) -> EINVAL
##          (b) flags=2, __reserved=1 -> EINVAL
##          (c) flags=2|0x40 (unknown bit) -> EINVAL
##          (d) flags=2|0x04 (NUMA) -> EINVAL
##          (e) flags=2 (SIZE_U32), uaddr=0 -> EFAULT
##              (validates that dispatch reaches
##              handlers::sys_futex_wait and the page-validation gate
##              fires)
##          (f) flags=2|0x80 (SIZE_U32 | PRIVATE), uaddr=0 -> EFAULT
##              (validates PRIVATE is accepted as a no-op hint and
##              the dispatch still reaches the handler)
##
##      Known limitations / follow-ups:
##        * Multi-key wait (nr > 1) still returns ENOSYS.  Adding
##          true multi-key wait requires extending `crate::futex` so
##          a single parker can be queued on multiple keys with
##          atomic unpark-from-any.  This is the same waitqueue
##          design problem Linux solved with the futex2 series in
##          5.16; we'd port the same pattern when there's a concrete
##          caller demanding it.
##        * The `clockid` field of futex_waitv selects CLOCK_REALTIME
##          (0) or CLOCK_MONOTONIC (1) for the *timeout* clock, but
##          our underlying `handlers::sys_futex_wait_timeout` takes a
##          relative nanosecond delta.  We compute the relative delta
##          ourselves via saturating_sub against the current clock
##          reading.  A more faithful implementation would carry the
##          absolute deadline + clockid into the futex parker and
##          re-check on spurious wake, but the saturating_sub pattern
##          matches what FUTEX_WAIT_BITSET already does and is good
##          enough for all real-world callers we've observed.
##        * The `val` field is 64-bit in the futex_waitv ABI but the
##          futex word is 32-bit when SIZE_U32 is in effect.  We
##          truncate `val` to u32 for the compare.  Linux does the
##          same — the upper 32 bits are unused for SIZE_U32 entries.
##          When other size classes are eventually implemented, the
##          dispatch will need per-size compare helpers.
##        * The boot-test harness can't exercise the actual block /
##          wake path because there's no scheduler context in the
##          self-test (handlers::sys_futex_wait would deadlock
##          waiting for a wake that can never arrive).  Coverage is
##          limited to the validation gates and the dispatch-reaches-
##          handlers proof via EFAULT on uaddr=0.  Full block/wake
##          coverage is deferred to the Linux-ELF integration test
##          pass.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).

## 161. Linux ABI: io_uring_enter(2) / io_uring_register(2) input
##      validation (batch 132).
##
##      Issue:  Both syscalls were bare ENOSYS stubs with zero input
##              validation.  Two problems: (1) inconsistent with the
##              rest of this file's hygiene policy (every other
##              syscall validates inputs before deciding the truthful
##              errno); (2) it hides "no io_uring rings exist" from
##              feature-detection libraries.  Linux's actual answer
##              for an io_uring op on a non-ring fd is EBADF (the fd
##              does exist, it just isn't a ring); we should match
##              that to distinguish "ring file not supported" from
##              "kernel doesn't know this syscall."
##
##      Fix:    Replace the ENOSYS stubs with proper Linux 6.10-style
##              validation:
##
##              sys_io_uring_enter(fd, to_submit, min_complete,
##                                 flags, sig, sigsz):
##                * Flag bits outside 0x7F (GETEVENTS|SQ_WAKEUP|
##                  SQ_WAIT|EXT_ARG|REGISTERED_RING|ABS_TIMER|
##                  EXT_ARG_REG) -> EINVAL.
##                * sig != NULL: validate the pointer for 24 bytes
##                  when EXT_ARG set (struct io_uring_getevents_arg),
##                  8 bytes otherwise (sigset_t).  Faulting -> EFAULT.
##                * EXT_ARG clear + sig != NULL: require sigsz == 8.
##                  Mismatch -> EINVAL.
##                * EXT_ARG set without GETEVENTS -> EINVAL.
##                * Fall through to validate_linux_fd then EBADF.
##
##              sys_io_uring_register(fd, opcode, arg, nr_args):
##                * Strip USE_REGISTERED_RING (1<<31) from opcode
##                  before range-checking.
##                * Opcode > 31 -> EINVAL (forward-compat buffer over
##                  Linux 6.10's 0..=22).
##                * nr_args > 0 and arg != NULL: validate arg for 1
##                  byte (per-opcode sizes vary).
##                * nr_args > 0 and arg == NULL -> EFAULT.
##                * Fall through to validate_linux_fd then EBADF.
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        (a) io_uring_enter(fd=0, flags=0, sig=NULL) -> EBADF.
##        (b) io_uring_enter(flags=0x80) -> EINVAL (reserved bit).
##        (c) io_uring_enter(sig != NULL, EXT_ARG clear, sigsz=7) ->
##            EINVAL.
##        (d) io_uring_enter(EXT_ARG, !GETEVENTS, ptr-valid 24-byte
##            extarg) -> EINVAL.
##        (e) io_uring_enter(EXT_ARG|GETEVENTS, sig=NULL) -> EBADF.
##        (g) io_uring_register(fd=0, opcode=0, arg=NULL, nr_args=0)
##            -> EBADF.
##        (h) io_uring_register(opcode=64) -> EINVAL.
##        (i) io_uring_register(opcode=(1<<31)|2) -> EBADF (proves the
##            USE_REGISTERED_RING bit is masked off before range check).
##        (j) io_uring_register(opcode=0, arg=NULL, nr_args=4) -> EFAULT.
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * `validate_user_read` is permissive in kernel context
##          (`is_kernel_context()` short-circuits to Ok), so the
##          "bogus non-NULL pointer -> EFAULT" gates for both sig
##          (io_uring_enter) and arg (io_uring_register) can't be
##          exercised from a boot-time self-test.  Userspace callers
##          will still see those checks fire when the pointer is
##          genuinely unmapped.
##        * Returning EBADF for *every* fd is correct only as long as
##          no io_uring rings exist.  When sys_io_uring_setup is
##          implemented, both routines need a HandleKind::IoUring
##          dispatch arm before the EBADF fallback.
##        * The opcode cap of 31 is a soft forward-compat buffer,
##          not a Linux contract.  When new opcodes are added in
##          Linux 6.11+, raise the cap rather than turning it into an
##          allowlist — Linux returns EOPNOTSUPP from the dispatch
##          layer for unknown opcodes, not EINVAL.  Match more
##          precisely when there's a real implementation behind these
##          calls.
##        * EXT_ARG_REG (bit 6, Linux 6.10+) is accepted by the flag
##          mask but not interpreted — it implies a registered-ring
##          extended-arg lookup which needs the registered-ring table
##          to exist first.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   io_uring_enter/register
##      validation: OK".

## 162. Linux ABI: process_mrelease(2) / process_madvise(2) pidfd-aware
##      discrimination (batch 133).
##
##      Issue:  Both syscalls take a pidfd as their first argument.
##              Pre-batch:
##                * process_mrelease: validated flags then returned ENOSYS
##                  unconditionally, ignoring the fd entirely.
##                * process_madvise: validated iov/iovcnt/advice/flags
##                  then `validate_linux_fd(fd)` then returned EBADF
##                  unconditionally, with a comment claiming "no real
##                  pidfd exists in our kernel."
##              That comment became stale in batch 124 when pidfd_open
##              landed `HandleKind::PidFd` in real callers' fd tables.
##              Today a userspace program that opens a pidfd and tries
##              process_madvise on it gets EBADF — the same answer it
##              would get for a non-pidfd — making it impossible to
##              distinguish "kernel doesn't implement the syscall"
##              (ENOSYS) from "you passed the wrong kind of fd" (EBADF).
##              process_mrelease was even worse: it returned ENOSYS for
##              a bogus fd, hiding the descriptor error.
##
##      Fix:    Match Linux's `pidfd_get_pid` discrimination after fd
##              validation:
##                * Bad fd or non-pidfd kind -> EBADF (Linux returns
##                  -EBADF for both cases from inside pidfd_get_pid).
##                * Real pidfd -> ENOSYS, since neither syscall has a
##                  real reclaim/advice path in this kernel yet.
##              Implementation reuses the existing `lookup_caller_fd`
##              helper (kernel-context callers see EBADF directly, same
##              as userspace passing a non-pidfd — exactly the desired
##              behaviour).
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * process_mrelease(fd=0, flags=1) -> EINVAL (unchanged).
##        * process_mrelease(fd=0, flags=0) -> EBADF (was ENOSYS; new
##          discrimination correctly reports the non-pidfd error from
##          kernel-context lookup, matching what userspace would see
##          for a non-pidfd fd).
##        * process_madvise(fd=0, advice=0, flags=0) -> EBADF
##          (unchanged: kernel-context lookup still returns EBADF
##          before reaching the ENOSYS path).
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * The "valid pidfd -> ENOSYS" branch cannot be exercised in a
##          boot-time self-test because there is no caller PCB to hang
##          a real pidfd off of.  Userspace callers holding a real
##          pidfd from pidfd_open(2) will see ENOSYS.  Logical
##          correctness is verified by code review against
##          sys_pidfd_send_signal which uses the same pattern.
##        * process_mrelease's actual semantics — drop the target
##          task's anonymous memory after OOM-kill — require integration
##          with the reclaim path.  process_madvise's semantics — apply
##          MADV_* to another task's address space — require a
##          cross-process VMA walk.  Both are non-trivial; ENOSYS lets
##          systemd-oomd / earlyoom / glibc fall back to their pre-5.15
##          paths.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   process_mrelease pidfd
##      discrimination: OK".

## 163. Linux ABI: waitid(P_PIDFD) pidfd validation (batch 134).
##
##      Issue:  `waitid(idtype, id, infop, options, rusage)` validates
##              idtype against {0,1,2,3} but treats `id` as an opaque
##              u64 regardless of idtype.  When idtype == P_PIDFD (3),
##              Linux's actual implementation looks up `id` as a fd via
##              pidfd_get_pid() and returns -EBADF if it isn't a real
##              pidfd or doesn't exist.  Without that check, our code
##              silently accepts any u64 in `id` and rolls through to
##              the ECHILD/WNOHANG response — wrong for posix_spawn,
##              glibc's posix_spawn_pidfd, and any other pidfd-based
##              reaper.
##
##      Fix:    When idtype == 3, validate `id` as a Linux fd via the
##              shared validate_linux_fd + lookup_caller_fd helpers, and
##              require HandleKind::PidFd.  Bad fd or wrong kind ->
##              EBADF (matches Linux's pidfd_get_pid).  Real pidfd ->
##              fall through to the same WNOHANG/ECHILD handling as the
##              other idtype values (we still don't track per-process
##              state changes, but at least the fd-shape error is
##              correct).
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * waitid(idtype=3, id=0, WEXITED) -> EBADF.  Kernel-context
##          lookup_caller_fd returns EBADF directly, exactly matching
##          what userspace would see for a non-pidfd fd.
##        * Existing waitid tests (bad idtype, no event class, bad
##          options, WNOHANG -> 0, blocking -> ECHILD) all still pass
##          unchanged.
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * "Valid pidfd -> ECHILD/WNOHANG-0" cannot be exercised in a
##          boot-time self-test (no caller PCB to hang a real pidfd
##          off).  Userspace callers holding a real pidfd from
##          pidfd_open will get the same answer as the existing
##          idtype<3 paths.
##        * Real child-state-change tracking is still missing — when a
##          child actually exits, waitid should fill the siginfo_t and
##          return the pid, not 0/ECHILD.  That's a bigger refactor
##          tied to the wait4 reaping path.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED on re-run (BOOT_OK at 23s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   waitid P_PIDFD validation:
##      OK".
##
##      Bug noticed during this batch:
##        * Intermittent boot-test hang in [rcu]'s "Callback
##          registration" self-test (the run before BOOT_OK timed out
##          at 600s with serial logs frozen mid-rcu test).  Re-run
##          completed normally in 23s, so this is a pre-existing
##          flake unrelated to batch 134.  Likely a race in
##          rcu::self_test's callback path — investigate when the
##          rcu/kernel-core zone is touched next.  See serial log
##          showing the last successful line was "Callback
##          registration: OK" then no further output.

## 164. Linux ABI: signalfd(2) / signalfd4(2) fd-shape validation
##      (batch 135).
##
##      Issue:  Both syscalls validate the sigsetsize, the mask
##              pointer, and (signalfd4) the flags, then return
##              ENOSYS regardless of `fd`.  But `fd` has Linux-
##              specific semantics: `-1` means "create a new
##              signalfd", `>= 0` means "modify the mask on an
##              existing signalfd."  Linux returns EINVAL when the
##              fd is present but not a signalfd (kernel/signalfd.c:
##              do_signalfd4 calls fdget + checks file->f_op ==
##              signalfd_fops, returns -EINVAL otherwise).  We were
##              accepting "update the mask on fd 5" and silently
##              returning ENOSYS, hiding the descriptor-shape error
##              from glibc / libsystemd / qemu signal handling.
##
##      Fix:    Add an fd-shape gate after the mask validation:
##                * fd == -1: fall through to ENOSYS (create not
##                  implemented).
##                * fd != -1: validate_linux_fd; if missing, EBADF;
##                  if present, EINVAL (no HandleKind::SignalFd
##                  exists in this kernel, so any present fd is by
##                  definition the wrong kind).
##              Identical change to both signalfd and signalfd4.
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * signalfd(fd=-1, mask=ok) -> ENOSYS (was unchanged path).
##        * signalfd(fd=0, mask=ok) -> EINVAL (new — stdin is a
##          Console handle, not a signalfd, so the kind check fires).
##        * signalfd4(fd=-1, flag=SFD_NONBLOCK) -> ENOSYS.
##        * signalfd4(fd=0, mask=ok) -> EINVAL.
##        * signalfd4(bogus flag bit) -> EINVAL (unchanged).
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * Real signalfd functionality is still unimplemented — the
##          ENOSYS path on fd=-1 returns the same answer it did
##          pre-batch.  When HandleKind::SignalFd lands, the kind
##          check needs a `kind == SignalFd` allowlist on the >=0
##          path to permit mask updates and a real create routine on
##          the -1 path.
##        * sigsetsize is checked against 8 (sizeof(sigset_t) on
##          x86_64) — Linux 32-bit compat code accepts 4 too, which
##          we don't support yet (Linux ABI here is x86_64-only).
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   signalfd/signalfd4 fd
##      discrimination: OK".

## 165. Linux ABI: perf_event_open(2) attr/flag/pid-cpu validation
##      (batch 136).
##
##      Issue:  perf_event_open returned ENOSYS after only checking
##              that `attr` was non-NULL and readable for 8 bytes.  A
##              probe that wanted to feature-test the syscall would
##              get the same answer (ENOSYS) for "kernel doesn't have
##              perf" as for "I just passed a malformed attr."  Linux
##              checks several gates *before* the ENOSYS-equivalent
##              (which is actually a per-cpu-state lookup), and
##              userspace (libbpf, glibc, `perf record`) walks the
##              attr_size up to negotiate the highest version both
##              sides support — see Linux 6.x's PERF_ATTR_SIZE_VER0..7
##              compatibility table.  Without our gates that
##              negotiation incorrectly returns ENOSYS instead of the
##              expected EINVAL / E2BIG.
##
##      Fix:    Match Linux's pre-dispatch validation:
##                * attr != NULL and at least the 8-byte header (type
##                  + size) readable -> EFAULT otherwise (kept).
##                * Read the size field (offset 4, u32), validate:
##                  - size < PERF_ATTR_SIZE_VER0 (64) -> EINVAL.
##                  - size > LINUX_PAGE_SIZE (4096) -> E2BIG.
##                  - Re-validate the full `size` bytes are readable.
##                * flags must be a subset of PERF_FLAG_FD_NO_GROUP |
##                  FD_OUTPUT | PID_CGROUP | FD_CLOEXEC (0x0F) ->
##                  EINVAL otherwise.
##                * pid == -1 && cpu == -1 -> EINVAL (Linux explicitly
##                  rejects this combination — no task to attach the
##                  event to and no per-cpu counter to allocate).
##                * After all gates pass -> ENOSYS.
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * perf_event_open(NULL,...) -> EFAULT (unchanged).
##        * perf_event_open(size=0 attr) -> EINVAL.
##        * perf_event_open(size=8192 attr) -> E2BIG.
##        * perf_event_open(valid attr, flags=0x10) -> EINVAL.
##        * perf_event_open(valid attr, pid=-1, cpu=-1) -> EINVAL.
##        * perf_event_open(valid attr, pid=0, cpu=0) -> ENOSYS.
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * Linux's PAGE_SIZE for x86_64 is 4096, while we use 16 KiB
##          internally.  We pick the Linux x86_64 bound (4096) for
##          the E2BIG gate to keep userspace probes that walk
##          attr_size up to PAGE_SIZE-1 working.  A probe that
##          specifically probes 4097-byte attrs to detect 16 KiB pages
##          would see E2BIG even though our page size is larger; this
##          is acceptable since perf doesn't expose page size in any
##          probed way.
##        * We don't validate the attr.config / sample_type / read_format
##          fields — those are perf-internal and rejected only by the
##          real dispatcher.  When perf lands here, the dispatcher
##          will run after these gates and the existing tests need to
##          continue to hold.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   perf_event_open validation
##      gates: OK".

## 166. Linux ABI: bpf(2) cmd/size validation hygiene
##      (Batch 137, 2026-06-05)
##
##      Issue:  sys_bpf only validated (size==0 -> EINVAL) and (attr
##              NULL+nonzero size -> EFAULT) before falling through to
##              ENOSYS.  Userspace probes that pass a wildly-out-of-
##              range cmd, or an oversized attr struct, expected EINVAL/
##              E2BIG (matching Linux's __sys_bpf which range-checks
##              cmd against __MAX_BPF_CMD and caps size at PAGE_SIZE).
##              Our handler instead reported ENOSYS, which made probes
##              think the syscall was missing.
##
##      Fix:    Added an early-gate ladder in sys_bpf:
##                * cmd as i32: cmd < 0 || cmd > 64 -> EINVAL
##                  (forward-compat cap: Linux 6.10's __MAX_BPF_CMD is
##                  ~36; we leave headroom to 64 so future cmds don't
##                  hit EINVAL here while still gating clearly bogus
##                  values like 0x10000+).
##                * size == 0 -> EINVAL (preserved).
##                * size > 4096 -> E2BIG (Linux x86_64 PAGE_SIZE).
##                * attr == NULL -> EFAULT (preserved).
##                * validate_user_read(attr, size) -> EFAULT on bad
##                  range.
##                * After all gates pass -> ENOSYS.
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time, in the BPF/perf/userfaultfd block):
##        * bpf(0, NULL, 0) -> EINVAL (size=0; unchanged).
##        * bpf(0, NULL, 8) -> EFAULT (NULL attr; unchanged).
##        * bpf(0, attr, 8) -> ENOSYS (valid attr, valid cmd; unchanged).
##        * bpf(cmd=100, attr, 8) -> EINVAL (cmd > 64 cap).
##        * bpf(0, attr, 8192) -> E2BIG (size > Linux PAGE_SIZE).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   bpf cmd/size validation: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * The cmd > 64 cap is a soft forward-compat buffer, not a hard
##          spec.  When real bpf cmds land we'll switch to a precise
##          per-cmd switch; until then 64 keeps any conformance probe
##          happy while still rejecting trash.
##        * Like the perf gate, the size cap uses Linux's x86_64
##          PAGE_SIZE (4096) rather than our internal 16 KiB.  Userspace
##          probes that grow attr up to PAGE_SIZE-1 will work; a probe
##          that specifically probes 4097-byte attrs to detect 16 KiB
##          pages will see E2BIG — acceptable per the perf rationale.
##        * validate_user_read is permissive in kernel context, so the
##          self-test for "bad-range EFAULT" only triggers from real
##          userspace (matches the io_uring/perf pattern documented in
##          entries 161/165).
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 21s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   bpf cmd/size validation: OK"
##      at line 433 of build/serial-test.txt.

## 167. Linux ABI: keyctl(2) cmd range validation
##      (Batch 138, 2026-06-05)
##
##      Issue:  sys_keyctl returned ENOSYS unconditionally — a known cmd
##              like KEYCTL_GET_KEYRING_ID and a wildly-bogus cmd like
##              cmd=999 both reported the syscall as missing.  Linux's
##              security/keys/keyctl.c uses a switch whose default arm
##              returns -EOPNOTSUPP (95) for unknown cmds, not -ENOSYS.
##              Probes / fuzzers that pass garbage cmds to detect
##              keyctl behaviour saw a different shape on our ABI than
##              on Linux.
##
##      Fix:    Range-check the cmd before falling through to ENOSYS.
##              Linux 6.10's UAPI defines cmds 0..=33 (KEYCTL_LSM_CONTROL
##              is the newest); cap at 64 for forward-compat buffer so
##              cmds added in 6.11+ aren't pre-rejected here, while
##              still flagging values that fell out of integer overflow:
##                * cmd < 0 -> EOPNOTSUPP
##                * cmd > 64 -> EOPNOTSUPP
##                * otherwise -> ENOSYS (preserved)
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * keyctl(cmd=0,...) -> ENOSYS (unchanged; KEYCTL_GET_KEYRING_ID
##          is a valid cmd, we just don't have a backend).
##        * keyctl(cmd=100,...) -> EOPNOTSUPP (out of forward-compat
##          range).
##        * keyctl(cmd=u64::MAX,...) -> EOPNOTSUPP (sign-extends to -1
##          via `as i32`, exercises the cmd<0 branch).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   keyctl cmd range validation: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * The cmd > 64 cap is a soft forward-compat buffer, not a
##          hard spec.  When a real keyring backend lands we'll switch
##          to a per-cmd dispatch; until then 64 keeps any conformance
##          probe happy while still rejecting trash.
##        * Per-cmd buffer validation (KEYCTL_UPDATE/INSTANTIATE take a
##          payload, KEYCTL_DESCRIBE/READ/GET_SECURITY take a writable
##          buffer) is not yet wired — those user-pointer checks would
##          fire ahead of ENOSYS on Linux but currently we accept any
##          arg2/arg3/arg4.  Deferred until the keyring backend exists,
##          since the per-cmd plumbing belongs there.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   keyctl cmd range validation:
##      OK" at line 435 of build/serial-test.txt.

## 168. Linux ABI: add_key(2) payload/plen validation
##      (Batch 139, 2026-06-05)
##
##      Issue:  sys_add_key validated only the type/description
##              pointers and fell through to ENOSYS, leaving payload
##              (arg2) and plen (arg3) unchecked.  Linux's
##              security/keys/keyctl.c::sys_add_key validates these
##              ahead of any keyring work:
##                * plen > 1 MiB - 1 -> EINVAL.
##                * plen > 0 with payload == NULL -> EFAULT.
##                * plen > 0 with payload range not readable -> EFAULT.
##                * plen == 0 with payload == NULL is a valid "no
##                  payload" call (some key types accept it).
##              Without these gates, probes passing oversize or NULL
##              payloads saw ENOSYS instead of the precise Linux
##              errnos.
##
##      Fix:    Added the same gate ladder in sys_add_key:
##                * plen > (1 << 20) - 1 -> EINVAL.
##                * plen > 0 && payload == 0 -> EFAULT.
##                * plen > 0 -> validate_user_read(payload, plen).
##                * plen == 0 -> fall through (payload not consulted).
##                * Otherwise -> ENOSYS (preserved).
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * add_key(NULL,...) -> EFAULT (unchanged).
##        * add_key(t,d,NULL,0,_) -> ENOSYS (no-payload valid path).
##        * add_key(t,d,NULL,8,_) -> EFAULT (NULL payload, plen > 0).
##        * add_key(t,d,payload,1<<21,_) -> EINVAL (plen > 1 MiB).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   add_key payload/plen validation:
##          OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * validate_user_read is permissive in kernel context, so the
##          "valid payload" case (plen=32, payload=0x3000) returns
##          ENOSYS only because the kernel-context bypass lets it
##          through.  From real userspace with a mapped 32-byte buffer
##          it would also return ENOSYS, but from a kernel-context
##          self-test we can't distinguish the "buffer was actually
##          mapped" case.  The NULL-with-plen>0 case still fires the
##          EFAULT branch because the pointer-NULL check runs first.
##        * request_key (arg2 = callout_info, optional C string) has
##          analogous validation in Linux but isn't covered here —
##          will fold in next batch if it stays as a Rosetta gap.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   add_key payload/plen
##      validation: OK" at line 436 of build/serial-test.txt.

## 169. Linux ABI: request_key(2) callout_info validation
##      (Batch 140, 2026-06-05)
##
##      Issue:  sys_request_key validated only type & description and
##              fell through to ENOSYS without checking callout_info.
##              Linux's security/keys/keyctl.c::sys_request_key reads
##              callout_info with strndup_user when it's non-NULL,
##              which returns EFAULT for an unmapped range.  Probes
##              passing a bogus callout_info saw ENOSYS where Linux
##              gives EFAULT, masking real callout_info pointer bugs
##              in userspace.
##
##      Fix:    When `args.arg2 != 0`, validate it as a readable C
##              string (validate_user_read with len=1, matching the
##              validate_user_str helper) before falling through to
##              ENOSYS.  callout_info == NULL stays valid ("no callout").
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * request_key(t,d,callout_info,_) -> ENOSYS (positive: new
##          gate doesn't reject a valid callout pointer; in kernel
##          context validate_user_read is permissive).
##        * request_key(t,d,NULL,_) -> ENOSYS (NULL callout skips gate).
##        * request_key(NULL,d,...) -> EFAULT (type required; unchanged).
##        * request_key(t,NULL,_,_) -> EFAULT (desc required; unchanged).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   request_key callout/type/desc
##          validation: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * Same kernel-context permissive-validate_user_read note as
##          batches 161/165/166: the "bad callout_info pointer ->
##          EFAULT" path only fires from real userspace.  In the boot
##          self-test, any non-NULL pointer falls through the gate
##          successfully and we test for ENOSYS instead.
##        * Linux's strndup_user caps the string at PAGE_SIZE; we
##          only verify byte 0 is readable, matching the validate_user_str
##          pattern used elsewhere in this file.  An attacker-shaped
##          callout_info that's readable at offset 0 but unmapped past
##          PAGE_SIZE would fail Linux's full copy but pass our gate;
##          the keyring backend (when it lands) would catch this when
##          it actually reads the full string.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   request_key callout/type/desc
##      validation: OK" at line 437 of build/serial-test.txt.

## 170. Linux ABI: io_uring_setup(2) entries/flags/resv validation
##      (Batch 141, 2026-06-05)
##
##      Issue:  sys_io_uring_setup validated only entries != 0 and a
##              non-NULL params pointer.  Linux's io_uring/io_uring.c
##              has more gates that probes rely on:
##                * entries > IORING_MAX_ENTRIES (32768) -> EINVAL
##                  (without IORING_SETUP_CLAMP).
##                * params.flags must contain only valid bits;
##                  anything else -> EINVAL.
##                * params.resv[3] (offset 28..40) must be all zeros;
##                  Linux returns -EINVAL so the reserved space can be
##                  repurposed by future ABI extensions.
##              Without these gates a probe passing entries=999_999 or
##              params.flags=0x8000_0000 saw ENOSYS where Linux gives
##              EINVAL.
##
##      Fix:    Added the gate ladder to sys_io_uring_setup:
##                * entries > 32768 -> EINVAL.
##                * Read first 40 bytes via copy_from_user.
##                * flags & !0xF_FFFF -> EINVAL (Linux 6.10 uses bits
##                  0..=16; cap at bit 19 for forward-compat buffer).
##                * Any non-zero byte in resv[3] (28..40) -> EINVAL.
##                * Otherwise -> ENOSYS (preserved).
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * io_uring_setup(0,_) -> EINVAL (unchanged).
##        * io_uring_setup(65536, params) -> EINVAL (entries > cap).
##        * io_uring_setup(8, NULL) -> EFAULT (unchanged).
##        * io_uring_setup(8, params with flags bit 31 set) -> EINVAL.
##        * io_uring_setup(8, params with resv[0]=0xff) -> EINVAL.
##        * io_uring_setup(8, zeroed params) -> ENOSYS.
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   io_uring_setup entries/flags/resv
##          validation: OK".
##
##      Implementation notes:
##        * Uses core::hint::black_box(&params) around the post-
##          dispatch reset of params[28] = 0 — without the hint the
##          compiler treats the reset as a dead store, since
##          dispatch_linux reads `params` through an opaque u64
##          pointer the optimizer can't see.  black_box stops the
##          unused_assignments lint and keeps the self-test honest.
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * The forward-compat cap for flags (bit 19) is a soft buffer,
##          not a hard spec.  When real io_uring lands we'll switch to
##          an exact per-flag dispatch; until then 0xF_FFFF keeps any
##          conformance probe happy while still rejecting trash.
##        * sq_thread_cpu, sq_thread_idle, wq_fd, and the sq_off /
##          cq_off submembers are not validated — they only matter
##          once a real ring exists.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   io_uring_setup entries/flags/
##      resv validation: OK" at line 432 of build/serial-test.txt.

## 171. Linux ABI: quotactl(2) type validation
##      (Batch 142, 2026-06-05)
##
##      Issue:  sys_quotactl validated only the optional `special`
##              pointer and returned EPERM for everything.  Linux's
##              fs/quota/quota.c::sys_quotactl decodes the cmd as
##              `(subcmd << 8) | type` and returns -EINVAL when
##              type >= MAXQUOTAS (3) ahead of any privilege check.
##              Probes passing a junk type (e.g. type=0xff) saw EPERM
##              where Linux gives EINVAL, masking the cap from
##              feature detection.
##
##      Fix:    Added `let typ = (args.arg0 & 0xff) as u32; if typ >= 3
##              { return linux_err(errno::EINVAL); }` ahead of the
##              special-pointer check.  USRQUOTA(0), GRPQUOTA(1), and
##              PRJQUOTA(2) continue to reach the EPERM path.
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * quotactl(cmd=0,NULL,_,_) -> EPERM (type=0 valid; unchanged).
##        * quotactl(cmd=0x800003,NULL,_,_) -> EINVAL (Q_QUOTAOFF
##          encoded with type=3 — out of range).
##        * quotactl(cmd=0xff,NULL,_,_) -> EINVAL (junk type).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   quotactl type validation: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * The subcmd portion (cmd >> 8) is not validated against the
##          known Linux subcommand set.  Linux's switch returns EINVAL
##          for unknown subcmds, but the privilege check fires first,
##          so most probes never see it.  Since EPERM is correct
##          regardless ("you lack privilege"), we don't add the subcmd
##          gate here — the type gate is the one with a different
##          errno on Linux.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   quotactl type validation:
##      OK" at line 440 of build/serial-test.txt.

## 172. Linux ABI: reboot(2) EPERM ordering bugfix
##      (Batch 143, 2026-06-05)
##
##      Issue:  sys_reboot returned -EINVAL when `magic1 != 0xfee1dead`
##              and only fell through to -EPERM for the right magic.
##              Linux's kernel/reboot.c::SYSCALL_DEFINE4(reboot) does
##              the opposite: `capable(CAP_SYS_BOOT)` runs *before*
##              magic validation, so an unprivileged caller passing
##              magic1=0 sees -EPERM, not -EINVAL.  This is Linux's
##              intentional design — denying that information to
##              non-root keeps unprivileged code from confirming the
##              syscall accepts the documented magic constants.
##
##      Fix:    sys_reboot now returns EPERM unconditionally.  This
##              kernel exposes no path to acquiring CAP_SYS_BOOT from
##              userspace, so every caller is unprivileged in Linux's
##              model — EPERM is the truthful, Linux-shaped answer for
##              any reboot(2) shape.
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * reboot(0xdead,0,0,0) -> EPERM (previously asserted EINVAL).
##        * reboot(0xfee1dead,0,0,0) -> EPERM (unchanged).
##        * reboot(0,0,0,0) -> EPERM (new: every shape collapses to
##          EPERM).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   reboot EPERM ordering: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * Once we add a proper CAP_SYS_BOOT capability and a system
##          shutdown path that consumes it, the EPERM-unconditional
##          stub will need to switch to: check CAP_SYS_BOOT first,
##          then magic1/magic2, then cmd switch, mirroring Linux's
##          ordering.  Until then EPERM is correct.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   reboot EPERM ordering: OK"
##      at line 442 of build/serial-test.txt.

## 173. Linux ABI: swapon(2) swap_flags validation ordering
##      (Batch 144, 2026-06-05)
##
##      Issue:  sys_swapon validated only that the path pointer was
##              non-NULL and readable, then returned EPERM.  Linux's
##              mm/swapfile.c::SYSCALL_DEFINE2(swapon) checks the
##              swap_flags mask against SWAP_FLAGS_VALID *before* the
##              CAP_SYS_ADMIN gate, so an unprivileged caller passing a
##              junk flag bit sees -EINVAL ahead of the -EPERM that
##              would otherwise apply.  A probe passing
##              swapflags=0x8000_0000 saw EPERM where Linux gives
##              EINVAL, masking the flag-bit gate from feature detection.
##
##      Fix:    Added the SWAP_FLAGS_VALID gate at the top of
##              sys_swapon:
##                const SWAP_FLAGS_VALID: u64 = 0x7_FFFF;
##                if args.arg1 & !SWAP_FLAGS_VALID != 0 {
##                    return linux_err(errno::EINVAL);
##                }
##              The mask is the union of:
##                * SWAP_FLAG_PRIO_MASK     = 0x7FFF (priority 0..32767)
##                * SWAP_FLAG_PREFER        = 0x8000
##                * SWAP_FLAG_DISCARD       = 0x10000
##                * SWAP_FLAG_DISCARD_ONCE  = 0x20000
##                * SWAP_FLAG_DISCARD_PAGES = 0x40000
##              Valid flag combinations still fall through to the
##              existing NULL/EFAULT path and finally EPERM.
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * swapon(path, 0) -> EPERM (valid empty flags).
##        * swapon(path, 0x8000_0000) -> EINVAL (bit 31 outside mask).
##        * swapon(path, 0x8000|5) -> EPERM (PREFER + prio=5, valid).
##        * swapoff(path) -> EPERM (unchanged).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   swapon flags validation: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * The priority value within SWAP_FLAG_PRIO_MASK is not
##          separately validated.  Linux's only enforced upper bound
##          is the mask itself (0..0x7FFF), so any value that fits in
##          15 bits is accepted and we honour the same envelope.
##        * Once a real swap subsystem lands, the post-validation
##          path will need: VFS path resolution, file-vs-blockdev
##          discrimination, swap signature read, and the actual
##          CAP_SYS_ADMIN check.  Until then EPERM is correct for
##          every flag-valid call shape.
##        * sys_swapoff is unchanged — Linux's path validation for it
##          is just `getname(specialfile)` which short-circuits to
##          EFAULT/EPERM the same way our stub does.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   swapon flags validation: OK"
##      at line 441 of build/serial-test.txt.

## 174. Linux ABI: mseal(2) addr-alignment and len=0 success path
##      (Batch 145, 2026-06-05)
##
##      Issue:  sys_mseal validated only `flags == 0` and a raw
##              `addr.checked_add(len)` overflow, returning ENOSYS for
##              everything else.  Linux's mm/mseal.c::do_mseal()
##              actually enforces, ahead of any VMA walk:
##                * `if (!PAGE_ALIGNED(start)) return -EINVAL;`
##                * `end = start + PAGE_ALIGN(len);` (the PAGE_ALIGN
##                  rounding can overflow on its own — Linux catches
##                  this via the `end < start` check).
##                * `if (end == start) return 0;` — zero-length call
##                  is an explicit no-op success, NOT a failure.
##              Probes seeing ENOSYS for `mseal(unaligned, anything)`
##              or `mseal(addr, 0)` were unable to tell that the
##              alignment gate exists, and `mseal(addr, 0)` callers
##              would have failed where Linux silently succeeds.
##
##      Fix:    Inserted the two gates and the success path:
##                const ABI_PAGE_SIZE: u64 = 4096;
##                if args.arg2 != 0 { ... EINVAL }
##                if args.arg0 & (ABI_PAGE_SIZE - 1) != 0 { EINVAL }
##                let len_aligned = match args.arg1
##                    .checked_add(ABI_PAGE_SIZE - 1) {
##                    Some(v) => v & !(ABI_PAGE_SIZE - 1),
##                    None => return EINVAL,
##                };
##                if args.arg0.checked_add(len_aligned).is_none() {
##                    return EINVAL;
##                }
##                if len_aligned == 0 { return Ok(0); }
##                ENOSYS
##              The overflow check now uses the page-aligned length,
##              matching Linux exactly (e.g. mseal(addr, u64::MAX) is
##              EINVAL because PAGE_ALIGN(u64::MAX) overflows).
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * mseal(0, 0, flags=1) -> EINVAL (unchanged).
##        * mseal(u64::MAX & ~0xFFF, 0x2000, 0) -> EINVAL (overflow
##          using a page-aligned addr so the alignment gate doesn't
##          preempt).
##        * mseal(0x1001, 0x1000, 0) -> EINVAL (unaligned addr; new).
##        * mseal(0x1000, 0, 0) -> 0 (zero-length success; new).
##        * mseal(0x1000, 0x1000, 0) -> ENOSYS (unchanged).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   mseal addr/len validation: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * The actual VMA walk that mseal performs on Linux — checking
##          every VMA in [start, end) is mapped (else -ENOMEM) and then
##          recording the seal bit on each — remains unimplemented.
##          Until we have a VMA list with seal bits, the answer for any
##          valid non-empty range stays ENOSYS.  Documented in todo 130
##          / 131 / 132 territory.
##        * `PAGE_ALIGN(len)` is computed using Linux's 4 KiB ABI page
##          size, not our 16 KiB internal frame size.  This is what
##          probes expect; an internal seal implementation would need
##          to round the validated range up to the next 16 KiB frame
##          boundary before consulting the VMA list.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   mseal addr/len validation:
##      OK" at line 444 of build/serial-test.txt.

## 175. Linux ABI: userfaultfd(2) CAP_SYS_PTRACE privilege gate
##      (Batch 146, 2026-06-05)
##
##      Issue:  sys_userfaultfd validated only the flags mask and
##              returned ENOSYS for any otherwise-valid call shape.
##              Linux's fs/userfaultfd.c::SYSCALL_DEFINE1(userfaultfd)
##              has a privilege gate that depends on the flags:
##                * UFFD_USER_MODE_ONLY (bit 0) NOT set -> caller must
##                  hold CAP_SYS_PTRACE; unprivileged callers see
##                  -EPERM before the open-file allocation.
##                * UFFD_USER_MODE_ONLY set -> privilege check skipped;
##                  the caller is asking for the user-mode-only
##                  restricted variant.
##              Returning ENOSYS for userfaultfd(0) made every
##              userfaultfd-detection probe (CRIU, postcopy migration
##              tooling, the kernel test suite) believe userfaultfd
##              is unavailable on this system, when in Linux the
##              probe would see EPERM and immediately try
##              UFFD_USER_MODE_ONLY as a fallback.
##
##      Fix:    Added the privilege gate after the flag-mask check:
##                let flags = args.arg0;
##                if flags & !VALID_FLAGS != 0 { return EINVAL; }
##                if flags & UFFD_USER_MODE_ONLY == 0 {
##                    return EPERM;  // CAP_SYS_PTRACE unavailable
##                }
##                ENOSYS
##              No caller in this kernel can hold CAP_SYS_PTRACE
##              today (we have no ptrace capability nor a way to
##              grant it), so every caller is unprivileged in Linux's
##              model and EPERM is the truthful answer.
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * userfaultfd(0x8000_0000) -> EINVAL (unchanged).
##        * userfaultfd(0) -> EPERM (was ENOSYS; new).
##        * userfaultfd(UFFD_USER_MODE_ONLY) -> ENOSYS (was effectively
##          unreachable before since flags=0 used to pass; now this is
##          the only flag-valid shape that reaches the stub).
##        * userfaultfd(UFFD_USER_MODE_ONLY | O_CLOEXEC) -> ENOSYS.
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   userfaultfd privilege gate: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * The userfaultfd implementation itself remains absent —
##          UFFDIO_API, UFFDIO_REGISTER, the read() loop returning
##          struct uffd_msg, and the page-fault handler interposition
##          are all unimplemented.  Until those land, the
##          UFFD_USER_MODE_ONLY shape stays ENOSYS.
##        * Once we have a real CAP_SYS_PTRACE capability, this gate
##          will switch from "always EPERM if !USER_MODE_ONLY" to
##          "check the cap and proceed".  The flag mask itself is
##          already complete (UFFD_USER_MODE_ONLY | O_CLOEXEC |
##          O_NONBLOCK).
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 25s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   userfaultfd privilege gate:
##      OK" at line 439 of build/serial-test.txt.

## 176. Linux ABI: io_submit(2) / io_cancel(2) pointer validation
##      (Batch 147, 2026-06-05)
##
##      Issue:  sys_io_submit checked nr < 0 and (iocbpp == NULL when
##              nr > 0) but skipped the full access_ok check on the
##              iocbpp array.  sys_io_cancel had NO validation at all —
##              it returned EINVAL for every shape, hiding the EFAULT
##              gates Linux applies to iocb and result pointers.
##              fs/aio.c::do_io_submit checks
##              `access_ok(iocbpp, nr * sizeof(struct iocb *))` before
##              the context lookup, and fs/aio.c::SYSCALL_DEFINE3(
##              io_cancel) reads through `iocb->aio_key` (EFAULT) and
##              later writes through `result` (EFAULT) before the
##              context lookup.  Probes passing NULL iocb / NULL result
##              into io_cancel saw EINVAL where Linux gives EFAULT.
##
##      Fix:    sys_io_submit:
##                * nr < 0 -> EINVAL (unchanged).
##                * nr > 0 + arg2 == 0 -> EFAULT (unchanged).
##                * nr > 0 + nr*8 overflow -> EINVAL.
##                * nr > 0 -> validate_user_read(iocbpp, nr*8) before
##                  the EINVAL terminal answer.
##                * nr == 0 -> fall through to EINVAL (Linux's context
##                  lookup happens regardless).
##              sys_io_cancel:
##                * iocb == 0 -> EFAULT.
##                * validate_user_read(iocb, 1) -> EFAULT on failure
##                  (1 byte is enough to detect NULL/unmapped; the
##                  full 64-byte iocb layout isn't committed to).
##                * result == 0 -> EFAULT.
##                * validate_user_write(result, 32) (sizeof io_event)
##                  -> EFAULT on failure.
##                * Otherwise -> EINVAL (no context exists).
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * io_submit(_, -1, _) -> EINVAL (unchanged).
##        * io_submit(_, 1, NULL) -> EFAULT (new).
##        * io_submit(_, 0, _) -> EINVAL (new positive coverage).
##        * io_cancel(_, NULL, _) -> EFAULT (new).
##        * io_cancel(_, valid iocb, NULL result) -> EFAULT (new).
##        * io_cancel(_, valid iocb, valid result) -> EINVAL (new
##          positive coverage; no AIO context exists).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   io_submit/io_cancel pointer
##          validation: OK".
##
##      Implementation notes:
##        * Uses core::hint::black_box(&iocb) and (&result_buf)
##          between dispatch_linux calls so the optimizer doesn't
##          recycle the backing stack slot before dispatch_linux
##          reads through the opaque u64 pointer.  Same pattern as
##          io_uring_setup (todo 170 / batch 141).
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * validate_user_read / validate_user_write are no-ops in
##          kernel-context, so the boot self-tests can only exercise
##          the explicit NULL check, not the full mapping validation
##          gate.  When a real userspace caller hits these syscalls,
##          the mapping check will fire correctly.  The same pattern
##          applies to every Linux ABI gate that uses validate_user_*.
##        * AIO contexts themselves remain unimplemented — io_setup
##          returns ENOSYS, so any valid-shaped io_cancel still
##          terminates at EINVAL (no context to look up).  Once we
##          add a real fs/aio.c equivalent, the EINVAL terminal in
##          io_cancel will switch to the actual cancel path.
##        * struct iocb is 64 bytes on Linux x86_64, but we only
##          validate 1 byte — that's enough to catch NULL / unmapped
##          and matches what Linux's `get_user(key, &iocb->aio_key)`
##          actually touches.  When we read the full iocb we'll
##          extend the validation accordingly.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 24s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   io_submit/io_cancel pointer
##      validation: OK" at line 432 of build/serial-test.txt.

## 177. Linux ABI: mq_timedsend(2) MQ_PRIO_MAX validation
##      (Batch 148, 2026-06-05)
##
##      Issue:  sys_mq_timedsend validated msg/timeout pointers and
##              the fd, then returned EBADF.  Linux's
##              ipc/mqueue.c::do_mq_timedsend() rejects out-of-range
##              priorities ahead of the fd lookup:
##                if (unlikely(msg_prio >= (unsigned long) MQ_PRIO_MAX))
##                    return -EINVAL;
##              MQ_PRIO_MAX is 32768 (15 bits) on Linux x86_64.  A probe
##              passing prio=70000 with a junk fd saw EBADF where Linux
##              gives EINVAL, hiding the priority validation from
##              feature-detection probes (libstdc++'s std::__poll_traits
##              wrapper, glibc's mqueue self-test, the Linux Test
##              Project's mq_timedsend01 test).
##
##      Fix:    Added `const MQ_PRIO_MAX: u64 = 32768;` and an early
##              `if args.arg3 >= MQ_PRIO_MAX { return EINVAL; }` at
##              the top of sys_mq_timedsend, before the msg/timeout
##              pointer validation.  Valid priorities (0..=32767) fall
##              through to the existing path.
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * mq_timedsend(0, NULL, _, 0, _) -> EFAULT (unchanged).
##        * mq_timedsend(_, _, _, 32768, _) -> EINVAL (boundary; new).
##        * mq_timedsend(_, _, _, 70000, _) -> EINVAL (well above; new).
##        * mq_timedsend(0, NULL, _, 32767, _) -> EFAULT (highest valid
##          priority passes the gate; NULL msg is then the next
##          failure).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   mq_timedsend prio validation: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * sys_mq_timedreceive doesn't take a prio argument (it
##          receives prio_ptr instead, which we already validate), so
##          no companion gate is needed there.
##        * The actual POSIX message queue subsystem remains
##          unimplemented — mq_open returns ENOSYS, and any valid
##          mq_timedsend shape still terminates at EBADF.  When real
##          queues land, MQ_PRIO_MAX stays as the pre-fd gate so the
##          full implementation can rely on `prio <= 32767` thereafter.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 21s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   mq_timedsend prio
##      validation: OK" at line 445 of build/serial-test.txt.

## 178. Linux ABI: msgsnd(2) / msgrcv(2) size and msqid validation
##      (Batch 149, 2026-06-05)
##
##      Issue:  sys_msgsnd and sys_msgrcv checked only `msgp == NULL`
##              before returning EINVAL.  Linux's
##              ipc/msg.c::do_msgsnd() validates ahead of the queue
##              lookup and any pointer touch:
##                if (msgsz > ns->msg_ctlmax || (long) msgsz < 0
##                    || msqid < 0)
##                    return -EINVAL;
##              `msg_ctlmax` defaults to MSGMAX = 8192.  The
##              `(long) msgsz < 0` cast catches size_t values above
##              LONG_MAX (i.e. callers who pass (size_t)-1 hoping
##              for overflow in downstream arithmetic).  A probe
##              passing msgsz=(size_t)-1 or msqid=-1 saw EINVAL only
##              after the queue lookup terminal — same errno but no
##              way to tell which gate fired.  More importantly, a
##              valid NULL-pointer probe with msgsz beyond MSGMAX saw
##              EFAULT where Linux gives EINVAL.
##
##      Fix:    Added the three pre-pointer gates in Linux's order:
##                if args.arg2 > MSGMAX { return EINVAL; }
##                if (args.arg2 as i64) < 0 { return EINVAL; }
##                if (args.arg0 as i32) < 0 { return EINVAL; }
##                if args.arg1 == 0 { return EFAULT; }
##                ...
##              The MSGMAX cap is the kernel-namespace default; once
##              we add per-namespace IPC settings, this can switch to
##              the real ns->msg_ctlmax lookup.  Same gates applied
##              symmetrically to sys_msgrcv.
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * msgsnd(0, NULL, 8, _) -> EFAULT (unchanged; valid size).
##        * msgsnd(_, _, 8193, _) -> EINVAL (size > MSGMAX; new).
##        * msgsnd(_, _, u64::MAX, _) -> EINVAL ((long) msgsz < 0; new).
##        * msgsnd(msqid=-1, _, 8, _) -> EINVAL (qid gate; new).
##        * msgrcv(0, NULL, 8, _, _) -> EFAULT (unchanged).
##        * msgrcv(_, _, 8193, _, _) -> EINVAL (new).
##        * msgrcv(msqid=-1, _, 8, _, _) -> EINVAL (new).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   msgsnd/msgrcv size/qid validation:
##          OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * The MSGMAX cap is hard-coded to the kernel-default 8192.
##          Real Linux exposes /proc/sys/kernel/msgmax which lets root
##          raise it; we don't have that knob yet.  When sysctl support
##          for IPC namespaces lands, this gate switches to the per-ns
##          value.
##        * sys_msgsnd does NOT validate `mtype < 1` because that
##          requires reading the first 8 bytes of *mtext — and
##          validate_user_read is a no-op in kernel-context, so the
##          self-test couldn't exercise it.  The mtype gate goes in
##          when we implement the actual message-queue copy_from_user
##          path.
##        * The full SysV message queue implementation remains
##          absent — msgget returns ENOSYS, and any valid-shaped
##          msgsnd/msgrcv still terminates at EINVAL.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   msgsnd/msgrcv size/qid
##      validation: OK" at line 445 of build/serial-test.txt.

## 179. Linux ABI: semget/semop/semtimedop SEMMSL/SEMOPM gates.
##
##      Where:  kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs::sys_semget,
##              kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs::sys_semop,
##              kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs::sys_semtimedop.
##
##      Problem: SysV semaphore stubs were terminating too coarsely.
##              sys_semget only rejected nsems<0 — a probe asking for
##              33000 semaphores (above SEMMSL) saw ENOSYS instead of
##              the documented EINVAL.  sys_semop / sys_semtimedop did
##              not check `semid < 0` ahead of the NULL pointer test
##              (so a caller passing semid=-1 + valid sops would see
##              EINVAL only after the namespace lookup, instead of
##              the early Linux gate), and did not check `nsops >
##              SEMOPM` at all (so an oversized batch saw EFAULT or
##              EINVAL instead of the distinct E2BIG that Linux uses
##              to mean "too many ops").
##
##      Fix:    Added Linux's documented gate order, per
##              ipc/sem.c::SYSCALL_DEFINE3(semget) and
##              ipc/sem.c::do_semtimedop():
##                semget:
##                  if (nsems < 0 || nsems > SEMMSL) return EINVAL;
##                semop / semtimedop:
##                  if (nsops < 1) return EINVAL;
##                  if (semid < 0) return EINVAL;
##                  if (nsops > SEMOPM) return E2BIG;
##                  if (sops == NULL) return EFAULT;
##                  validate_user_read(sops, nsops*6) ...
##              SEMMSL = 32000, SEMOPM = 500 — kernel-namespace
##              defaults.  Note the distinct errno: E2BIG, not EINVAL,
##              for too-many-ops.
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * semget(_, -1, 0) -> EINVAL (unchanged).
##        * semget(_, 4, 0) -> ENOSYS (unchanged; 4 <= SEMMSL).
##        * semget(_, 32001, 0) -> EINVAL (SEMMSL cap; new).
##        * semop(_, _, 0) -> EINVAL (unchanged; nsops==0).
##        * semop(0, NULL, 1) -> EFAULT (unchanged).
##        * semop(semid=-1, NULL, 1) -> EINVAL (new; semid<0 gate
##          ordered before NULL-pointer check).
##        * semop(_, _, 501) -> E2BIG (new; SEMOPM cap, distinct
##          errno).
##        * semtimedop(0, NULL, 1, _) -> EFAULT (unchanged).
##        * semtimedop(semid=-1, NULL, 1, _) -> EINVAL (new).
##        * semtimedop(_, _, 501, _) -> E2BIG (new).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   semget/semop SEMMSL/SEMOPM
##          validation: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * SEMMSL / SEMOPM are hard-coded to Linux's kernel-namespace
##          defaults (32000 / 500).  Real Linux exposes both via
##          /proc/sys/kernel/sem (and per-IPC-namespace).  When sysctl
##          support for IPC namespaces lands, these gates switch to
##          the per-ns values.
##        * sys_semctl is still a single-line EINVAL stub — the full
##          cmd-dispatch (IPC_STAT/IPC_SET/GETALL/SETVAL/...) is
##          deferred until we have a real semaphore set table.
##        * No semaphore-set table yet — every valid-shaped semop
##          terminates at EINVAL.  This is correct shape but not
##          functional; a full SysV semaphore implementation is on
##          the longer roadmap.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   semget/semop SEMMSL/SEMOPM
##      validation: OK" in build/serial-test.txt.

## 180. Linux ABI: kexec_load / kexec_file_load flag validation.
##
##      Where:  kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs::sys_kexec_load,
##              kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs::sys_kexec_file_load.
##
##      Problem: kexec_load and kexec_file_load accepted any `flags`
##              value and returned EPERM unconditionally — the
##              terminal "we don't load replacement kernels" answer.
##              That hid the EINVAL Linux returns for malformed flag
##              bits and for foreign-architecture requests.  A probe
##              passing `flags = 0x4` (reserved low bit) or `flags =
##              0x0001_0000` (foreign arch e_machine) saw EPERM and
##              concluded "permission, not validity" — a feature-
##              detection probe would then try a different code path
##              after dropping privileges, only to hit the real
##              EINVAL on the second attempt.
##
##      Fix:    Added Linux's documented gates from
##              kernel/kexec.c::kexec_load_check() and
##              kernel/kexec_file.c::SYSCALL_DEFINE5(kexec_file_load),
##              run ahead of the EPERM terminal so visibility extends
##              to unprivileged callers:
##                kexec_load:
##                  if ((flags & ~KEXEC_ARCH_MASK) & ~KEXEC_FLAGS) != 0
##                      -> EINVAL  (reserved low bit)
##                  arch = flags & KEXEC_ARCH_MASK
##                  if arch != 0 && arch != KEXEC_ARCH_X86_64
##                      -> EINVAL  (foreign arch)
##                  (existing nr_segments / segments-pointer gates,
##                   then EPERM)
##                kexec_file_load:
##                  if flags & ~KEXEC_FILE_FLAGS != 0  -> EINVAL
##                  (existing fd / cmdline gates, then EPERM)
##              Constants:
##                KEXEC_FLAGS = KEXEC_ON_CRASH | KEXEC_PRESERVE_CONTEXT
##                             = 0x3
##                KEXEC_ARCH_MASK = 0xffff_0000
##                KEXEC_ARCH_X86_64 = EM_X86_64 << 16 = 62 << 16
##                                  = 0x003e_0000
##                KEXEC_FILE_FLAGS = UNLOAD | ON_CRASH | NO_INITRAMFS
##                                  | DEBUG = 0xf  (Linux 6.10)
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * kexec_load with reserved low bit (flags=0x4) -> EINVAL
##          (new; previously EPERM).
##        * kexec_load with foreign arch (flags=0x0001_0000) ->
##          EINVAL (new; previously EPERM).
##        * kexec_load with valid flags (ON_CRASH | X86_64 arch) ->
##          EPERM (new; verifies the gates allow legitimate flags
##          through to the terminal answer).
##        * kexec_file_load with reserved flag (0x10) -> EINVAL
##          (new; previously EPERM).
##        * kexec_file_load with KEXEC_FILE_DEBUG (0x8) -> EPERM
##          (new; verifies a legitimate flag is accepted by the
##          gate).
##        * All pre-existing kexec tests still pass (nr_segments>16,
##          NULL segments with nr>0, bad kernel_fd, NULL cmdline,
##          valid -> EPERM).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   kexec_load/kexec_file_load flag
##          validation: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * KEXEC_FILE_FLAGS is hard-coded to 0xf, the Linux 6.10
##          value.  When KEXEC_FILE_OK_TO_LOAD_OLD or other new flag
##          bits land in upstream Linux, this cap will need to
##          widen.  Documented here so the next maintainer knows to
##          re-check against the current Linux UAPI when raising
##          our minimum target kernel version.
##        * KEXEC_ARCH_X86_64 is the only non-zero arch we accept.
##          When 32-bit x86 compatibility lands (KEXEC_ARCH_386),
##          this list should grow.  For an x86_64-only OS the
##          current list is exhaustive.
##        * The terminal answer remains EPERM for every valid call
##          — we don't load replacement kernels from userspace.
##          That's the same answer Linux gives an unprivileged
##          caller, and matches the design (kexec is a root-only
##          operation we won't grant access to).
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   kexec_load/kexec_file_load
##      flag validation: OK" in build/serial-test.txt.


## 181. Linux ABI: seccomp per-op flag validation.
##
##      Where:  kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs::sys_seccomp.
##
##      Problem: sys_seccomp had three coarse gates run in sequence
##              for all operations:
##                1. op > 3 -> EINVAL
##                2. op == 0 (STRICT) && flags != 0 -> EINVAL
##                3. op > 0 && uargs == NULL -> EFAULT
##              then ENOSYS.  This collapsed every "needs uargs" op
##              into one EFAULT check and never validated the
##              SECCOMP_FILTER_FLAG_MASK bits.  Linux's
##              kernel/seccomp.c::do_seccomp() dispatches on op and
##              applies per-op validation:
##                * SET_MODE_STRICT (0): flags != 0 || uargs != NULL
##                  -> EINVAL.
##                * SET_MODE_FILTER (1): flags & ~MASK -> EINVAL,
##                  uargs == NULL -> EFAULT.
##                * GET_ACTION_AVAIL (2): flags != 0 -> EINVAL,
##                  uargs == NULL -> EFAULT.
##                * GET_NOTIF_SIZES (3): flags != 0 -> EINVAL,
##                  uargs == NULL -> EFAULT.
##              A probe passing op=GET_ACTION_AVAIL with flags=1 saw
##              ENOSYS (skipping the flag-validation entirely) instead
##              of the EINVAL Linux returns; libseccomp's feature
##              detection walks flag bits to discover the highest
##              accepted value, and would conclude "all bits valid"
##              and try to set every flag on a real filter — only to
##              hit ENOSYS later on a path it thought was supported.
##
##      Fix:    Refactored sys_seccomp into a `match op` that applies
##              Linux's per-op gates ahead of the terminal ENOSYS:
##                  match op {
##                      0 => { flags!=0 || uargs!=0 -> EINVAL }
##                      1 => {
##                          flags & ~0x3f -> EINVAL,
##                          uargs==0 -> EFAULT,
##                          validate_user_read(uargs, 4)
##                      }
##                      2|3 => {
##                          flags!=0 -> EINVAL,
##                          uargs==0 -> EFAULT,
##                          validate_user_read(uargs, 4)
##                      }
##                      _ => EINVAL,
##                  }
##                  ENOSYS
##              SECCOMP_FILTER_FLAG_MASK = TSYNC | LOG | SPEC_ALLOW
##                                       | NEW_LISTENER | TSYNC_ESRCH
##                                       | WAIT_KILLABLE_RECV
##                                       = 0x3f  (Linux 6.10).
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * seccomp(0, 0, buf_ptr) -> EINVAL  (STRICT+uargs; new).
##        * seccomp(1, 0x80, buf_ptr) -> EINVAL  (FILTER bad flag
##          bit; new — previously ENOSYS).
##        * seccomp(1, 0x3f, buf_ptr) -> ENOSYS  (FILTER all valid
##          flags; new — verifies the mask accepts every documented
##          bit).
##        * seccomp(2, 1, buf_ptr) -> EINVAL  (GET_ACTION_AVAIL with
##          flags!=0; new — previously ENOSYS).
##        * seccomp(2, 0, buf_ptr) -> ENOSYS  (GET_ACTION_AVAIL
##          valid; new — verifies the gate allows op==2 through).
##        * seccomp(3, 1, buf_ptr) -> EINVAL  (GET_NOTIF_SIZES with
##          flags!=0; new — previously ENOSYS).
##        * seccomp(3, 0, buf_ptr) -> ENOSYS  (GET_NOTIF_SIZES
##          valid; new).
##        * All pre-existing seccomp tests still pass (bad op 99 ->
##          EINVAL, STRICT+flags=1 -> EINVAL, FILTER NULL -> EFAULT,
##          STRICT all-zero -> ENOSYS).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   seccomp per-op flag validation:
##          OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * SECCOMP_FILTER_FLAG_MASK is hard-coded to 0x3f, the
##          Linux 6.10 value.  When new flag bits land upstream
##          (e.g. a SECCOMP_FILTER_FLAG_KILL_PROCESS_TGID hypothetical)
##          this mask will need to widen.  Documented here so the
##          next maintainer knows to re-check against the current
##          Linux UAPI on bump.
##        * The validate_user_read(uargs, 4) call is a 4-byte
##          probe — enough to surface unmapped pages but not a full
##          struct sock_fprog validation.  We can't usefully validate
##          the filter contents until we have a real seccomp
##          implementation; today the terminal answer is ENOSYS so
##          the probe length is sufficient to mirror Linux's "I
##          touched the pointer before erroring" behavior on a
##          mapping issue.
##        * The terminal answer remains ENOSYS for every valid call.
##          glibc, runc, systemd, and libseccomp all degrade
##          gracefully (run-without-filter for ENOSYS) — matches the
##          documented "seccomp not configured into the kernel"
##          fallback path.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   seccomp per-op flag
##      validation: OK" in build/serial-test.txt.


## 182. Linux ABI: fanotify_init flag validation.
##
##      Where:  kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs::sys_fanotify_init.
##
##      Problem: sys_fanotify_init unconditionally returned ENOSYS
##              and ignored both `flags` and `event_f_flags` entirely.
##              That hid the EINVAL Linux returns for malformed flag
##              bits, dual-class combinations, broken FID interlocks,
##              and out-of-range access modes.  A probe passing
##              `flags = FAN_REPORT_NAME` (without FAN_REPORT_DIR_FID)
##              saw ENOSYS — concluding "the kernel doesn't have
##              fanotify at all" — when Linux would give EINVAL
##              meaning "the kernel has it; you specified an invalid
##              combination."  libfanotify, systemd-tmpfiles, and
##              audit feature detection walk the flag-bit space
##              expecting EINVAL to mean "valid syscall, bad flags"
##              and ENOSYS to mean "syscall absent"; collapsing both
##              into ENOSYS broke that distinction.
##
##      Fix:    Ported Linux's fs/notify/fanotify/fanotify_user.c
##              flag-validation table from SYSCALL_DEFINE2(fanotify_init):
##                * FANOTIFY_INIT_FLAGS = FAN_CLOEXEC | FAN_NONBLOCK
##                                      | FAN_CLASS_MASK (0xc)
##                                      | FAN_UNLIMITED_QUEUE
##                                      | FAN_UNLIMITED_MARKS
##                                      | FAN_ENABLE_AUDIT
##                                      | FAN_REPORT_PIDFD
