##                                      | FAN_REPORT_TID
##                                      | FAN_REPORT_FID
##                                      | FAN_REPORT_DIR_FID
##                                      | FAN_REPORT_NAME
##                                      | FAN_REPORT_TARGET_FID
##                                      = 0x1fff (Linux 6.10)
##                * Unknown bits in `flags` -> EINVAL.
##                * Class field must NOT be 0xc (CONTENT |
##                  PRE_CONTENT both set) -> EINVAL.
##                * FAN_REPORT_NAME requires FAN_REPORT_DIR_FID
##                  -> EINVAL otherwise.
##                * FAN_REPORT_TARGET_FID requires the full FID
##                  trio (FID | DIR_FID | NAME) -> EINVAL otherwise.
##                * FANOTIFY_INIT_FD_FLAGS = O_ACCMODE | O_LARGEFILE
##                                          | O_CLOEXEC | O_APPEND
##                                          | O_NONBLOCK | O_NOATIME
##                * Unknown bits in `event_f_flags` -> EINVAL.
##                * (event_f_flags & O_ACCMODE) == 3 -> EINVAL.
##                * Terminal answer: ENOSYS for well-formed calls
##                  (userspace falls back to inotify).
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * fanotify_init(0, 0) -> ENOSYS (pre-existing; verifies
##          all-zero passes the new gates and reaches the terminal
##          answer).
##        * fanotify_init(0x2000, 0) -> EINVAL (unknown bit above
##          FANOTIFY_INIT_FLAGS; new).
##        * fanotify_init(0xc, 0) -> EINVAL (dual class CONTENT |
##          PRE_CONTENT; new).
##        * fanotify_init(FAN_REPORT_NAME=0x800, 0) -> EINVAL
##          (NAME requires DIR_FID; new).
##        * fanotify_init(NAME | DIR_FID=0xc00, 0) -> ENOSYS
##          (verifies the dependency, when satisfied, lets the call
##          reach the terminal; new).
##        * fanotify_init(NAME|DIR_FID|TARGET_FID=0x1c00, 0)
##          -> EINVAL (TARGET_FID requires FID too; new).
##        * fanotify_init(FID|DIR_FID|NAME|TARGET_FID=0x1e00, 0)
##          -> ENOSYS (verifies full FID trio; new).
##        * fanotify_init(0, 0x10) -> EINVAL (unknown event_f_flags
##          bit; new).
##        * fanotify_init(0, 3) -> EINVAL (O_ACCMODE=3 is the
##          invalid all-three combination; new).
##        * fanotify_init(0, O_CLOEXEC | O_NONBLOCK) -> ENOSYS
##          (verifies a typical real-world event_f_flags passes; new).
##        * fanotify_mark(0,...) -> ENOSYS (pre-existing).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   fanotify_init flag validation:
##          OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * FANOTIFY_INIT_FLAGS is hard-coded to 0x1fff, the Linux
##          6.10 layout.  When new flag bits land upstream (a
##          hypothetical FAN_REPORT_FD_ERROR or similar) this mask
##          needs to widen.  Documented here so the next maintainer
##          knows to re-check against the current Linux UAPI on a
##          minimum-kernel-version bump.
##        * The terminal answer for fanotify_init is ENOSYS rather
##          than the EPERM Linux gives an unprivileged caller.  We
##          have no CAP_SYS_ADMIN model for the linux ABI yet, and
##          ENOSYS is the path userspace handles with a clean
##          fallback to inotify.  When capability checks land for
##          the linux ABI, this should become EPERM for the
##          ADMIN-only init-flag combinations and ENOSYS only for
##          the FAN_CLASS_NOTIF unprivileged path (since 5.13).
##        * sys_fanotify_mark still only validates the pathname's
##          first byte.  Full flag-bit validation (FAN_MARK_ADD/
##          REMOVE/FLUSH exclusivity, FAN_MARK_MOUNT/FILESYSTEM
##          exclusivity, FANOTIFY_MARK_FLAGS mask) is a future batch.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   fanotify_init flag
##      validation: OK" in build/serial-test.txt.


## 183. Linux ABI: fanotify_mark flag validation.
##
##      Where:  kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs::sys_fanotify_mark.
##
##      Problem: sys_fanotify_mark validated only the pathname's
##              first byte and returned ENOSYS for any flag/mask
##              combination.  A probe walking the FANOTIFY_MARK_FLAGS
##              space (looking for which combinations of ADD/REMOVE/
##              FLUSH and MOUNT/FILESYSTEM/inode the kernel accepts)
##              saw ENOSYS uniformly and concluded the syscall was
##              absent — when Linux would give EINVAL for the
##              malformed flag combos and reach the kernel's actual
##              dispatch path for well-formed ones.  Batch 182 fixed
##              the same hole in fanotify_init; this batch follows
##              up with fanotify_mark so the pair is consistent.
##
##      Fix:    Ported Linux's fs/notify/fanotify/fanotify_user.c
##              flag-validation gates from SYSCALL_DEFINE5(fanotify_mark):
##                * FANOTIFY_MARK_FLAGS = ADD | REMOVE | DONT_FOLLOW
##                                      | ONLYDIR | MOUNT
##                                      | IGNORED_MASK
##                                      | IGNORED_SURV_MODIFY | FLUSH
##                                      | FILESYSTEM | EVICTABLE
##                                      | IGNORE = 0x7ff (Linux 6.10)
##                * Unknown bits -> EINVAL.
##                * Exactly one of ADD (1), REMOVE (2), FLUSH (0x80)
##                  must be set -> EINVAL if zero or more than one.
##                * MOUNT (0x10) and FILESYSTEM (0x100) cannot both
##                  be set (default mark type is inode) -> EINVAL.
##                * EVICTABLE (0x200) requires inode mark type
##                  (no MOUNT, no FILESYSTEM) -> EINVAL.
##                * IGNORE (0x400) and IGNORED_MASK (0x20) are
##                  mutually exclusive (Linux 6.0+ deprecated
##                  IGNORED_MASK in favor of IGNORE) -> EINVAL.
##                * IGNORED_SURV_MODIFY (0x40) requires one of
##                  IGNORED_MASK or IGNORE -> EINVAL otherwise.
##                * pathname (if non-NULL): existing 1-byte
##                  validate_user_read for EFAULT.
##                * Terminal: ENOSYS for well-formed calls (matches
##                  batch 182's fanotify_init terminal).
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * fanotify_mark(0, 0, ...) -> EINVAL  (no op bit; new —
##          previously ENOSYS).
##        * fanotify_mark(_, ADD=0x1, ...) -> ENOSYS  (valid ADD;
##          new — replaces the old "flags=0 -> ENOSYS" test which
##          now correctly EINVALs).
##        * fanotify_mark(_, 0x801, ...) -> EINVAL  (unknown bit
##          0x800 above FANOTIFY_MARK_FLAGS; new).
##        * fanotify_mark(_, ADD|REMOVE=0x3, ...) -> EINVAL  (op
##          exclusivity; new).
##        * fanotify_mark(_, ADD|FLUSH=0x81, ...) -> EINVAL  (op
##          exclusivity; new).
##        * fanotify_mark(_, ADD|MOUNT|FILESYSTEM=0x111, ...)
##          -> EINVAL  (type exclusivity; new).
##        * fanotify_mark(_, ADD|MOUNT|EVICTABLE=0x211, ...)
##          -> EINVAL  (EVICTABLE+MOUNT; new).
##        * fanotify_mark(_, ADD|IGNORE|IGNORED_MASK=0x421, ...)
##          -> EINVAL  (mutually exclusive; new).
##        * fanotify_mark(_, ADD|IGNORED_SURV_MODIFY=0x41, ...)
##          -> EINVAL  (SURV_MODIFY without IGNORED_MASK/IGNORE; new).
##        * fanotify_mark(_, ADD|IGNORED_MASK|IGNORED_SURV_MODIFY=0x61,
##          ...) -> ENOSYS  (verifies the dependency, satisfied,
##          lets the call through to the terminal; new).
##        * fanotify_mark(_, FLUSH=0x80, ...) -> ENOSYS  (FLUSH
##          alone is a valid op; new).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   fanotify_mark flag validation:
##          OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * The mask argument (arg2) is not validated against
##          FANOTIFY_EVENTS / FANOTIFY_EVENT_FLAGS.  Linux's
##          fanotify_user.c::fanotify_events_supported() does a
##          per-mark-type bitmask check that's specific to the mount
##          flags of the target — we can't usefully replicate that
##          without a real fanotify implementation behind it.  When
##          the implementation lands, this needs the mask gate.
##        * dirfd (arg3) is not validated.  We don't have a real
##          fanotify fd in arg0 anyway (fanotify_init returns
##          ENOSYS), so the fd table check would be vacuous against
##          a probe's random fd values.  When fanotify_init returns
##          a real fd this needs the validate_linux_fd gate plus a
##          HandleKind::FanotifyFd check on arg0.
##        * pathname's full string is not validated (only first
##          byte).  Sufficient for EFAULT visibility on unmapped
##          pages; not sufficient for ENAMETOOLONG.  Linux applies
##          ENAMETOOLONG after looking up dirfd, which we can't
##          do until real fanotify is wired up.
##        * Terminal remains ENOSYS rather than the EBADF Linux
##          gives for "not a fanotify fd."  EBADF would require
##          us to first reject every fd as not-fanotify which is
##          also true but less informative for "syscall isn't
##          implemented" probes that drive userspace fallback paths.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   fanotify_mark flag
##      validation: OK" in build/serial-test.txt.


## 184. Linux ABI: quotactl cmds-whitelist validation.
##
##      Where:  kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs::sys_quotactl.
##
##      Problem: sys_quotactl already gated `type >= MAXQUOTAS (3)`
##              with EINVAL, but accepted any `cmds = cmd >> 8`
##              value and fell through to the terminal EPERM.
##              A libquota / setquota / repquota probe walking
##              the cmd space to discover which ops the kernel
##              implements saw EPERM uniformly — including for
##              obvious garbage like cmd = 0xdeadbeef00 — when
##              Linux's fs/quota/quota.c::SYSCALL_DEFINE4(quotactl)
##              dispatches on `cmds` and the default arm returns
##              EINVAL.  Collapsing "valid op + unprivileged caller"
##              with "garbage op" into the same answer defeats
##              probes that need to distinguish "this kernel
##              doesn't know that op" from "this kernel knows it
##              but won't let me run it without CAP_SYS_ADMIN".
##
##      Fix:    Mirrored Linux's gate order from
##              fs/quota/quota.c::SYSCALL_DEFINE4(quotactl):
##                1. typ = cmd & SUBCMDMASK (0xff); typ >= 3
##                   -> EINVAL (kept).
##                2. cmds = cmd >> SUBCMDSHIFT (8); reject ops
##                   outside the two known whitelists:
##                     * Generic ops 0x800001..=0x800009
##                       (Q_SYNC..Q_GETNEXTQUOTA per
##                       include/uapi/linux/quota.h).
##                     * XFS ops 0x5801..=0x5809
##                       (Q_XQUOTAON..Q_XGETNEXTQUOTA, encoded
##                       as XQM_CMD(n) = ('X'<<8) | n).
##                3. Validate `special` (arg1) with
##                   validate_user_read for 1 byte if non-NULL
##                   (EFAULT for unmapped) — kept.
##                4. Terminal: EPERM for well-formed calls
##                   (placeholder until real quota support).
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * quotactl(cmd=Q_SYNC<<8|0 = 0x80000100, NULL, ...)
##          -> EPERM  (valid generic op; gate passes, terminal
##          fires; replaces the old "arg0=0 -> EPERM" test which
##          now correctly EINVALs since cmds=0 is not whitelisted).
##        * quotactl(cmd=0x800003, ...) -> EINVAL  (type=3
##          rejected ahead of cmds check; kept).
##        * quotactl(cmd=0xff, ...) -> EINVAL  (type=0xff
##          rejected; kept).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   quotactl type validation: OK".
##        * quotactl(cmd=Q_GETQUOTA<<8|0 = 0x80000700, NULL, ...)
##          -> EPERM  (valid generic op at top of range; new).
##        * quotactl(cmd=Q_XQUOTAON<<8|0 = 0x580100, NULL, ...)
##          -> EPERM  (valid XFS op at bottom of range; new).
##        * quotactl(cmd=Q_XGETNEXTQUOTA<<8|0 = 0x580900, NULL,
##          ...) -> EPERM  (valid XFS op at top of range; new).
##        * quotactl(cmd=0, NULL, ...) -> EINVAL  (cmds=0 is
##          not in any whitelist; new — was EPERM pre-batch).
##        * quotactl(cmd=0x80000a00, ...) -> EINVAL  (cmds=
##          0x80000a, one past generic range; new).
##        * quotactl(cmd=0x580a00, ...) -> EINVAL  (cmds=
##          0x580a, one past XFS range; new).
##        * quotactl(cmd=0xdeadbe00, ...) -> EINVAL  (cmds=
##          0xdeadbe, obvious garbage; new).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   quotactl cmd validation: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * `special` (path) is only validated 1 byte deep, not
##          the full string.  Sufficient for EFAULT visibility
##          on unmapped pages; not for ENAMETOOLONG.
##        * `addr` (arg2) and `id` (arg3) are not validated.
##          Linux validates these per-op: Q_GETQUOTA needs a
##          writable dqblk; Q_SETINFO needs a readable
##          dqinfo; etc.  Without an actual quota implementation
##          there's no struct layout to check against.  When
##          real quota lands, this needs the per-op argument
##          validation.
##        * Terminal remains EPERM rather than per-op
##          behavior.  Linux gives EPERM only when the caller
##          lacks CAP_SYS_ADMIN for ops that need it; some
##          ops (Q_SYNC with NULL special) work without
##          privilege.  Distinguishing requires a real
##          quota module.
##        * Q_SETQB / Q_GETQB obsolete v1 ops are not in the
##          whitelist (Linux dropped them).  Probes targeting
##          those see EINVAL, which matches Linux 2.6+.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##      Serial confirms both "[syscall/linux]   quotactl type
##      validation: OK" and "[syscall/linux]   quotactl cmd
##      validation: OK" in build/serial-test.txt.


## 185. Linux ABI: mount_setattr struct-field validation.
##
##      Where:  kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs::sys_mount_setattr.
##
##      Problem: sys_mount_setattr validated the AT_* `flags`
##              argument, the `path` string, and that
##              `attr != NULL && size >= 32`, then returned
##              ENOSYS for every well-formed call.  Linux's
##              fs/namespace.c::SYSCALL_DEFINE5(mount_setattr)
##              and `build_mount_kattr()` enforce additional
##              gates on `struct mount_attr` fields ahead of the
##              kernel-mode dispatch:
##                * `size > PAGE_SIZE` -> E2BIG.
##                * `attr_set` outside MOUNT_ATTR_MASK -> EINVAL.
##                * `attr_clr` outside MOUNT_ATTR_MASK -> EINVAL.
##                * `attr_set & MOUNT_ATTR__ATIME` not in
##                  {RELATIME(0), NOATIME(0x10), STRICTATIME(0x20)}
##                  -> EINVAL.
##                * `attr_clr` clears ATIME bits without `attr_set`
##                  also setting one -> EINVAL.
##                * `propagation` not in {0, MS_UNBINDABLE,
##                  MS_PRIVATE, MS_SLAVE, MS_SHARED} -> EINVAL.
##              A util-linux `unshare --mount-setattr` or systemd
##              MountAPIVFS probe walking the attr fields to find
##              what the kernel supports saw ENOSYS uniformly
##              for both garbage and well-formed configurations
##              and concluded the syscall was absent — when Linux
##              would have given precise per-field EINVAL.
##
##      Fix:    Mirrored Linux's gate order:
##                1. Flags AT_* mask check (kept).
##                2. Path string validation if non-NULL (kept).
##                3. attr == NULL || size < 32 -> EINVAL (kept).
##                4. size > 4096 (Linux x86_64 PAGE_SIZE) -> E2BIG.
##                5. validate_user_read(attr, 32) -> EFAULT.
##                6. copy_from_user the 32-byte struct.
##                7. attr_set / attr_clr outside MOUNT_ATTR_MASK
##                   (0x1|0x2|0x4|0x8|0x70|0x80|0x100000|0x200000)
##                   -> EINVAL.
##                8. attr_set ATIME bits in
##                   {RELATIME=0, NOATIME=0x10, STRICTATIME=0x20}
##                   exclusively -> else EINVAL.
##                9. attr_clr ATIME without attr_set ATIME -> EINVAL.
##               10. propagation in whitelist or 0 -> else EINVAL.
##               11. Terminal: ENOSYS (unchanged).
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * mount_setattr(flags=0x80) -> EINVAL  (bad flag; kept).
##        * mount_setattr(size=16) -> EINVAL  (too small; kept).
##        * mount_setattr(flags=AT_RECURSIVE, all-zero attr) ->
##          ENOSYS  (valid; kept).
##        * mount_setattr(size=4097) -> E2BIG  (PAGE_SIZE; new).
##        * mount_setattr(attr_set=0x400) -> EINVAL  (bit outside
##          MOUNT_ATTR_MASK; new).
##        * mount_setattr(attr_clr=0x400) -> EINVAL  (bit outside
##          MOUNT_ATTR_MASK; new).
##        * mount_setattr(attr_set=0x30=NOATIME|STRICTATIME) ->
##          EINVAL  (ATIME exclusivity; new).
##        * mount_setattr(attr_set=0x40) -> EINVAL  (undefined
##          bit in __ATIME mask; new).
##        * mount_setattr(attr_clr=0x10, attr_set=0) -> EINVAL
##          (cannot clear ATIME without setting one; new).
##        * mount_setattr(propagation=MS_SHARED=0x100000) ->
##          ENOSYS  (valid propagation; new).
##        * mount_setattr(propagation=MS_SHARED|MS_PRIVATE) ->
##          EINVAL  (mutually exclusive; new).
##        * mount_setattr(propagation=0xdead) -> EINVAL  (not in
##          whitelist; new).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   mount_setattr attr
##          validation: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * `userns_fd` (struct mount_attr field at offset 24) is
##          not validated.  Linux uses it only when attr_set has
##          MOUNT_ATTR_IDMAP — without a real userns implementation
##          there's no fd to look up.  When IDMAP support lands,
##          this needs `validate_linux_fd(userns_fd)` plus a
##          HandleKind::UserNs check, conditional on
##          (attr_set & MOUNT_ATTR_IDMAP) != 0.
##        * `dirfd` is not validated.  We have no real mount fd
##          (open_tree returns EPERM) so the fd table check would
##          be vacuous against probe inputs.  When fsmount/
##          open_tree return real fds this needs the fd gate plus
##          a HandleKind::MountFd check.
##        * Terminal remains ENOSYS rather than the EBADF Linux
##          gives once a fully validated call reaches a non-mount
##          fd.  ENOSYS is more informative for "syscall not
##          implemented" probes that drive userspace fallback
##          paths.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   mount_setattr attr
##      validation: OK" in build/serial-test.txt.


## 186. Linux ABI: open_by_handle_at handle/fd validation.
##
##      Where:  kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs::sys_open_by_handle_at.
##
##      Problem: sys_open_by_handle_at validated `handle != NULL`
##              and that the 8-byte header was readable, then
##              jumped straight to the terminal EPERM.  It did
##              not validate `handle_bytes` (the first field of
##              struct file_handle) and did not check `mountdirfd`
##              at all.  Linux's fs/fhandle.c::handle_to_path
##              enforces gates ahead of the privilege check:
##                * `handle_bytes == 0` -> EINVAL.
##                * `handle_bytes > MAX_HANDLE_SZ (128)` -> EINVAL.
##                * `mountdirfd` (unless AT_FDCWD) must be a
##                  valid fd -> EBADF otherwise.
##                * The full `8 + handle_bytes` payload must be
##                  readable.
##              A glibc `open_by_handle_at` wrapper, libmount
##              probe, or `name_to_handle_at`/`open_by_handle_at`
##              round-trip test walking handle sizes to find the
##              kernel's limit saw EPERM for everything (including
##              obvious garbage like handle_bytes=0xffffffff),
##              missing the EINVAL signal that Linux gives for
##              malformed handle headers.
##
##      Fix:    Mirrored Linux's gate order:
##                1. mountdirfd != AT_FDCWD (-100): validate_linux_fd
##                   -> EBADF if not in the fd table.  AT_FDCWD is
##                   allowed (Linux's get_path_anchor short-circuits
##                   to current->fs->pwd).
##                2. handle == NULL -> EFAULT (kept).
##                3. validate_user_read(handle, 8) -> EFAULT (kept).
##                4. copy_from_user the 8-byte header and parse
##                   handle_bytes as u32 from offset 0.
##                5. handle_bytes == 0 -> EINVAL.
##                6. handle_bytes > MAX_HANDLE_SZ (128) -> EINVAL.
##                7. validate_user_read(handle, 8 + handle_bytes)
##                   -> EFAULT.
##                8. Terminal: EPERM (CAP_DAC_READ_SEARCH; unchanged).
##
##              No flag gate added — Linux's file_open_root
##              silently ignores O_CREAT/O_TRUNC/O_EXCL for handle
##              opens, so they're not malformed inputs.
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * open_by_handle_at(0, NULL, 0) -> EFAULT  (kept).
##        * open_by_handle_at(0, handle_ptr_with_handle_bytes=0, 0)
##          -> EINVAL  (replaces old "valid -> EPERM" test which
##          now correctly EINVALs since the all-zero buffer has
##          handle_bytes=0; new).
##        * open_by_handle_at(0, handle_ptr_with_handle_bytes=129, 0)
##          -> EINVAL  (above MAX_HANDLE_SZ; new).
##        * open_by_handle_at(0, valid_handle_ptr_with_handle_bytes=8,
##          0) -> EPERM  (terminal; full 16-byte buffer readable; new).
##        * open_by_handle_at(AT_FDCWD=-100, valid_handle, 0)
##          -> EPERM  (AT_FDCWD bypasses fd-table check; new).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   open_by_handle_at handle
##          validation: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * `flags` (arg2) are not validated.  Linux's
##          build_open_flags applies the same masking as openat
##          and silently clears O_CREAT/O_TRUNC/O_EXCL.  When
##          we wire up real file-handle dispatch this needs the
##          openat flag-mask check.
##        * `mountdirfd != AT_FDCWD` is only checked for fd-table
##          validity, not that it refers to a mount.  Linux's
##          get_path_anchor walks the file's mnt and rejects with
##          EINVAL for non-mount fds.  When real mount fds exist
##          this needs a HandleKind::MountFd check.
##        * Terminal remains EPERM rather than EINVAL/ENOENT
##          that Linux would give for a non-decodable handle.
##          EPERM is the correct early answer for our "no
##          file-handle support, no CAP_DAC_READ_SEARCH ever"
##          stub.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 24s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   open_by_handle_at
##      handle validation: OK" in build/serial-test.txt.


## 187. Linux ABI: landlock_create_ruleset ERRATA flag + dispatch.
##
##      Where:  kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs::sys_landlock_create_ruleset.
##
##      Problem: sys_landlock_create_ruleset treated `flags` as a
##              bitmask masking against LANDLOCK_CREATE_RULESET_VERSION
##              (0x1) only.  Linux 6.10 added
##              LANDLOCK_CREATE_RULESET_ERRATA (0x2) for the errata-
##              bitmap query path; we returned EINVAL for that flag,
##              so probes calling `landlock_create_ruleset(NULL, 0,
##              ERRATA)` to discover known errata couldn't even tell
##              whether the kernel knew about errata at all (they
##              had to guess between "unsupported flag" and "no
##              errata to report").  Also, Linux's real check is an
##              equality dispatch — `flags == VERSION`, `flags == ERRATA`,
##              or `flags == 0` — not a bitmask, so the combination
##              `VERSION | ERRATA = 0x3` should EINVAL but we never
##              tested it.
##
##      Fix:    Rewrote the flag handling to match Linux's
##              security/landlock/syscalls.c::SYSCALL_DEFINE3 dispatch:
##                * `flags == 0`: enter real-ruleset construction path
##                  (kept; same EFAULT/EINVAL/ENOSYS terminals).
##                * `flags == VERSION (0x1)` with `attr == NULL` and
##                  `size == 0`: return ABI version 0 (kept).
##                * `flags == ERRATA (0x2)` with `attr == NULL` and
##                  `size == 0`: return errata bitmap 0 (new).
##                * Anything else (including `VERSION | ERRATA`,
##                  `VERSION` with non-NULL attr, `ERRATA` with
##                  non-zero size, undefined high bits): EINVAL.
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * landlock_create_ruleset(NULL, 0, VERSION=1) -> 0  (kept).
##        * landlock_create_ruleset(NULL, 0, ERRATA=2) -> 0  (new).
##        * landlock_create_ruleset(NULL, 0, 3=VERSION|ERRATA)
##          -> EINVAL  (combinations not allowed; new).
##        * landlock_create_ruleset(attr=8, 0, VERSION) -> EINVAL
##          (query needs NULL attr; new).
##        * landlock_create_ruleset(NULL, 8, ERRATA) -> EINVAL
##          (query needs size=0; new).
##        * landlock_create_ruleset(NULL, 0, 0xff) -> EINVAL  (kept).
##        * landlock_create_ruleset(NULL, 0, 0) [flags=0 NULL attr]
##          -> EFAULT  (real path NULL attr; kept).
##        * landlock_create_ruleset(attr=8, 8, 0) -> ENOSYS  (real
##          path; kept).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   landlock_create_ruleset flag
##          dispatch: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * `handled_access_fs` (first u64 of struct
##          landlock_ruleset_attr) is not bit-validated against
##          LANDLOCK_ACCESS_FS_MASK (0xffff in 6.10+).  Linux's
##          build_check_abi rejects unknown bits.  Since we always
##          return ENOSYS for the real-ruleset path, an
##          unvalidated handled_access_fs only matters for hostile
##          probes that walk the bits to find the kernel's supported
##          set — they'll see ENOSYS and infer "no Landlock"
##          regardless.  When real Landlock lands, this needs the
##          per-bit mask check.
##        * `handled_access_net` (Linux 6.7, second u64) and
##          `scoped` (Linux 6.10, third u64) extensions to the
##          attr struct are not validated either, for the same
##          reason.
##        * `landlock_abi_errata` constant is reported as 0 — no
##          errata.  Real Linux 6.12 reports a non-zero bitmap with
##          known errata flags; we don't have any to surface
##          because we don't have Landlock.  Userspace should
##          interpret "0 errata" as "no errata-affected behaviors
##          present," which is truthful for a kernel without
##          Landlock at all.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   landlock_create_ruleset
##      flag dispatch: OK" in build/serial-test.txt.


## 188. Linux ABI: landlock_restrict_self log flags (6.10 + 6.12).
##
##      Where:  kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs::sys_landlock_restrict_self.
##
##      Problem: sys_landlock_restrict_self rejected any non-zero
##              `flags` with EINVAL.  Linux 6.10 introduced
##              LANDLOCK_RESTRICT_SELF_LOG_SAME_EXEC_OFF (1<<0) and
##              LANDLOCK_RESTRICT_SELF_LOG_NEW_EXEC_ON (1<<1) for
##              audit-log control during the no-new-privs exec
##              transition, and Linux 6.12 added
##              LANDLOCK_RESTRICT_SELF_LOG_SUBDOMAINS_OFF (1<<2)
##              to suppress audit logs in child rulesets.  Probes
##              calling `landlock_restrict_self(fd,
##              LANDLOCK_RESTRICT_SELF_LOG_*)` to set up sane
##              audit defaults saw EINVAL on a kernel that ought
##              to accept the bits, masking a behavioural difference
##              behind a parse error.  Since `landlock_create_ruleset`
##              honestly reports ABI version 0, no caller can
##              actually reach a state where this matters today —
##              but the parse error surfaces on probes that
##              optimistically pass log flags without checking the
##              ABI version first.
##
##      Fix:    Defined LANDLOCK_RESTRICT_SELF_FLAGS = 0x7 and
##              switched to `flags & !FLAGS != 0 -> EINVAL`.  The
##              terminal EBADF (no real ruleset fd) is preserved
##              for any well-formed call.
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * landlock_restrict_self(fd=0, flags=8) -> EINVAL
##          (undefined bit 0x8; new — replaces old
##          "flags=1 -> EINVAL" test which now correctly EBADFs).
##        * landlock_restrict_self(fd=0, flags=0) -> EBADF  (kept).
##        * landlock_restrict_self(fd=0, flags=1 = LOG_SAME_EXEC_OFF)
##          -> EBADF  (Linux 6.10; new).
##        * landlock_restrict_self(fd=0, flags=2 = LOG_NEW_EXEC_ON)
##          -> EBADF  (Linux 6.10; new).
##        * landlock_restrict_self(fd=0, flags=4 = LOG_SUBDOMAINS_OFF)
##          -> EBADF  (Linux 6.12; new).
##        * landlock_restrict_self(fd=0, flags=7 = all log) -> EBADF
##          (new).
##        * landlock_restrict_self(fd=0, flags=0x101 = LOG_SAME +
##          undefined 0x100) -> EINVAL  (mask is bit-precise; new).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   landlock_restrict_self log
##          flags: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * The log flags are accepted but have no effect — we
##          never actually restrict the process or emit audit
##          logs, since Landlock is reported absent at ABI version 0.
##          When real Landlock lands, this needs the per-flag
##          semantics: SAME_EXEC_OFF disables the audit-log entry
##          on the calling exec, NEW_EXEC_ON enables it on the
##          next exec, and SUBDOMAINS_OFF inhibits audit emission
##          from rules layered on top by descendant tasks.
##        * Terminal remains EBADF rather than EOPNOTSUPP that
##          a real "Landlock missing" kernel would give.  EBADF
##          is correct given the ruleset_fd is never a real
##          Landlock fd (landlock_create_ruleset never produces
##          one), and probes ordering EBADF before kernel-feature
##          checks see a meaningful "fd doesn't exist" signal.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   landlock_restrict_self
##      log flags: OK" in build/serial-test.txt.



## 189. Linux ABI: landlock_add_rule rule_attr validation.
##
##      Where:  kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs::sys_landlock_add_rule.
##
##      Problem: sys_landlock_add_rule accepted any non-NULL
##              rule_attr pointer and went straight to the
##              terminal EBADF on validate_linux_fd(ruleset_fd).
##              Linux gates rule_attr fields ahead of the ruleset
##              capability check:
##                * `flags` (arg3) must be 0 — reserved.
##                * `rule_type` (arg1) must be PATH_BENEATH (1) or
##                  NET_PORT (2); other values -> EINVAL.
##                * `rule_attr` (arg2) must be non-NULL and
##                  readable for 16 bytes.
##                * `allowed_access` (first u64) must not be zero —
##                  Linux returns ENOMSG ("Empty rule body").
##                * For PATH_BENEATH: allowed_access bits outside
##                  LANDLOCK_ACCESS_FS_MASK (0xffff) -> EINVAL.
##                * For NET_PORT: allowed_access bits outside
##                  LANDLOCK_ACCESS_NET_MASK (0x3) -> EINVAL, and
##                  port (second u64) > 65535 -> EINVAL.
##              Probes walking allowed_access bits to find the
##              kernel's supported FS rights, or trying NET_PORT
##              with a stray bit, saw uniform EBADF instead of
##              the proper parse errors and concluded the rule
##              shape itself was rejected.
##
##      Fix:    Added the gating layer in order:
##                1. flags != 0 -> EINVAL.
##                2. rule_type not in {1, 2} -> EINVAL.
##                3. rule_attr == 0 -> EFAULT.
##                4. validate_user_read(rule_attr, 16) ->
##                   linux_errno_for on failure.
##                5. copy_from_user 16 bytes; parse allowed_access
##                   (u64 LE at offset 0) and port (u64 LE at 8).
##                6. allowed_access == 0 -> ENOMSG.
##                7. PATH_BENEATH: allowed_access & ~0xffff != 0
##                   -> EINVAL.
##                8. NET_PORT: allowed_access & ~0x3 != 0 -> EINVAL;
##                   port > 65535 -> EINVAL.
##                9. Fall through to validate_linux_fd(ruleset_fd)
##                   and the terminal EBADF.
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * landlock_add_rule(fd=0, type=1, attr=NULL, flags=0)
##          -> EFAULT  (kept; rule_attr NULL is the early
##          EFAULT after the type gate).
##        * landlock_add_rule(fd=0, type=99, attr=big_ptr, flags=0)
##          -> EINVAL  (unknown rule_type; kept).
##        * landlock_add_rule(fd=0, type=1, attr=NULL, flags=1)
##          -> EINVAL  (flags != 0 short-circuits before
##          EFAULT — new ordering).
##        * landlock_add_rule(fd=0, type=1, attr=lpb_attr, flags=0)
##          where lpb_attr = {allowed=0x1, parent_fd=0} -> EBADF
##          (new; valid PATH_BENEATH rule, falls through to
##          ruleset_fd check).
##        * landlock_add_rule(fd=0, type=1, attr=lpb_empty, flags=0)
##          where lpb_empty = {allowed=0, parent_fd=0} -> ENOMSG
##          (new; Linux's "empty rule body").
##        * landlock_add_rule(fd=0, type=1, attr=lpb_bad_access,
##          flags=0) where allowed=0x10000 (bit outside FS mask)
##          -> EINVAL  (new).
##        * landlock_add_rule(fd=0, type=2, attr=lnp_attr, flags=0)
##          where lnp_attr = {allowed=0x1, port=8080} -> EBADF
##          (new; valid NET_PORT rule).
##        * landlock_add_rule(fd=0, type=2, attr=lnp_bad_access,
##          flags=0) where allowed=0x4 (outside NET mask 0x3)
##          -> EINVAL  (new).
##        * landlock_add_rule(fd=0, type=2, attr=lnp_bad_port,
##          flags=0) where allowed=0x1, port=65536 -> EINVAL
##          (new).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   landlock_add_rule attr
##          validation: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * `ruleset_fd` is not validated against
##          HandleKind::Landlock — we have no real Landlock
##          object, and validate_linux_fd's kernel-context EBADF
##          is the terminal regardless.  When real Landlock lands,
##          the type check needs to follow:
##          add_rule on a non-Landlock fd should be EBADFD, not
##          EBADF.
##        * The FS / NET access mask is global (0xffff and 0x3),
##          not per-ruleset.  Linux checks that the rule's
##          allowed_access is a *subset* of the ruleset's
##          declared handled_access.  Since we don't carry a real
##          ruleset, the global-mask check is the strictest gate
##          we can apply; it correctly rejects truly-undefined
##          bits but accepts bits that a specific ruleset would
##          have excluded from its handled set.  Real Landlock
##          must compare against the bound ruleset's
##          handled_access_fs / handled_access_net.
##        * Linux 6.7 added `parent_fd` semantics for PATH_BENEATH
##          (open dirfd whose subtree the rule covers); we don't
##          validate parent_fd at all because the terminal EBADF
##          on ruleset_fd preempts that check.  When real
##          Landlock lands, parent_fd needs O_PATH-dirfd
##          validation between the access-mask check and the
##          ruleset-fd check.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   landlock_add_rule
##      attr validation: OK" in build/serial-test.txt.


## 190. Linux ABI: io_pgetevents __aio_sigset sigsetsize validation.
##
##      Where:  kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs::sys_io_pgetevents.
##
##      Problem: sys_io_pgetevents validated the outer 16-byte
##              __aio_sigset struct pointer (validate_user_read of
##              args.arg5 + 16) but did not parse the struct's fields
##              and gate them.  Linux's
##              SYSCALL_DEFINE6(io_pgetevents) does:
##                copy_from_user(&ksig, usig, sizeof(ksig));
##                ret = set_user_sigmask(ksig.sigmask, ksig.sigsetsize);
##                if (ret) return ret;
##                ret = do_io_getevents(...);
##              and set_user_sigmask (kernel/signal.c) returns -EINVAL
##              when sigsetsize != sizeof(sigset_t) (8 bytes on x86_64).
##              That gate fires *before* the AIO context lookup, so a
##              probe pairing a non-8 sigsetsize with a known-bad ctx_id
##              would on Linux see EINVAL-from-sigmask, but on our stub
##              saw the same EINVAL emanating from the terminal "no
##              context" path — masking the parse error behind the
##              context error.
##
##      Fix:    After validate_user_read of the 16-byte sigset struct,
##              copy_from_user the 16 bytes, parse sigmask (u64 LE at
##              offset 0) and sigsetsize (u64 LE at offset 8).
##              - sigsetsize != 8 -> EINVAL (matches set_user_sigmask).
##              - sigmask != 0 -> validate_user_read(sigmask, 8)
##                returning linux_errno_for on fault.
##              Then fall through to the terminal EINVAL (no ctx).
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * io_pgetevents bad nr ordering (min>nr) -> EINVAL  (kept).
##        * io_pgetevents valid no-sigset -> EINVAL (terminal)  (kept).
##        * io_pgetevents sigset {mask=NULL, size=8} -> EINVAL
##          (terminal; new — confirms the valid sigset path falls
##          through cleanly).
##        * io_pgetevents sigset {size=7} -> EINVAL  (new; size gate).
##        * io_pgetevents sigset {size=9} -> EINVAL  (new; size gate;
##          exactly-8 only).
##        * io_pgetevents sigset {size=0} -> EINVAL  (new; size gate).
##        * io_pgetevents sigset {mask=kernel-stack-buf, size=8}
##          -> EINVAL (terminal; new — confirms the sigmask
##          validate_user_read accepts a real readable pointer and
##          falls through).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   io_pgetevents sigset validation:
##          OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * The EFAULT path on a bad sigmask pointer cannot be
##          exercised from kernel-context self-tests because
##          mm::user::is_kernel_context() bypasses validate_user_read
##          (no owning user process means every address is trusted).
##          The validate_user_read call site is correct; only the
##          test harness can't reach it.  When a userspace task
##          test harness lands, add EFAULT coverage for sigmask
##          pointing at unmapped/kernel addresses and for the outer
##          sigset struct pointer being bad.
##        * sigmask contents (the 8 sigset bytes) are not interpreted
##          — we don't actually install a temporary sigmask because
##          we don't return events to wait on.  Linux's
##          set_user_sigmask saves/restores the current sigset around
##          the syscall; that's a no-op on our terminal EINVAL path,
##          but real AIO support needs the save/restore wired through
##          signal-mask state.
##        * The 16-byte __aio_sigset layout assumes 64-bit (sizeof
##          pointer == 8, sizeof size_t == 8).  32-bit compat needs
##          the COMPAT_SYSCALL_DEFINE entry with a 12-byte struct
##          (4-byte pointer + 8-byte size_t with padding); we don't
##          have a 32-bit ABI so this isn't an issue today, but
##          when COMPAT lands, the struct read has to come from the
##          compat wrapper.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   io_pgetevents sigset
##      validation: OK" in build/serial-test.txt.


## 191. Linux ABI: settimeofday timeval_valid gate.
##
##      Where:  kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs::sys_settimeofday.
##
##      Problem: sys_settimeofday validated only the tv/tz pointer
##              shapes (16-byte and 8-byte struct reads) before
##              returning the terminal EPERM.  Linux's
##              SYSCALL_DEFINE2(settimeofday) in kernel/time.c does
##              copy_from_user the tv, calls timeval_valid() on it,
##              and returns -EINVAL if the values are out of range —
##              ahead of CAP_SYS_TIME / do_sys_settimeofday64.
##              timeval_valid:
##                tv_sec  >= 0
##                tv_usec >= 0
##                tv_usec <  USEC_PER_SEC (1_000_000)
##              Probes calling settimeofday({-1, 0}, NULL) to test
##              "what does the kernel do with a malformed tv" saw
##              EPERM and concluded the values were accepted (just
##              that the privilege check failed) — when in reality
##              even a privileged caller would have seen EINVAL.
##
##      Fix:    After validate_user_read of the 16-byte tv, copy the
##              struct in, parse tv_sec (i64 LE@0) and tv_usec
##              (i64 LE@8).  Reject:
##                tv_sec  <  0          -> EINVAL
##                tv_usec <  0          -> EINVAL
##                tv_usec >= 1_000_000  -> EINVAL
##              Then continue to the tz validation and the terminal
##              EPERM.  tz alone (NULL tv) still EPERMs because
##              setting just the timezone is also CAP_SYS_TIME-gated.
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * settimeofday(tv={0,0}, NULL) -> EPERM  (kept; zero is
##          a valid timeval).
##        * settimeofday(tv={0,0}, tz) -> EPERM  (kept).
##        * settimeofday(tv={-1, 0}, NULL) -> EINVAL  (new;
##          tv_sec<0 gate).
##        * settimeofday(tv={0, -1}, NULL) -> EINVAL  (new;
##          tv_usec<0 gate).
##        * settimeofday(tv={0, 1_000_000}, NULL) -> EINVAL  (new;
##          tv_usec >= USEC_PER_SEC gate; exact boundary).
##        * settimeofday(tv={0, 999_999}, NULL) -> EPERM  (new;
##          boundary just inside valid — confirms 999999 *is*
##          accepted as a microsecond value, not folded as "1
##          second minus 1 us").
##        * settimeofday(NULL, tz) -> EPERM  (new; tz-only path
##          still privilege-gated).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   settimeofday timeval_valid: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * tz_minuteswest range (Linux conventionally [-720, 720]
##          for -12h..+12h) is not validated.  Linux itself does
##          not enforce this range in the syscall path either —
##          do_sys_settimeofday64 accepts any i32 — so leaving this
##          unvalidated matches Linux behaviour.  warn_bad_tz used
##          to fire on dsttime != 0 in much older kernels (pre-2.6
##          DST support), but modern Linux silently ignores tz_dsttime.
##        * The 16-byte tv layout (i64 sec + i64 usec) is the
##          x86_64 native struct timeval.  glibc's
##          __kernel_old_timeval and __kernel_sock_timeval on 32-bit
##          archs differ; when COMPAT lands, the compat wrapper
##          must use i32 sec + i32 usec (matching old_timeval32) for
##          x86_32 callers.
##        * timeval_valid in Linux also rejects tv_sec > KTIME_SEC_MAX
##          (~292 billion years).  We don't enforce that upper bound;
##          even tv_sec = i64::MAX is currently accepted and reaches
##          the terminal EPERM.  When real settimeofday lands, the
##          upper bound must come in alongside the do_sys_settimeofday64
##          range check.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   settimeofday
##      timeval_valid: OK" in build/serial-test.txt.


## 192. Linux ABI: ioperm turn_on=0 revoke + tightened range check.
##
##      Where:  kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs::sys_ioperm.
##
##      Problem: sys_ioperm returned EPERM unconditionally after the
##              range checks.  Linux's ksys_ioperm
##              (arch/x86/kernel/ioport.c) gates the CAP_SYS_RAWIO
##              check on `turn_on != 0`:
##                if (turn_on && !capable(CAP_SYS_RAWIO))
##                    return -EPERM;
##              Revoking permission you never held is a no-op success
##              (0).  glibc's iopl-bypass teardown idiom
##              `ioperm(port, n, 0)` runs after the protected I/O
##              block to release the port grant; our pre-batch stub
##              made that teardown fail with EPERM, which propagates
##              through libpam and inetutils' rsh as a misleading
##              "I/O permission denied" error from a path that's
##              actually just resetting state.
##
##              Bonus: the range gate was too tight.  Pre-batch
##              rejected `num > 0xFFFF` and `from > 0xFFFF`
##              individually, but Linux's check is `(from + num) >
##              IO_BITMAP_BITS (=65536)` — `from=0, num=65536` is
##              the full-range revoke and should be valid, not
##              EINVAL.  Glibc's port-reset helpers actually use the
##              full range.
##
##      Fix:    Two changes:
##                1. Range check: `from.checked_add(num)` for
##                   overflow detection, then `end > 0x1_0000`
##                   rejects.  Allows from=0, num=65536 (end=
##                   65536, <= IO_BITMAP_BITS).  Still rejects
##                   from=65536 (end=65537 > 65536) and from=
##                   0xFFF0, num=0x20 (end=0x10010 > 0x10000).
##                2. After range checks pass, `turn_on == 0` ->
##                   SyscallResult::ok(0).  Only `turn_on != 0`
##                   falls through to the terminal EPERM (we don't
##                   grant direct port access; drivers run as IPC
##                   servers behind capabilities).
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * ioperm(from=0x1_0000, num=1, on=1) -> EINVAL  (kept).
##        * ioperm(from=0xFFF0, num=0x20, on=1) -> EINVAL  (kept;
##          end > 0x10000).
##        * ioperm(from=0x100, num=4, on=1) -> EPERM  (kept;
##          renamed marker to clarify on=1 path).
##        * ioperm(from=0x100, num=4, on=0) -> 0  (new; revoke
##          succeeds without CAP_SYS_RAWIO).
##        * ioperm(from=0, num=0x1_0000, on=0) -> 0  (new; full-
##          range revoke — this case was EINVAL pre-batch because
##          of the `num > 0xFFFF` cap).
##        * ioperm(from=0xFFF0, num=0x20, on=0) -> EINVAL  (new;
##          range gate fires ahead of the turn_on=0 short-circuit,
##          matches Linux ordering).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   ioperm turn_on=0 revoke path:
##          OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * The turn_on=0 success path does not actually clear any
##          I/O bitmap — we never allocate one because we never
##          grant access in the first place.  This is fine
##          semantically (revoking permission you never held is a
##          no-op success), but if real ioperm support lands the
##          syscall has to walk the per-task TSS I/O bitmap and
##          clear the bits.
##        * `turn_on` is taken as u32 (low 32 bits of arg2); Linux
##          uses `int turn_on`.  A negative C int casts to a u32
##          with the top bit set, which is non-zero — same EPERM
##          path.  A future "explicit -1 means something" extension
##          would need an i32 cast and a signed check.
##        * No per-port granularity surface yet — we treat the
##          whole [0, 65536) range as "no access," same EPERM
##          either way.  Real driver allocation will need a finer
##          policy (e.g., a driver IPC server holding ioperm caps
##          for the legacy keyboard ports only).
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 24s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   ioperm turn_on=0 revoke
##      path: OK" in build/serial-test.txt.


## 193. Linux ABI: iopl level=0 no-op success.
##
##      Where:  kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs::sys_iopl.
##
##      Problem: sys_iopl returned EPERM for every in-range level
##              including 0.  Linux's
##              arch/x86/kernel/ioport.c::SYSCALL_DEFINE1(iopl)
##              gates the CAP_SYS_RAWIO check on level > current_iopl:
##                if (level > old && !capable(CAP_SYS_RAWIO))
##                    return -EPERM;
##              Every task starts at IOPL 0, so `level == 0` always
##              passes "0 > 0 == false" and is a no-op success
##              regardless of capability.  glibc's iopl(0) teardown
##              after a protected I/O block (mirror of the ioperm
##              revoke pattern) and probes verifying "I have no IOPL
##              right now" both expect 0; our pre-batch stub gave
##              EPERM and made the probe conclude the kernel was
##              actively denying a request it should have accepted.
##
##      Fix:    After the `level > 3 -> EINVAL` range check, treat
##              `level == 0` as a no-op success (SyscallResult::ok(0)).
##              Levels 1/2/3 still EPERM — we don't grant direct
##              port access to userspace because drivers run as
##              IPC servers behind capabilities.
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * iopl(4) -> EINVAL  (kept; out of range).
##        * iopl(3) -> EPERM  (renamed marker to clarify
##          "level=3 needs CAP_SYS_RAWIO").
##        * iopl(0) -> 0  (new; no-op success).
##        * iopl(1) -> EPERM  (new; loop also covers level=2).
##        * iopl(2) -> EPERM  (new).
##        * iopl(u64::MAX) -> EINVAL  (new; -1 as i32 outside 0..=3).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   iopl level=0 noop, >0 EPERM:
##          OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * The success path doesn't actually adjust any per-task
##          IOPL — there's nothing to lower because we never raise.
##          Real iopl support needs to write `t->iopl = level <<
##          12` into the task struct and refresh the FLAGS image so
##          IRET picks up the new IOPL.
##        * `args.arg0 as i32` truncates the high u64 bits before
##          the range check.  A u64 value with low 32 bits in
##          [0, 3] but high bits set (e.g., 0x1_0000_0000) would
##          truncate to 0 and slide through as a no-op success.
##          Linux uses `unsigned int level` and accepts any u32
##          value <= 3 with the same effect, so this matches
##          Linux's behaviour on 32-bit-truncated arg passing.
##          Adding an explicit `args.arg0 > 3 -> EINVAL` against
##          the raw u64 would be stricter than Linux; left as-is.
##        * iopl is deprecated in Linux 6.x in favour of ioperm
##          for the common case (single-port grant) and remains
##          for legacy callers.  No need to deprecate ours.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s; two prior
##      runs hit the intermittent RCU self-test stall —
##      same flake observed in batches 155 / 159 / others — third
##      attempt passed).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   iopl level=0 noop, >0
##      EPERM: OK" in build/serial-test.txt.


## 194. Linux ABI: acct CAP_SYS_PACCT-first ordering.
##
##      Where:  kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs::sys_acct.
##
##      Problem: sys_acct validated the filename pointer (when
##              non-NULL) via validate_user_str *before* returning
##              the terminal EPERM.  Linux's
##              kernel/acct.c::SYSCALL_DEFINE1(acct) is in the
##              opposite order:
##                if (!capable(CAP_SYS_PACCT))
##                    return -EPERM;
##                if (name) { tmp = getname(name); ... }
##              The CAP check is the first gate.  We never grant
##              CAP_SYS_PACCT, so a probe calling
##              `acct(unmapped_or_kernel_ptr)` on Linux gets EPERM
##              (the cap check fires before getname).  Our stub
##              returned EFAULT from validate_user_str, leaking the
##              "I would have read the path" signal to a fuzzer or
##              ABI-shape detector — they could distinguish "EPERM
##              because no cap" from "EFAULT because pointer bad"
##              when Linux conflates both into EPERM.
##
##      Fix:    Drop the path validation entirely.  Return EPERM
##              unconditionally.  The function now takes _args
##              because the pointer is never inspected.
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * acct(NULL) -> EPERM  (kept).
##        * acct("/var/log/acct") -> EPERM  (kept).
##        * acct(0xffff_8000_0000_0000) -> EPERM  (new; kernel-
##          space ptr — pre-batch returned EFAULT from
##          validate_user_str).
##        * acct(0xdead_beef_dead_beef) -> EPERM  (new; arbitrary
##          junky ptr).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   acct CAP_SYS_PACCT first: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * Real process accounting is not implemented.  If
##          CAP_SYS_PACCT ever becomes grantable, the full Linux
##          flow needs the getname/acct_on/putname dance and the
##          per-superblock accounting hook.
##        * Linux's acct also validates the path is a regular file
##          (acct_on returns -EACCES for directories, -EROFS for
##          read-only fs, etc.).  None of that is reachable here
##          because we never get past the cap check.
##        * Linux 5.9+ deprecated the per-process accounting subsystem
##          in favour of cgroup-based bookkeeping; new code is
##          expected to use cgroups instead.  Our EPERM matches what
##          a kernel built without CONFIG_BSD_PROCESS_ACCT returns,
##          which is the safest "this feature is gone" signal.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   acct CAP_SYS_PACCT
##      first: OK" in build/serial-test.txt.


## 195. Linux ABI: lsm_get_self_attr UNDEF / SINGLE gates.
##
##      Where:  kernel/src/syscall/linux.rs::sys_lsm_get_self_attr.
##
##      Problem: sys_lsm_get_self_attr's gates didn't match Linux's
##              security/lsm_syscalls.c::lsm_getselfattr.  Three
##              specific divergences:
##                1. attr range: pre-batch required attr in
##                   100..=105 (the known LSM_ATTR_CURRENT..
##                   SOCKCREATE constants).  Linux only checks
##                   `attr == LSM_ATTR_UNDEF (=0) -> EINVAL`; any
##                   other value walks the security hook list and
##                   returns the per-hook default (EOPNOTSUPP when
##                   no LSM declares the attr).  Our over-tight
##                   check returned EINVAL for attr=50 and attr=200,
##                   which a probe interprets as "syscall rejected
##                   the attr index" rather than "no LSM provides
##                   that attribute."  Distinguishing those two is
##                   how a tool like libapparmor's feature probe
##                   decides whether to fall back to /proc/*/attr.
##                2. size == NULL: Linux returns EINVAL via an
##                   explicit `if (size == NULL) return -EINVAL;`
##                   ahead of get_user.  We returned EFAULT.
##                3. flags == LSM_FLAG_SINGLE (1) but ctx (arg1) ==
##                   NULL: Linux returns EINVAL.  We accepted any
##                   non-NULL or NULL ctx and fell through to
##                   EOPNOTSUPP, missing the "SINGLE without ctx
##                   is malformed" check.
##
##      Fix:    Three gate changes in order:
##                - `attr == 0 -> EINVAL` (replaces 100..=105 range
##                  check; any non-zero attr falls through).
##                - `args.arg2 == 0 -> EINVAL` (replaces EFAULT).
##                - `args.arg3 != 0 && args.arg3 != 1 -> EINVAL`
##                  (already there, kept; bit-precise on flags).
##                - `args.arg3 == 1 && args.arg1 == 0 -> EINVAL`
##                  (new).
##              Terminal remains EOPNOTSUPP (LSM_RET_DEFAULT
##              (getselfattr) for no providers).
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * lsm_get_self_attr(attr=0, ctx, size) -> EINVAL  (kept,
##          reason changed: explicit LSM_ATTR_UNDEF check).
##        * lsm_get_self_attr(attr=50, ctx, size) -> EOPNOTSUPP
##          (new; was EINVAL pre-batch).
##        * lsm_get_self_attr(attr=200, ctx, size) -> EOPNOTSUPP
##          (new; was EINVAL pre-batch).
##        * lsm_get_self_attr(attr=100, ctx, size=NULL) -> EINVAL
##          (new errno; was EFAULT pre-batch).
##        * lsm_get_self_attr(attr=100, ctx, size, flags=2) ->
##          EINVAL  (kept).
##        * lsm_get_self_attr(attr=100, ctx, size, flags=3) ->
##          EINVAL  (new; SINGLE + extra bit).
##        * lsm_get_self_attr(attr=100, ctx=NULL, size, flags=1)
##          -> EINVAL  (new; SINGLE-without-ctx gate).
##        * lsm_get_self_attr(attr=100, ctx, size, flags=1) ->
##          EOPNOTSUPP  (new; SINGLE valid happy path).
##        * lsm_get_self_attr(attr=100, ctx, size) -> EOPNOTSUPP
##          (kept; bare valid attr).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   lsm_get_self_attr
##          UNDEF/SINGLE gating: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * The SINGLE+ctx path doesn't validate ctx contents (Linux
##          does copy_from_user(&lctx, uctx, sizeof(lctx)) then
##          checks lctx.id != LSM_ID_UNDEF).  We don't have any
##          LSM module so even the lctx.id check would just lead
##          back to EOPNOTSUPP; the read isn't observable.  When
##          real LSM lands, add the 32-byte lsm_ctx copy_from_user
##          and the lctx.id check.
##        * size validation reads/writes 4 bytes but on x86_64
##          size_t is 8 bytes.  The Linux header declares it as
##          `size_t __user *` so the buffer should be 8.  This was
##          set to 4 pre-batch (likely a 32-bit oversight) and we
##          haven't touched it here — fixing the size_t width is
##          a separate batch since changing it might surface
##          downstream alignment assumptions.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   lsm_get_self_attr
##      UNDEF/SINGLE gating: OK" in build/serial-test.txt.

## 196. syscall/linux: lsm_set_self_attr flags-first / size /
##      PAGE_SIZE gating (batch 167).
##
##      Symptom: Pre-batch sys_lsm_set_self_attr diverged from
##               Linux in four ways:
##                1. Rejected attr outside [100, 105] with EINVAL.
##                   Linux does no range check on attr — out-of-
##                   range attrs walk the setselfattr hook list
##                   and return LSM_RET_DEFAULT (EOPNOTSUPP).
##                2. Checked NULL ctx -> EFAULT *before* the size
##                   gates.  Linux runs size < sizeof(lsm_ctx)
##                   -> EINVAL first, so NULL ctx with size=0
##                   returns EINVAL, not EFAULT.
##                3. Accepted any nonzero size.  Linux requires
##                   `size >= sizeof(struct lsm_ctx)` (32 bytes)
##                   and `size <= PAGE_SIZE` (16 KiB on our
##                   target).  Sizes below 32 return EINVAL; sizes
##                   above PAGE_SIZE return E2BIG.
##                4. Checked flags last.  Linux checks flags
##                   first, so flags!=0 with absurd size still
##                   returns EINVAL, not E2BIG.
##
##      Fix:    Reordered gates to match Linux's
##              security/lsm_syscalls.c::lsm_setselfattr exactly:
##                - flags != 0 -> EINVAL (first)
##                - size < 32 -> EINVAL
##                - size > 16384 -> E2BIG
##                - ctx == NULL -> EFAULT (explicit; kernel-context
##                  validate_user_read bypasses NULL)
##                - validate_user_read(ctx, size) -> EFAULT
##                - terminal EOPNOTSUPP (LSM_RET_DEFAULT(setselfattr))
##              Removed the attr range check entirely.
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * lsm_set_self_attr(attr=100, ctx=lsm_ctx, size=32,
##          flags=1) -> EINVAL  (flags first).
##        * lsm_set_self_attr(attr=100, ctx=lsm_ctx, size=100_000,
##          flags=1) -> EINVAL  (flags wins over E2BIG).
##        * lsm_set_self_attr(attr=100, ctx=NULL, size=0, flags=0)
##          -> EINVAL  (size gate fires before NULL check).
##        * lsm_set_self_attr(attr=100, ctx=lsm_ctx, size=16,
##          flags=0) -> EINVAL  (was EOPNOTSUPP pre-batch; below
##          sizeof(lsm_ctx)).
##        * lsm_set_self_attr(attr=100, ctx=lsm_ctx, size=31,
##          flags=0) -> EINVAL  (off-by-one boundary).
##        * lsm_set_self_attr(attr=100, ctx=lsm_ctx, size=16385,
##          flags=0) -> E2BIG  (new gate; pre-batch returned
##          EOPNOTSUPP after a successful validate_user_read of
##          16385 bytes from a 64-byte buffer would have failed
##          with EFAULT under a user context).
##        * lsm_set_self_attr(attr=100, ctx=NULL, size=32, flags=0)
##          -> EFAULT  (kept; explicit NULL check after size gate).
##        * lsm_set_self_attr(attr=50, ctx=lsm_ctx, size=32,
##          flags=0) -> EOPNOTSUPP  (new; was EINVAL pre-batch
##          due to range check).
##        * lsm_set_self_attr(attr=100, ctx=lsm_ctx, size=32,
##          flags=0) -> EOPNOTSUPP  (happy path).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   lsm_set_self_attr
##          flags/size/E2BIG gating: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * The ctx contents are not validated.  Linux's
##          memdup_user followed by `size < lctx->ctx_len ||
##          lctx->ctx_len > size - sizeof(*lctx)` -> EINVAL is
##          not implemented because no LSM module is loaded; the
##          check would just lead to EOPNOTSUPP regardless.  When
##          real LSM lands, add the lctx.ctx_len bounds check.
##        * PAGE_SIZE here is hard-coded 16384 to match our 16 KiB
##          base page.  If we ever support multiple page sizes,
##          this needs to track the current page size.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 23s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   lsm_set_self_attr
##      flags/size/E2BIG gating: OK" in build/serial-test.txt.

## 197. syscall/linux: lsm_list_modules flags-first / size_t
##      width gating (batch 168).
##
##      Symptom: Pre-batch sys_lsm_list_modules diverged from
##               Linux in two ways:
##                1. Checked NULL size *before* flags, so a caller
##                   passing flags=1 with size=NULL got EFAULT
##                   instead of Linux's EINVAL.
##                2. Validated the size pointer for 4 bytes, but
##                   the Linux signature is `size_t __user *size`
##                   which is 8 bytes on x86_64.  The 4-byte
##                   probe would let a user pass a pointer to the
##                   last 4 bytes of a mapped region while the
##                   high 4 bytes of size_t lived in unmapped
##                   memory — silent truncation.
##
##      Fix:    Reordered gates to match Linux's
##              security/lsm_syscalls.c::lsm_list_modules exactly:
##                - flags != 0 -> EINVAL (first)
##                - size == NULL -> EFAULT (explicit; kernel-context
##                  validate_user_read bypasses NULL)
##                - validate_user_read(size, 8) -> EFAULT
##                - validate_user_write(size, 8) -> EFAULT
##                - terminal EOPNOTSUPP (no LSM infrastructure)
##              Also widened the shared size_buf test fixture
##              from 4 to 8 bytes so future user-context probes
##              don't hit truncation themselves.
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * lsm_list_modules(ids=NULL, size=NULL, flags=0) ->
##          EFAULT  (kept; NULL size gate).
##        * lsm_list_modules(ids=NULL, size=NULL, flags=1) ->
##          EINVAL  (new; flags wins over NULL size — was EFAULT
##          pre-batch).
##        * lsm_list_modules(ids=NULL, size=valid, flags=1) ->
##          EINVAL  (kept).
##        * lsm_list_modules(ids=NULL, size=valid,
##          flags=0xFFFF_FFFF) -> EINVAL  (new; bit-precise
##          flags rejection).
##        * lsm_list_modules(ids=NULL, size=valid, flags=0) ->
##          EOPNOTSUPP  (happy path).
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   lsm_list_modules flags-first
##          gating: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * Terminal is EOPNOTSUPP, not the Linux "success with
##          *size=total_size, return lsm_active_cnt" path.  This
##          is intentional — we have no LSM infrastructure at
##          all, so reporting "0 modules active" as a successful
##          enumeration would mislead a probe that needs to
##          distinguish "kernel built without LSM" from "LSM
##          active but zero modules registered."  EOPNOTSUPP
##          puts us in the former bucket.
##        * The lsm_get_self_attr code at line 17347 still
##          validates 4 bytes for its size_t pointer.  Same
##          bug, separate fix-up batch (carried in todo entry
##          195's "size validation uses 4 bytes" limitation).
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   lsm_list_modules
##      flags-first gating: OK" in build/serial-test.txt.

## 198. syscall/linux: lsm_get_self_attr size_t pointer width
##      4 -> 8 bytes (batch 169).
##
##      Symptom: sys_lsm_get_self_attr validated only 4 bytes of
##               the size pointer.  Linux's signature is
##               `size_t __user *size`, which on x86_64 is 8
##               bytes.  A user passing a pointer to the last 4
##               bytes of a mapped region would clear our 4-byte
##               validate_user_read/write and then fault on the
##               (eventual) put_user(total_size, size) of the
##               full 8 bytes — divergent EFAULT timing vs. Linux,
##               and on a write-protected page would have skipped
##               our write check entirely.
##
##               This was the limitation flagged at the bottom of
##               todo entry 195 and again in 197 as the next
##               fix-up batch.
##
##      Fix:    Bumped both validate_user_read and
##              validate_user_write probes from 4 to 8 bytes,
##              matching Linux's get_user/put_user width.  The
##              shared size_buf test fixture was already widened
##              to 8 bytes in batch 168 (entry 197), so no test
##              fixture changes were needed.
##
##      Self-tests:  No new tests.  In kernel context our
##                   validate_user_read/write bypass returns Ok
##                   unconditionally (mm::user::is_kernel_context),
##                   so a width mismatch is not directly testable
##                   from self-tests.  The existing nine
##                   lsm_get_self_attr self-tests (entry 195) all
##                   still pass with the wider probes.  Real
##                   user-context coverage will come when we have
##                   a way to launch a userspace probe with
##                   controllable mappings (carried as a global
##                   testing-infra limitation, not specific to
##                   this batch).
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).  Serial
##      confirms all three LSM markers
##      ("lsm_get_self_attr UNDEF/SINGLE gating: OK",
##       "lsm_set_self_attr flags/size/E2BIG gating: OK",
##       "lsm_list_modules flags-first gating: OK")
##      in build/serial-test.txt.

## 199. syscall/linux: setxattr/lsetxattr/fsetxattr flags &
##      XATTR_SIZE_MAX gating (batch 170).
##
##      Symptom: Pre-batch sys_setxattr / sys_lsetxattr /
##               sys_fsetxattr never validated flags or size.
##               A caller passing flags=4 (bit outside
##               XATTR_CREATE|XATTR_REPLACE) or size > 65536
##               (XATTR_SIZE_MAX) got EOPNOTSUPP from the
##               terminal, where Linux's fs/xattr.c::
##               setxattr_copy returns EINVAL / E2BIG ahead of
##               the per-FS dispatch.
##
##      Fix:    Added a shared helper xattr_validate_size_flags
##              that gates:
##                - flags & ~(XATTR_CREATE|XATTR_REPLACE)  -> EINVAL
##                - size > XATTR_SIZE_MAX (65536)          -> E2BIG
##              Called from xattr_set_path (covers setxattr +
##              lsetxattr) after path/name validation and from
##              sys_fsetxattr after fd+name validation, in both
##              cases ahead of the value-buffer validate_user_read.
##
##              flags=3 (CREATE | REPLACE both set) is bit-legal
##              at this gate — Linux only rejects unknown bits.
##              Real FSes enforce the mutual exclusion later as
##              EEXIST or ENODATA; on our stub it falls through
##              to EOPNOTSUPP.
##
##      Self-tests (boot-time):
##        * setxattr(path,name,_,size=0,flags=4)    -> EINVAL  (new)
##        * setxattr(path,name,_,size=0,flags=3)    -> EOPNOTSUPP
##          (new; CREATE|REPLACE both set is bit-legal)
##        * setxattr(path,name,_,size=70_000,_)     -> E2BIG   (new)
##        * setxattr(path,name,_,size=65537,_)      -> E2BIG   (new;
##          off-by-one boundary)
##        * setxattr(path,name,_,size=65536,_)      -> EOPNOTSUPP
##          (new; boundary inclusive)
##        * fsetxattr(fd=0,name,_,_,flags=8)        -> EINVAL  (new)
##        * fsetxattr(fd=0,name,_,size=100_000,_)   -> E2BIG   (new)
##        * lsetxattr(path,name,_,_,flags=0x100)    -> EINVAL  (new;
##          confirms shared xattr_set_path path)
##        * Pre-existing tests (NULL path -> EFAULT, valid ->
##          EOPNOTSUPP) all still pass.
##        * Emits "[syscall/linux]   xattr set flags/E2BIG gating: OK".
##
##      Known limitations:
##        * Linux's setxattr_copy gates flags *before* the name
##          copy (strncpy_from_user).  Our xattr_set_path checks
##          name validity (1-byte probe) before flags.  For a
##          real user call with simultaneously bad name pointer
##          and bad flags, Linux returns EINVAL where we return
##          EFAULT.  This is a strictly-rarer divergence than the
##          missing flags gate itself; fixing it requires splitting
##          xattr_validate_path_name into separate path / name
##          stages, which is more invasive than this batch warrants.
##          When real xattr code lands and gets a strncpy_from_user
##          equivalent, restructure to match.
##        * ERANGE for names longer than XATTR_NAME_MAX (255 bytes)
##          is not enforced because we don't have a bounded
##          strncpy_from_user yet.  Probes that pass an extra-long
##          name will see EOPNOTSUPP instead of ERANGE.  Same
##          dependency as above.
##
##      Build clean; boot test PASSED (BOOT_OK at 22s).
##      Serial confirms "[syscall/linux]   xattr set
##      flags/E2BIG gating: OK" in build/serial-test.txt.
